Objection and Answer on Positive Attributes and Mental Existence
Al-Jurjani records an objection about 'positive things' and the answer distinguishing real positivity from negative judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against the proof of mental existence is answered by redefining 'positive things' as concepts free from negation, and how this preserves the argument.

Core Idea
Positive attributes in kalam mean 'concepts without negation', not external existence.

This passage presents a critical objection to the standard argument for mental existence: if 'positive things' are external, they cannot be predicated of non-existents; if they are mental, the argument begs the question. Al-Jurjani records the answer that 'positive' here means concepts whose definition does not include negation, such as existence itself, thus avoiding both horns of the dilemma. We'll walk through the objection, the answer, and the detailed logical discussion that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the two-horned dilemma
Read the part where the objector presents the disjunction: if 'positive things' are external, then we don't concede; if mental, then it's a begging of the question.
Notice how the objection challenges the proof from the very meaning of 'positive'.
2
Study the answer distinguishing 'positive' from 'existent'
The answer defines 'positive' as that which does not involve negation in its concept, so negative predicates like 'non-existence' are excluded.
This shift avoids the dilemma because the term is not about external existence.
3
Consider the discussion on affirmative with negative predicate
The commentary examines a potential counter-argument: even affirmative propositions with negative predicates require mental existence of the subject.
This detail shows the depth of the debate; it does not undermine the answer.
4
Conclude with the final resolution
The truth is that such propositions are affirmative in form but negative in reality, and only real affirmation requires existence. Thus the objection fails.
This lesson clarifies a common point of confusion in kalam logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقد اعترض على متمسكهم فيه إشارة إلى أنه وارد على متمسكهم حيث ذكر فيه الأمور الثبوتية وأما على ما ذكره المصنف فإن حرر على طبق متمسكهم بأن يراد بالأحكام الأمور التي حكم بها، كما هو الظاهر فإن قوله : يحكم عليه، بمعنى يحمل عليه والباء صلة له، قوله : (فهو المراد بالوجود (الذهني) هذا بظاهره مناف لما سيأتي في بحث الكيف في المقصد السادس من مقاصد العلم من أن الارتسام في غير العقل الإنساني ينافي الوجود الذهني . وقد اعترض على متمسكهم بأنه إن أريد بالأمور الثبوتية أمور ثابتة في الخارج، فلا نسلم أنا نحكم بها على ما لا وجود له في الخارج، كيف ولو سلم لزم كون المحكوم عليه موجوداً في الخارج، وإن أريد بها أمور ثابتة في الذهن كان ذلك مصادرة على المطلوب، وأجيب بأن المراد بالثبوتية ما ليس السلب داخلا في مفهومها، واحترز بذلك عن الموجبة السالبة المحمول فإنها مساوية للسالبة فلا تقتضي وجود الموضوع وكون الحكم إيجاباً مستفاد من يحكم عليه، لأنه المتبادر منه فوارد عليه، وأما إذا أريد بالإمكان النسب الجزئية وبالثبوتية الإيجابية، وتكون الباء زائدة كما هو رأي الأخفش أو للملابسة ملابسة العام للخاص، ويكون المحكوم به متروكاً لعدم تعلق الغرض به لان الاحتراز من السالبة المحمول، حاصل بالثبوتية لعدم كون الإيجاب فيها حقيقة ويصير المعنى ويحكم عليه بأمور أحكاماً إيجابية صادقة، فلا ورود لهذا الاعتراض أصلاً، كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( كان ذلك مصادرة إلخ لأن الوجود الذهني موقوف على ثبوت الأمور في الذهن الذي هو الوجود في الذهن. قوله : ( بأن المراد إلخ) يعني ليس الثبوتية بمعنى الموجودة حتى يصح الترديد المذكور، بل بمعنى ما ليس السلب داخلاً في مفهومه قوله : ( فإنها مساوية (للسالبة لكون الإيجاب اعتبارياً محضاً، إذ ليس فيها حقيقة إلا سلب المحمول عن الموضوع، لكون العقل اعتبر أنه إذا سلب عنه المحمول كان متصفاً بالسلب ولا اتصاف في نفس الأمر وإلا لزم التسلسل في الاتصافات الثابتة في نفس الأمر. قوله : ( فإنها مساوية للسالبة فلا تقتضي وجود الموضوع فيه بحث لأن معنى الموجبة السالبة المحمول كما صرح به القطب في شرح المطالع ، إن (ج) شيء يسلب عنه (ب) ولا شك أن صدق هذا الإيجاب يتوقف على ثبوت مفهوم شيء يسلب عنه (ب) (لج) أمر، وإن ثبوت شيء لشيء فرع ثبوت المثبت له فيلزم أن تقتضي الموجبة السالبة المحمول وجود الموضوع، ولو في الذهن كسائر الموجبات المقتضية له بلا فرق ومن هاهنا، قال الفاضل الرومي في حاشيته المراد بعدم استدعاء الموجبة السالبة المحمول وجود الموضوع عدم استدعائها إياه بحسب الحقيقة، والخارج وأما استدعاؤه وجوده في الذهن، فلا محيص عنه إذ لا فرق بين الموجبة السالبة المحمول وبين الموجبة المعدولة المحمول في استدعاء وجود الموضوع في الذهن، سواء اعتبر حال ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، أو حال الحكم بالثبوت والحق أن الموجبة السالبة المحمول موجبة في الظاهر سالبة في الحقيقة كما يدل عليه قول الشارح في بعض حواشيه إذا حمل انتفاء الكتابة عن زيد عليه كانت موجبة سالبة المحمول راجعة إلى السلب، وإذا حمل مفهوم عدم الكتابة على زيد كانت موجبة معدولة المحمول، قد أثبت فيها للموضوع مفهوم عدمي، وليس راجعاً إلى حقيقة السلب بل هو إيجاب يلزمه السلب ولا يساويه، وقال في بعض كتبه مرجع مرجع اتصاف الشيء بالصفات السلبية عدم اتصافه بما هو مسلوب عنه ولا شك أن المقتضى لوجود الموضوع حقيقة الإيجاب لا صورته فقط فلا ورود للبحث، وأما ما
His saying: (And an objection has been raised against their argument) — there is an indication that it is directed against their argument where the author mentioned positive things. As for what the author wrote, if it is formulated according to their argument so that by 'rulings' are meant the things that are judged, as is apparent, for his saying 'he judges upon it' means 'he predicates upon it', and the 'ba'' is a pleonastic particle. His saying: (Thus it is what is meant by mental existence) — this apparently conflicts with what will come in the discussion of kvalitya in the sixth maqsad of the sciences, that impression in other than the human intellect contradicts mental existence. And an objection was raised against their argument: if by 'positive things' are meant things established in the external world, then we do not concede that we judge by them of what has no existence externally; nay, if that were conceded, it would require the subject of judgment to exist externally. And if by them are meant things established in the mind, that would be a begging of the question. It was answered: what is meant by 'positive' is that whose concept does not involve negation, and by this he guards against the affirmative with a negative predicate, for it is equivalent to a negative and does not require the existence of the subject. And the judgment being affirmative is derived from 'he judges upon it', because that is the immediate understanding, so the objection is valid. But if by 'possibility' is meant particular relations and by 'positivity' is meant affirmativity, and the 'ba'' is pleonastic as al-Akhfash held, or for the relation of a universal to a particular, and the predicate is omitted because there is no purpose in specifying it — since guarding against the negative predicate is achieved by 'positivity' because the affirmation in it is not real — then the meaning becomes: and he judges upon it with truthful affirmative judgments. So this objection has no relevance at all, as is not hidden. His saying: (that would be a begging of the question) — because mental existence depends on the establishment of things in the mind, which is existence in the mind. His saying: (by saying that what is meant is...) — meaning that 'positivity' is not in the sense of 'existent' so that the aforementioned disjunction would be valid, but rather in the sense of that whose concept does not include negation. His saying: (For it is equivalent to the negative because the affirmation is purely mental) — since there is no reality in it except the negation of the predicate from the subject, because the mind considers that if it negates the predicate from the subject, then the subject is characterized by the negation, and there is no attribution in the external world, otherwise an infinite regress would follow in the attributions established in the external world. His saying: (For it is equivalent to the negative and does not require the existence of the subject — there is a discussion here, because the meaning of the affirmative with a negative predicate, as al-Qutb stated in Sharh al-Matali', is that a thing (c) is negated from (b). No doubt the truth of this affirmation depends on the existence of the concept 'a thing from which (b) is negated' for (c) something; and the existence of a thing for a thing is a branch of the existence of that to which it is attributed; so it follows that an affirmative with a negative predicate does require the existence of the subject, even if in the mind, like all other affirmatives that require it, without difference. Hence, al-Fadil al-Rumi said in his gloss: what is meant by the affirmative with a negative predicate not requiring the existence of the subject is its not requiring it in reality and externally; as for its requiring its existence in the mind, there is no escape from it, since there is no difference between an affirmative with a negative predicate and an affirmative with a privative predicate in requiring the existence of the subject in the mind, whether one considers the state of the predicate's existence for the subject or the state of judging the existence. And the truth is that an affirmative with a negative predicate is affirmative in appearance but negative in reality, as indicated by the commentator's saying in some of his glosses: if the negation of writing from Zayd is predicated of him, it is an affirmative with a negative predicate reverting to negation; and if the concept of non-writing is predicated of Zayd, it is an affirmative with a privative predicate, in which a privative concept is established for the subject, and it does not revert to the reality of negation, but rather it is an affirmation that entails negation and is not equivalent to it. And he said in some of his books: the reference of a thing's being characterized by negative attributes is the absence of its being characterized by what is negated from it, and there is no doubt that what requires the existence of the subject is the reality of affirmation, not its image only, so there is no basis for the discussion. And as for what...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section regarding the proof for mental existence. The objection concerns the term 'positive things' (al-umur al-thubutiyya) used in the argument. The objector argues: if 'positive things' means things existing externally, then we cannot predicate them of non-existents; if they mean things existing in the mind, that begs the question. The answer clarifies that 'positive' here means concepts whose definition does not involve negation (i.e., not negative concepts like 'non-existence'). This avoids the dilemma. The commentary then delves into a detailed discussion of propositions with negative predicates (al-mujaba al-saliba al-mahmul), explaining that such propositions are equivalent to negatives in reality and do not require external existence, but they do require mental existence. The author cites al-Qutb and al-Fadil al-Rumi to resolve a potential counter-argument about whether such propositions require existence. The conclusion is that the requirement for existence is only for the reality of affirmation, not for mere appearance, so the objection is invalid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Positive attributes must be real external attributes.
Correction: In this context, 'positive' means concepts free from negation, not necessarily externally existent.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the objection, what is meant by 'positive things' (al-umur al-thubutiyya)?
A) Things that exist externally.
B) Things that exist in the mind only.
C) Things whose concept does not involve negation.
D) Affirmative judgments only.

Key Takeaway
The term 'positive things' in the argument for mental existence refers to concepts without negation, not external realities, thus the objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a belief about yourself or the world because you assumed it was 'positive' (real), when in fact it was only a mental construct hiding a negation (like 'I am not a failure' meaning only the absence of success)? What would it cost you to examine that belief today using the definition from this lesson?
?
The objector's dilemma shows how easy it is to talk past each other when terms are ambiguous. In your most important relationship, is there a concept—like 'respect', 'love', or 'trust'—that you and the other person mean differently? Will you commit to clarifying it today, even if it opens a difficult conversation?
?
The answer distinguishes between affirmation in form and in reality. Consider your declaration of faith: is your 'La ilaha illa Allah' merely a form of words, or does its negation (rejection of false gods) penetrate your daily choices? What one decision would prove that your affirmation is real, not just apparent?

The Objection on Positive Things and Its Answer
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and answer about positive things requiring existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma posed against the argument from positive things and how the answer resolves it using the concept of nafs al-amr.

Core Idea
Positive things require existence, but the answer lies in nafs al-amr, avoiding both external existence and circularity.

In the debate over mental existence, an objection argues that claiming positive things (al-umur al-thubutiyya) are established for a subject leads to a dilemma: either they exist externally (impossible if the subject doesn't exist externally) or they exist mentally (leading to circularity). Al-Jurjani presents the objection and then the answer, which invokes the concept of 'nafs al-amr' (the thing itself) to break the dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the two horns of the dilemma. The objector presents two alternatives: external existence or mental existence.
Notice the structure: 'إن أردت ... وإن أردت'.
2
Analyze the problem with each horn
The first horn (external) is impossible because the subject may not exist externally; the second horn (mental) leads to circularity (musadara). Understand why both are problematic.
The objection is forceful because it seems no third option exists.
3
Examine the answer using nafs al-amr
The answer clarifies that 'positive things' are established for the subject in nafs al-amr, which is not limited to external or mental. This existence in nafs al-amr depends on the subject's existence; since not external, it must be mental. This avoids circularity because it does not assume mental existence upfront.
Think of nafs al-amr as the real state of affairs, encompassing both mind and extra-mental reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض أيضاً بأنك إن أردت أن تلك الأمور الثبوتية ثابتة في الخارج للموضوع المذكور، فهو ممنوع، كيف ولو صح ذلك كان الموضوع موجوداً في الخارج، وإن أردت أنها ثابتة له في الذهن، كان ذلك فرعاً لوجود الموضوع فيه فيكون مصادرة وأجيب : بأنا نريد أنها ثابتة للموضوع في نفس الأمر، وذلك موقوف على وجود الموضوع فيها، وإذ ليس في الخارج فهو في الذهن
He also objected: If you mean that those positive things are established in external reality for the mentioned subject, then it is denied; for if that were true, the subject would exist in external reality. And if you mean that they are established for it in the mind, then that is a branch of the subject's existence in the mind, thus it is a petition of principle. And it was answered: We mean that they are established for the subject in nafs al-amr (the thing itself), and that depends on the existence of the subject therein; and since it is not in external reality, it is in the mind.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the requirement of existence for positive predications in arguments for mental existence. The objector poses a dilemma: if the 'positive things' (al-umur al-thubutiyya) mentioned in the argument are taken to be established in external reality, then the subject must also exist externally, which is not universally true. If they are taken to be established in the mind, then the argument assumes mental existence to prove mental existence, which is circular. The answer clarifies that the intended meaning is that these things are established for the subject in 'nafs al-amr' (the thing itself), meaning in reality irrespective of external or mental considerations. This existence in nafs al-amr depends on the subject's existence in some locus; since it is not in external reality, it must be in the mind. Thus the argument does not beg the question but shows that mental existence is the only possible locus for such thubutiyya.

Key Terms
الأمور الثبوتية
الوجود الذهني
نفس الأمر
المصادرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer simply asserts that mental existence is the only option without argument, making it arbitrary.
Correction: The answer uses a disjunctive proof: since positive things must be established in some locus (nafs al-amr), and they are not in external reality, they must be in the mind. This is a logical deduction, not an arbitrary assertion.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's second horn in the dilemma?
A) Positive things exist externally and subject exists externally
B) Positive things exist in the mind and presuppose existence in the mind, leading to circularity
C) Positive things are negative
D) The subject does not exist

Key Takeaway
The concept of nafs al-amr allows positive things to be established for a subject without requiring external existence or causing circularity, demonstrating mental existence as the necessary locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the 'positive things' in your own life—your beliefs, values, and judgments—are you content to let them exist merely as mental constructs, or do you seek to ground them in something more real? What would it mean for you to commit to nafs al-amr as the standard of truth, even when external reality seems to contradict it?
?
The objection warns against circular reasoning: assuming what you want to prove. In your spiritual journey, are there places where you inadvertently assume God's existence or mercy without letting that assumption be tested by the reality of your actions? How can you ensure your faith is grounded in nafs al-amr rather than mere mental habit?
?
If positive things like justice, mercy, and truth are not always manifest in the external world, where do you locate their reality? Does this lesson challenge you to reconsider where you place your trust—in external circumstances or in the unchanging nature of nafs al-amr as known by God? How will this shift your priorities today?

Universality as Proof of Mental Existence
Al-Jurjani presents the second proof of mental existence from universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how universality (kulliyya) serves as the second proof for the reality of mental existence.

Core Idea
Universality is a positive attribute that can only belong to something existing.

In this lesson, Al-Jurjani presents the second argument for mental existence. He draws attention to universal concepts, which are characterized by universality. Since universality is a positive attribute, it cannot exist without a subject that exists. This subject cannot be external because external objects are particular, so it must be mental.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation. Note that the passage is cut off; the full argument concludes that the universal must exist in the mind.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on three terms: kulliyya (universality), sifa thubutiyya (positive attribute), and mafhum kulli (universal concept). Understand their meanings.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Follow the logical structure
The argment has three steps: (1) Some concepts are universal. (2) Universality is a positive attribute. (3) A positive attribute requires a subject that exists. Since external objects are particular, the universal must exist in the mind.
The missing conclusion is implied by the logic.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this argument is considered a proof of mental existence. How does it differ from the first proof?
This shows that mental existence is not just imaginary but necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمر ( الثاني ) من الأمور الدالة على الوجود الذهني أن يقال : من المفهومات ما هو كلي ( أي متصف بالكلية التي هي صفة ثبوتية فلا بد أن يكون
The second matter among the things indicating mental existence is that it is said: Among concepts there is what is universal, that is, described by universality which is a positive attribute, so it must exist.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof of mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). The author states that among concepts (mafhum), there is what is universal (kulli), meaning that it is characterized by universality (kulliyya). Since universality is a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya), it cannot be predicated of a non-existent thing. Therefore, the universal concept must exist — and since it does not exist in external reality as a concrete particular, it must exist in the mind. Thus universality serves as an indication that mental existence is real.

Key Terms
الكلية
الصفة الثبوتية
المفهوم الكلي
الوجود الذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think universality is merely a mental label with no basis in reality.
Correction: Universality is a real positive attribute, so it must inhere in a subject that exists—ultimately in the mind.

Checkpoint
Why does universality (kulliyya) indicate mental existence?
A) Because universality is a negative attribute
B) Because universality is a positive attribute that cannot apply to a non-existent
C) Because universals exist externally
D) Because the mind creates universals without basis

Key Takeaway
Universality, as a positive attribute, necessitates a subject that exists in the mind, proving mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You classify actions as 'just' or 'unjust' using universal concepts. But if that universal exists truly in your mind, why do you let your actions fall short of it? What would it take for your external life to match the universal that you already know mentally?
?
When you think of 'truth' as a universal, you affirm its reality in your mind. Yet how often do you betray that truth in your speech or dealings? Are you comfortable with a mental existence of virtue that your actions deny?
?
This lesson shows that universals must exist in the mind because they cannot exist externally. Your own concepts of good and evil have a real existence in your intellect. Do you treat them as mere abstractions, or as real standards that will judge you? How will you respond to that judgment?

The Universal Concept as Proof for Mental Existence
Iji presents the second argument and its first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from universal concepts supports mental existence and the objection that universality may be a negative attribute.

Core Idea
Universals cannot exist externally, so they must exist in the mind.

The second proof for mental existence builds on the nature of universals. If a universal is characterized by a positive attribute like universality, it must exist, but not in the external world. However, some argue that universality is merely a negative attribute, which would invalidate the proof. This lesson examines the core argument and its first objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a support, and an objection.
Pay attention to the key terms: كلي, كلية, صفة ثبوتية, صفة سلبية.
2
Identify the main argument
The argument states: universals are characterized by universality, which is a positive attribute; therefore the universal must exist. It cannot exist externally because external things are particular, so it exists in the mind.
This is the second proof for mental existence.
3
Understand the objection
The objection is that universality might be a negative attribute (the absence of prevention from sharing), not a positive one. Even if conceded as positive, it would fall under the first proof, making this redundant.
The objection challenges the premise that universality is positive.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this objection matters: if universality is negative, the argument collapses; if it is positive but redundant, the proof adds nothing new.
This sets up the need for alternative formulations in the next lesson.

Source Text

الأمر الثاني من الأمور الدالة على الوجود الذهني أن يقال : من المفهومات ما هو كلي أي متصف بالكلية التي هي صفة ثبوتية فلا بد أن يكون الموصوف بها موجوداً وليس في الخارج إذ كل موجود في الخارج فهو مشخص متعين في حد ذاته بحيث يمتنع فرض اشتراكه، فيكون موجوداً في الذهن ويرد عليه أن الكلية صفة سلبية لأنها عدم المنع من فرض الشركة، وإن سلم كونها ثبوتية كانت داخلة في الاستدلال الأول، فلا وجه لجعلها استدلالاً على حده وقد يقال المفهومية صفة ثبوتية اتصف بها الكلي، فيكون موجوداً، ليس في الخارج بل في الذهن ويرد عليه السؤال الثاني، وقد يقال أيضاً : للحقائق الكلية كالإنسان مثلاً وجود بالضرورة، وليس في الأعيان بل في الأذهان ويتجه عليه أن دعوى الضرورة في كون الحقائق أنفسها موجودة غير مسموعة، نعم أفراد هذه الحقائق موجودة في الخارج بالضرورة.
The second of the matters indicating mental existence is that it is said: among concepts there is what is universal, i.e., characterized by universality which is a positive attribute, so necessarily the thing characterized by it must exist, and it does not exist externally because everything existing externally is particular, determined in itself such that it is impossible to suppose sharing of it, so it exists in the mind. And it is objected against it that universality is a negative attribute because it is the absence of prevention from supposing sharing; and if it is conceded to be positive, it would be included in the first proof, so there is no reason to make it a separate proof. And it may be said: conceptuality is a positive attribute by which the universal is characterized, so it exists, not externally but in the mind, and it is objected to it the second question. And it may also be said: for universal realities, like man for example, there is existence necessarily, and it is not in external individuals but in minds, and it is directed against it that the claim of necessity regarding the existence of the realities themselves is not acceptable; indeed, the individuals of these realities exist externally necessarily.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). The argument begins with the claim that among concepts (mafhumāt) there are universals (kulliyyāt). A universal is characterized by universality (kulliyya), which is asserted to be a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya). Therefore, the thing characterized by universality must exist. It cannot exist externally because all external existents are particular (mushakhkhaṣ), determined in themselves such that sharing is impossible. Hence, it must exist in the mind. An objection follows: universality may be a negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya), since it is merely the absence of prevention from sharing. Even if conceded as positive, it would fall under the first proof, so it should not be a separate argument. Alternative formulations are then considered: conceptuality (al-mafhūmiyya) as a positive attribute, or the necessary existence of universal realities like 'man' in the mind. Each is met with objections. The passage concludes that only the individuals of these realities exist externally necessarily.

Key Terms
الوجود الذهني
كلي
كلية
صفة ثبوتية
مشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That universality is always a positive attribute that demands a real existent.
Correction: The passage shows that universality can be interpreted as a negative attribute (absence of prevention from sharing), which does not require a positive existent outside the mind.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why might universality not be a positive attribute?
A) Because it is a relation between concepts
B) Because it is the absence of prevention from sharing
C) Because it exists only in the mind
D) Because it is a necessary attribute of God

Key Takeaway
The second proof for mental existence is challenged by the possibility that universality is a negative attribute, not a positive one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of concepts like 'justice' or 'truth', do you treat them as real things in the world or as mental constructs? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider where your ideals truly reside?
?
If universality is merely a lack of prevention from sharing, what does that say about the reliability of your universal categories? Are you willing to accept that your most certain concepts may be defined by what they are not?
?
The objection suggests this proof might be redundant. How often do you hold onto arguments that seem independent but actually rest on the same foundations? What would it take for you to critically examine your own proofs for what you believe?

Alternative Formulations of the Universal Argument
Iji presents two alternative formulations and their objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two alternative formulations of the second proof for mental existence and their respective objections.

Core Idea
Different ways to state the argument face similar objections.

After the initial objection, proponents try to rephrase the argument. One focuses on conceptuality as a positive attribute, another on the necessary existence of universal realities. Both are met with objections. This lesson explores these alternatives and why they fail to establish mental existence definitively.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the second part of the passage covering the alternatives and objections. Notice the pattern: each alternative is presented and then refuted.
The passage uses 'wa qad yuqāl' (and it may be said) to introduce each alternative.
2
Identify the first alternative
The first alternative: conceptuality (al-mafhūmiyya) is a positive attribute characterizing the universal, so it exists in the mind. But the objection is the same as before (the 'second question').
The 'second question' likely refers to the earlier objection about positivity.
3
Identify the second alternative
The second alternative: universal realities like 'man' have necessary existence, but not externally; they exist in the mind. The objection is that the claim of necessity is not accepted; only individuals exist externally necessarily.
This shifts the argument from attributes to the nature of universal realities themselves.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Despite these attempts, the proof for mental existence from universals remains inconclusive. The objections show that the argument is not decisive.
This prepares for further proofs in the text.

The Third Proof: Real Propositions Demand Mental Existence
Iji argues that real propositions necessitate mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessity of real universal propositions demonstrates the reality of mental existence.

Core Idea
Real propositions prove that mental existence is necessary.

This lesson examines Iji's third argument for mental existence, which hinges on real propositions. These are judgments about universals that hold regardless of external existence. We'll see how the possibility of such propositions implies the mind's reality as a locus of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the definition of 'real proposition' and the example given.
2
Understand what a real proposition is
A real proposition judges about what a universal term applies to, whether or not those individuals exist externally. Recognize that this is a kind of universal judgment.
The example is 'the impossible is non-existent'—the subject has no external existence.
3
Identify the implication and the falsity of the consequent
The argument states that without mental existence, taking a real proposition would be impossible. But such propositions are actually possible (e.g., the example), so the consequent is false. Therefore, mental existence must be real.
The implication is that real propositions require mental existence for their subjects.
4
Examine the demonstration
The judgment in a real proposition is not conditioned on the subject's external existence. It can be about mentally apprehended individuals alone. If those individuals didn't exist in the mind, an affirmative judgment about them couldn't be true.
Thus, the truth of such judgments shows that mental existence is a reality.
5
Reflect on the significance
This argument strengthens the case for mental existence by showing it is necessary for the truth of certain propositions we commonly accept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمر الثالث لولا الوجود الذهني لم يمكن أخذ القضية الحقيقية للموضوع وهي التي حكم فيها على ما يصدق عليه في نفس الأمر الكلي الواقع عنواناً سواء كان موجوداً في الخارج محققاً أو مقدراً أو لا يكون موجوداً فيه أصلاً والتالي باطل وقد أشار إلى بيان الملازمة وبطلان التالي معاً بقوله : فإنا إذا قلنا : قوله : لولا الوجود الذهني إلخ تقريره لولا الوجود الذهني لم يمكن أخذ الحقيقية الموجبة الصادقة لكن أخذها ممكن بل واقع نحو الممتنع معدوم بيان الملازمة، إن الحكم فيها غير مشروط بوجود الموضوع في الخارج، فيجوز أن يكون الحكم فيها على الأفراد المعقولة فقط حكماً إيجابياً فلو لم تكن موجودة في الذهن لم يصدق ذلك الحكم.
The third: Without mental existence, it would not be possible to take the real proposition (al-qadiyya al-ḥaqīqiyya) of the subject, which is that in which judgment is made about what the universal, occurring as a title, is true of in itself, whether it exists externally actually, or hypothetically, or does not exist externally at all; and the consequent is false. And he pointed to the demonstration of the implication and the falsity of the consequent together by his saying: For if we say... [commentary:] His saying: 'Without mental existence etc.' its exposition: Without mental existence, it would not be possible to take the true affirmative real proposition, but taking it is possible, indeed actual, such as 'the impossible is non-existent.' The demonstration of the implication: The judgment in it is not conditioned on the existence of the subject externally, so it is permissible that the judgment in it is about the intellectually apprehended individuals only as an affirmative judgment, so if they were not existing in the mind, that judgment would not be true.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). It is based on real propositions (al-qadiyya al-ḥaqīqiyya), which predicate something of a universal regardless of whether its instances exist externally. For example, 'the impossible is non-existent' is a true real proposition even though the subject (the impossible) has no external existence. The argument claims that without mental existence, such propositions could not be formulated or true. The commentator explains the implication: the judgment in such a proposition is not conditioned on external existence of the subject; it can be about mentally apprehended individuals alone. If those individuals did not exist in the mind, the affirmative judgment could not be true. Since we do make such true judgments, mental existence is necessary.

Correction: The passage shows that real propositions can have subjects that do not exist externally, like 'the impossible', and still be true.

B) The formulation of true universal propositions with non-existent subjects
C) The proof of God's existence
D) The distinction between positive and negative attributes

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that true judgments about non-existent subjects require mental existence.

Non-Existent Distinction as a Branch of Mental Existence
Al-Jurjani states the main claim and answers an objection about the independence of non-existent differences from intellection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question of whether non-existents can be distinguished from one another is a branch of the debate about mental existence.

Core Idea
Non-existents can only differ in the mind, so the debate hinges on mental existence.

Al-Jurjani opens by stating that the dispute over the distinction of non-existents is not an independent issue but a branch of the larger debate about mental existence. He argues that whatever differences non-existents have can only be in the intellect, not in external reality, because they lack subsistence. This passage establishes the initial position and introduces a key objection that will be addressed later.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to 'فلا تمايز بينها إلا في العقل'. Note how al-Jurjani directly links the issue to mental existence.
2
Identify the reasoning
Follow the logic: non-existents have no external existence, so any distinction must be mental. If that distinction implied mental existence, then no absolute non-existent would be conceivable.
This leads to the first objection.
3
Examine the first objection
Read the objection that the difference in implications of non-existences does not depend on intellection, and the author's brief response that the answer is elsewhere.
This objection sets the stage for the later resolution.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

لأنه لا تمايز إلا في العقل بأن الدليل على ذلك التمايز اختلاف مقتضيات الأعدام كما تحققته وذلك الاختلاف والاقتضاء غير مشروط بالتعقل، إذ لو فرض أن لا عاقل في الوجود يكون الاختلاف، والاقتضاء بحاله فكذا التمايز، وقد نبهت على جوابه فيما سبق فليتذكر هذا واعترض بعض المتأخرين أيضاً على هذا الحق بأن بيان التفريع بهذا الوجه مع أنه مردود بأن الأمر بالعكس لأن الفلاسفة المثبتين للوجود الذهني يقولون : بتمايز الذهن . مناف لنفي التمايز عن المعدوم المطلق، فاندفع ما قاله صاحب المقاصد : من أن الأمر على عكس ما قال صاحب المواقف، لأن الحكماء المثبتين للوجود الذهني قائلون بالتمايز وجمهور المتكلمين النافين له قائلون بعدم التمايز، ومع ذلك لا يمكن إجراؤه في تمايز العدمات، إذ لا يمكن أن يقال : أن ذلك التمايز إذا كان لكونها موجودة في الذهن لم تخرج عن كونها إعداماً، بل عن كونها معدومات. أما الأول فلما القولين وأما الثاني فلان الكلام في تمايز المعدومات من حيث إنها معدومات وإذا كانت الأعدام موجودة في الذهن لم تكن معدومات، وكذا ظهر فساد ما ذكره شارح التجريد من أن الأولى في وجه التفريع أن يقال : لما كان التمايز وصفاً ثبوتياً يستدعي ثبوت المثبت له فمن أثبت الوجود الذهني حكم بتمايز الأعدام، والمعدومات الخارجية لما لها من الثبوت الذهني ومن نفاه حكم مر من اختلاف المصنف بيان ما هو الحق في هذه المسألة، وأن الخلاف في التمايز ينبغي أن يكون فرع الخلاف في الوجود الذهني، وإن لم يجعلوا كذلك، وليس مراده إنهم إنما اختلفوا في تمايز المعدوم بناء على اختلافهم في الوجود الذهني، وإن أشعر به كلام الشارح في المقصد السابع من مرصد الوحدة والكثرة على أن أبا علي ذكره في وجودية الإمكان أنه لو لم يكن وجودياً لم يكن فرق بين إمكانه لا ولا إمكان له لعدم التمايز بين العدمات فيفهم منه أن الحكماء لا يقولون بتمايز الأعدام على وفق ما ذكره المصنف، إلا أن يثبت أن ما ذكره أبو علي كلام إلزامي، وأما عن قوله لا يمكن إجراؤه في العدمات فهو أن الاختلاف في تمايز العدمات ليس من حيث إنها عدمات، بل من حيث إنها معدومات، وقد أشار إليه الشارح بقوله : وأما المعدومات التي من جملتها العدمات ففي تمايزها خلاف فعلى تقدير القول بالوجود الذهني تكون الأعدام متمايزة، لكن لا باعتبار أنها معدومات، بل باعتبار أنها موجودات في الذهن ولا يضرنا عدم خروجها بالوجود الذهني عن كونها عدمات بل يكفينا خروجها عن كونها معدومات فتأمل فإنه دقيق .
First Observatory - Fifth Goal: Do non-existents differ? [The statement] 'Non-existents are a branch of the dispute concerning mental existence, and that because there is no distinction between non-existents except in the intellect, for those judgments are attributed to non-existents according to the actual state of affairs in the intellect, not in the external world, since the external non-existent has no subsistence in the external world such that it could be described with something, so there is no distinction between them except in the intellect. If that distinction obtained for them in the intellect [and] they had existence in the mind, then no absolute non-existent would be conceived; rather, everything conceived from... (He said: 'i.e.') concerning the dispute etc. You know that this dispute is not specific to possible non-existents and to distinction in the external world; so whoever says: 'The intended [meaning] is the dispute between those who say there is no subsistence for the non-existent, otherwise the branching would not be correct,' has not come with anything. His statement: 'They are only described etc.' means that the intellect, when it observes them, finds them described with those judgments in their essences, disregarding any consideration and any supposition, and this abstract description does not depend on the existence of the intellect and its observation, so it is not objected that that difference and implication are not conditional upon intellection, for if one supposes its absence, rather the absence of the intellect, that difference would remain as it is. ...from the complete cause at all, so it does not necessitate the absence of the conditioned; but you may say: His intention is that the absence of the condition insofar as it is a condition necessitates the absence of the conditioned insofar as it is that, unlike the absence of something other than the condition insofar as it is that; yes, if it is observed insofar as it is another part of the complete cause, then its absence also entails the absence of the conditioned, and the first is more apparent. So it is not valid. His statement: '(i.e. concerning the dispute about the distinction of the non-existent etc.)' i.e., between those who say that there is no subsistence for the non-existent, otherwise the estimation... Then know that the impossible entails the impossible, and likewise imaginary things, so the issue is not a branch of the subsistence of the possible non-existent other than the imaginary. And an objection was raised against his statement: 'Because there is no distinction except in the intellect' that the evidence for that distinction is the difference in the implications of non-existences (privation) as you have verified, and that difference and implication are not conditional upon intellection, for if one supposes that there is no intellect in existence, the difference and implication would remain as they are, and likewise the distinction. And I have pointed out its answer previously, so let this be remembered. And some later scholars also objected to this truth that the explanation of the branching in this manner, while it is refuted by the point being the opposite, because the philosophers who affirm mental existence say: that non-existents are distinct, and the majority of theologians who deny mental existence are those who say they are not distinct, it cannot be applied to the distinction of non-existences (privation), for it cannot be said: If that distinction is because they exist in the mind, then the non-existences would not be distinct, for non-existences, because they exist in the mind, do not cease to be non-existences; rather, they only cease to be non-existents. So it is better to say: Since distinction is an affirmative attribute that requires the subsistence of the described, whoever affirms mental existence judges the distinction of non-existences and external non-existents, because they have mental subsistence, and whoever denies it judges the absence of distinction due to the absence of subsistence at all. This objection, along with the explanation of the branching in the mentioned manner, is mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid, except his statement: 'It cannot be applied to the distinction of non-existences etc.' And I say: As for the answer to the refutation that the matter is the opposite, it is that his intention [is that] non-existents and non-existences and the concept of absolute non-existent and absolute non-existence, if they existed in the mind, then the distinction obtained there is affirmed for the existent, not for the absolute non-existent which has no existence. His statement: '(Affirmed for the existent)' i.e., existence has an entry in distinction, for if it were not for it, distinction would be negated, so it is not objected that we do not grant that it is for existence necessarily, because the absence of conditions is distinct from the absence of something else without the two forms obtained from them, except that the locus of distinction is the intellect. His statement: '(Not for the absolute non-existent)' i.e., insofar as it is non-existent, even if it is affirmed for the essence of the non-existent. And this corresponds to what is in Ilahiyyat al-Shifa' and al-Tahsil concerning how can a judgment be imposed on the non-existent? The meaning of our saying: 'The non-existent is such-and-such' - the description of its being such-and-such is obtained for the non-existent, i.e., existent for it. So that description is either existent in itself or non-existent. If it is existent, then the non-existent has an existent attribute, so the described is necessarily existent, so the non-existent is existent. If the attribute is non-existent, then how can the non-existent be in itself existent for something? For that which is not existent in itself is impossible to be existent for something. End of quote. And their statement that non-existents are distinct means that external non-existents are distinct in the mind, which is not incompatible with the negation of distinction for the absolute non-existent. Thus what the author of al-Maqasid said is refuted: that the matter is the opposite of what the author of al-Mawaqif said, because the philosophers who affirm mental existence say distinction, and the majority of theologians who deny it say no distinction, and yet it cannot be applied to the distinction of non-existences, for it cannot be said: that distinction, if it is because they exist in the mind, they do not cease to be non-existences, but rather cease to be non-existents. As for the first, because of the two statements; and as for the second, because the discussion is about the distinction of non-existents insofar as they are non-existents, and if non-existences exist in the mind, they are not non-existents. Likewise, the corruption of what the commentator of al-Tajrid mentioned is apparent: that the best in the manner of branching is to say: Since distinction is an affirmative attribute that requires the subsistence of the affirmed, whoever affirms mental existence judges the distinction of non-existences and external non-existents because they have mental subsistence, and whoever denies it judges... ...from the difference of the author's statement of what is the truth in this issue, and that the dispute about distinction should be a branch of the dispute about mental existence, even if they did not make it so, and his intention is not that they only differed about the distinction of the non-existent based on their difference in mental existence, although the commentator's words in the Seventh Observatory of al-Wahda wa al-Kathra suggest that Abu Ali mentioned in his Wujudiyyat al-Imkan that if it were not existential, there would be no difference between its possibility and no possibility for it due to the lack of distinction between non-existences, from which it is understood that the philosophers do not affirm the distinction of non-existences in accordance with what the author mentioned, unless it is established that what Abu Ali mentioned is argumentative speech. And as for his statement that it cannot be applied to non-existences, it is that the difference in the distinction of non-existences is not insofar as they are non-existences, but insofar as they are non-existents, and the commentator indicated this by his saying: 'As for non-existents which include non-existences, there is dispute about their distinction. On the assumption of affirming mental existence, non-existences would be distinct, but not insofar as they are non-existences, but insofar as they are existents in the mind, and it does not harm us that they do not cease, by mental existence, to be non-existences; rather, it suffices us that they cease to be non-existents.' So ponder, for it is subtle.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a lengthy dialectical discussion about whether non-existents (المعدومات) can be distinct from one another. The author, al-Jurjani, begins by stating that the issue is a branch of the dispute about mental existence (الوجود الذهني). He explains that distinction between non-existents can only occur in the intellect, because in the external world they have no subsistence (ثبوت) to be described by anything. If, however, the distinction in the intellect implies that they have existence in the mind, then no absolute non-existent would be conceived—everything conceived would have some mental existence. The commentator then clarifies that the dispute is not restricted to possible non-existents or to external distinction. He addresses an objection that the differentiation of non-existents (like the difference between privations) does not depend on the intellect, because the differences in the implications of non-existences (مقتضيات الأعدام) are not conditional on intellection; even if no intellect existed, those differences would remain. The author notes that he has already answered this objection elsewhere. Then a later objection from some post-classical scholars is presented: the relation is actually the opposite of what the author claims, because the philosophers who affirm mental existence affirm distinction, while the theologians who deny mental existence deny it. Therefore, the dispute cannot be applied to non-existences (العدمات) in the same way, because if non-existences exist in the mind, they do not cease to be non-existences but merely cease to be non-existents. The objection recommends saying that distinction requires subsistence, so those who affirm mental existence judge distinction for non-existences and external non-existents, while those who deny it judge no distinction. The author responds by clarifying that the intended point is that the dispute *should* be a branch of the mental existence dispute, even if scholars did not actually make it so. He also distinguishes between non-existents (معدومات) and non-existences (عدمات): when non-existences are considered as existing in the mind, they are no longer non-existents (as such), so the distinction applies to them as mental existents, not as non-existences. He quotes Ibn Sina's Ilahiyyat al-Shifa' and al-Tahsil to argue that a judgment cannot be attributed to a non-existent unless that judgment itself has existence. Ultimately, the author concludes that the truth is that the dispute about distinction should be a branch of the mental existence dispute, and that the distinction of non-existents in the mind is not incompatible with the negation of distinction for the absolute non-existent. The passage ends with an encouragement to ponder, as it is subtle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the dispute about non-existent distinction is independent and not connected to mental existence.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explicitly states it is a branch of the mental existence debate, because distinction can only occur in the mind.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, why is the distinction of non-existents limited to the intellect?
A) Because non-existents have no subsistence in the external world.
B) Because the intellect creates the distinction arbitrarily.
C) Because the theologians agreed on this point.
D) Because non-existents are imaginary.

Key Takeaway
The question of whether non-existents differ hinges on the affirmation of mental existence, because external non-existents have no attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your sins that you have not committed yet (i.e., potential sins that you have only imagined), do you treat them as mere mental abstractions, or do they carry a weight of reality that affects your heart? How does the lesson that non-existents are only distinguished in the intellect challenge or affirm your feeling of accountability for intentions?
?
Consider a blessing you do not have—like wealth or health that you wish for. In your heart, do you feel its absence as a real thing with its own distinct reality? If the distinction of non-existents is only mental, what does that say about the 'reality' of your desires and fears? How does that affect your reliance on Allah?
?
The passage states that the impossible entails the impossible. Think of something impossible you still ponder—like a past mistake you cannot undo. Is that 'non-existent' (the alternative history) truly distinct from other non-existents? If not, can you free yourself from the illusion that this imagined scenario has a fixed reality that haunts you? What would it mean to accept that it is only a mental distinction?

Logic and Technical Terms
advanced
10 min
Resolving the Objection: Non-Existents vs. Non-Existences
Al-Jurjani resolves a strong objection by refining the terms of the debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani resolves the objection that the dispute over distinction is opposite to what he claimed, by distinguishing between non-existents (معدومات) and non-existences (عدمات).

Core Idea
Non-existences become distinct only as mental existents, not as pure nothings.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a later objection that the philosophers who affirm mental existence also affirm distinction, while the theologians who deny mental existence deny it—the opposite of what the initial claim suggested. He responds by carefully distinguishing between non-existents (معدومات) and non-existences (عدمات), and by quoting Ibn Sina to show that a judgment cannot be attributed to a pure non-existent. The resolution is subtle but crucial for understanding the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the later objection
Read from 'واعترض بعض المتأخرين' to 'لا يمكن إجراؤه في تمايز الأعدام'. Understand why some say the relation is opposite.
2
Follow the response
Al-Jurjani explains that the dispute is about non-existents (معدومات), not non-existences (عدمات). Non-existences, when considered in the mind, become existents and thus can be distinct.
He quotes Ibn Sina to support the point.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author asserts that the dispute should be a branch of mental existence, even if scholars did not explicitly make it so. The distinction of non-existents in the mind does not negate the absolute non-existent.
The passage ends with 'فتأمل فإنه دقيق'—a call for careful reflection.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : المعدومات هل تتمايز أم لا . المعدوم أنه فرع الخلاف في الوجود الذهني و ذلك لأنه ( لا) تمايز بين المعدومات ) إلا في العقل) فإن تلك الأحكام إنما تتصف بها المعدومات بحسب نفس الأمر في العقل لا في الخارج، إذ لا ثبوت للمعدوم الخارجي في الخارج، حتى يمكن اتصافه فيه بشيء فلا تمايز بينها إلا في العقل ( فإن كان ذلك ( التمايز الحاصل لها في العقل لوجود لها في الذهن لم يتصور معدوم مطلقاً) بل كل ما يتصور من ( قوله : أي) في الخلاف إلخ قد عرفت أن هذا الخلاف غير مختص بالمعدومات الممكنة وبالتمايز في الخارج، فمن قال : المراد الخلاف بين القائلين بأن لا ثبوت للمعدوم، وإلا فلا يصح التفريع لم يأت بشيء . قوله : ( إنما تتصف إلخ بمعنى أن العقل إذ لاحظها وجدها متصفة بتلك الأحكام في حد ذاتها مع قطع النظر عن اعتبار معتبر وفرض فارض وهذا الاتصاف الانتزاعي لا يتوقف على وجود العقل وملاحظته فلا يرد أن ذلك الاختلاف والاقتضاء غير مشروط بالتعقل، إذ لو فرض عدمه بل عدم العاقل يكون ذلك الاختلاف بحاله . من العلة التامة أصلاً ، فلا يوجب عدم المشروط ولك أن تقول : مراده أن عدم الشرط من حيث إنه شرط يوجب عدم المشروط من حيث هو كذلك بخلاف عدم غير الشرط من حيث هو كذلك نعم إذا لوحظ من حيث إنه جزء آخر من العلة التامة فعدمه أيضاً يستلزم عدم المشروط، والأول أظهر. فلا يصح ، قوله : ( أي في الخلاف في تمايز المعدوم إلخ) أي بين القائلين بأن لا ثبوت للمعدوم، وإلا التقدير، ثم اعلم أن الممتنع يستلزم الممتنع، وكذا الخياليات فلا تكون المسألة فرع ثبوت المعدوم الممكن غير الخيالي واعترض على قوله : لأنه لا تمايز إلا في العقل بأن الدليل على ذلك التمايز اختلاف مقتضيات الأعدام كما تحققته وذلك الاختلاف والاقتضاء غير مشروط بالتعقل، إذ لو فرض أن لا عاقل في الوجود يكون الاختلاف، والاقتضاء بحاله فكذا التمايز، وقد نبهت على جوابه فيما سبق فليتذكر هذا واعترض بعض المتأخرين أيضاً على هذا الحق بأن بيان التفريع بهذا الوجه مع أنه مردود بأن الأمر بالعكس لأن الفلاسفة المثبتين للوجود الذهني يقولون : بتمايز المعدومات وجمهور المتكلمين النافين للوجود الذهني هم القائلون بعدم تمايزها لا يمكن إجراؤه في تمايز العدمات، إذ لا يمكن أن يقال: إن كان ذلك التمايز لكونها موجودة في الذهن لم تكن الأعدام متمايزة، إذ الأعدام لكونها موجودة في الذهن لا تخرج عن كونها أعداماً ، بل إنما تخرج عن كونها معدومات، فالأولى أن يقال : لما كان التمايز وصفا ثبوتيا يستدعي ثبوت الموصوف به فمن أثبت الوجود الذهني حكم بتمايز الأعدام والمعدومات الخارجية، لما لها من الثبوت الذهني ومن نفاء حكم بعدم التمايز لعدم الثبوت أصلاً، وهذا الاعتراض مع بيان التفريع بالوجه المذكور مذكور في شرح المقاصد، سوى قوله : لا يمكن إجراؤه في تمايز الأعدام إلخ وأقول : أما الجواب عن الرد بأن الأمر بالعكس، فهو أن مراد المعدومات والعدمات ومفهوم المعدوم المطلق والعدم المطلق كان موجوداً في الذهن فالامتياز الحاصل هناك ثابت للموجود لا للمعدوم المطلق الذي لا وجود له . قوله : (ثابت للموجود أي للوجود مدخل في التمايز إذ لولاه انتفي التمايز، فلا يرد أنا لا نسلم كونه للوجود ضرورة أن عدم الشروط متمايز عن عدم غيره لا الصورتين الحاصلتين منهما إلا أن ظرف التمايز الذهن . ( قوله : (لا للمعدوم (المطلق أي من حيث إنه معدوم وإن كان ثابتاً لذات المعدوم، وهذا هو المطابق لما في إلهيات الشفاء والتحصيل من أنه كيف يوجب على المعدوم حكم ؟ ومعنى قولنا : أن المعدوم كذا إن وصف كونه كذا حاصل للمعدوم أي موجود له، فذلك الوصف لا يخلو، إما أن يكون في نفسه موجوداً أو معدوماً، فإن كان موجوداً، فيكون للمعدوم صفة موجودة فالموصوف بها موجود لا محالة فالمعدوم موجود، وإن كانت الصفة معدومة، فكيف يكون المعدوم في نفسه موجوداً لشيء؟ . فإن ما لا يكون موجوداً في نفسه يستحيل أن يكون موجوداً لشيء انتهى، وما قالوا من أن المعدومات متمايزة فمرادهم أن المعدومات الخارجية متمايزة في الذهن وهو غير مناف لنفي التمايز عن المعدوم المطلق، فاندفع ما قاله صاحب المقاصد : من أن الأمر على عكس ما قال صاحب المواقف، لأن الحكماء المثبتين للوجود الذهني قائلون بالتمايز وجمهور المتكلمين النافين له قائلون بعدم التمايز، ومع ذلك لا يمكن إجراؤه في تمايز العدمات، إذ لا يمكن أن يقال : أن ذلك التمايز إذا كان لكونها موجودة في الذهن لم تخرج عن كونها إعداماً، بل عن كونها معدومات. أما الأول فلما القولين وأما الثاني فلان الكلام في تمايز المعدومات من حيث إنها معدومات وإذا كانت الأعدام موجودة في الذهن لم تكن معدومات، وكذا ظهر فساد ما ذكره شارح التجريد من أن الأولى في وجه التفريع أن يقال : لما كان التمايز وصفاً ثبوتياً يستدعي ثبوت المثبت له فمن أثبت الوجود الذهني حكم بتمايز الأعدام، والمعدومات الخارجية لما لها من الثبوت الذهني ومن نفاه حكم مر من اختلاف المصنف بيان ما هو الحق في هذه المسألة، وأن الخلاف في التمايز ينبغي أن يكون فرع الخلاف في الوجود الذهني، وإن لم يجعلوا كذلك، وليس مراده إنهم إنما اختلفوا في تمايز المعدوم بناء على اختلافهم في الوجود الذهني، وإن أشعر به كلام الشارح في المقصد السابع من مرصد الوحدة والكثرة على أن أبا علي ذكره في وجودية الإمكان أنه لو لم يكن وجودياً لم يكن فرق بين إمكانه لا ولا إمكان له لعدم التمايز بين العدمات فيفهم منه أن الحكماء لا يقولون بتمايز الأعدام على وفق ما ذكره المصنف، إلا أن يثبت أن ما ذكره أبو علي كلام إلزامي، وأما عن قوله لا يمكن إجراؤه في العدمات فهو أن الاختلاف في تمايز العدمات ليس من حيث إنها عدمات، بل من حيث إنها معدومات، وقد أشار إليه الشارح بقوله : وأما المعدومات التي من جملتها العدمات ففي تمايزها خلاف فعلى تقدير القول بالوجود الذهني تكون الأعدام متمايزة، لكن لا باعتبار أنها معدومات، بل باعتبار أنها موجودات في الذهن ولا يضرنا عدم خروجها بالوجود الذهني عن كونها عدمات بل يكفينا خروجها عن كونها معدومات فتأمل فإنه دقيق .
First Observatory - Fifth Goal: Do non-existents differ? [The statement] 'Non-existents are a branch of the dispute concerning mental existence, and that because there is no distinction between non-existents except in the intellect, for those judgments are attributed to non-existents according to the actual state of affairs in the intellect, not in the external world, since the external non-existent has no subsistence in the external world such that it could be described with something, so there is no distinction between them except in the intellect. If that distinction obtained for them in the intellect [and] they had existence in the mind, then no absolute non-existent would be conceived; rather, everything conceived from... (He said: 'i.e.') concerning the dispute etc. You know that this dispute is not specific to possible non-existents and to distinction in the external world; so whoever says: 'The intended [meaning] is the dispute between those who say there is no subsistence for the non-existent, otherwise the branching would not be correct,' has not come with anything. His statement: 'They are only described etc.' means that the intellect, when it observes them, finds them described with those judgments in their essences, disregarding any consideration and any supposition, and this abstract description does not depend on the existence of the intellect and its observation, so it is not objected that that difference and implication are not conditional upon intellection, for if one supposes its absence, rather the absence of the intellect, that difference would remain as it is. ...from the complete cause at all, so it does not necessitate the absence of the conditioned; but you may say: His intention is that the absence of the condition insofar as it is a condition necessitates the absence of the conditioned insofar as it is that, unlike the absence of something other than the condition insofar as it is that; yes, if it is observed insofar as it is another part of the complete cause, then its absence also entails the absence of the conditioned, and the first is more apparent. So it is not valid. His statement: '(i.e. concerning the dispute about the distinction of the non-existent etc.)' i.e., between those who say that there is no subsistence for the non-existent, otherwise the estimation... Then know that the impossible entails the impossible, and likewise imaginary things, so the issue is not a branch of the subsistence of the possible non-existent other than the imaginary. And an objection was raised against his statement: 'Because there is no distinction except in the intellect' that the evidence for that distinction is the difference in the implications of non-existences (privation) as you have verified, and that difference and implication are not conditional upon intellection, for if one supposes that there is no intellect in existence, the difference and implication would remain as they are, and likewise the distinction. And I have pointed out its answer previously, so let this be remembered. And some later scholars also objected to this truth that the explanation of the branching in this manner, while it is refuted by the point being the opposite, because the philosophers who affirm mental existence say: that non-existents are distinct, and the majority of theologians who deny mental existence are those who say they are not distinct, it cannot be applied to the distinction of non-existences (privation), for it cannot be said: If that distinction is because they exist in the mind, then the non-existences would not be distinct, for non-existences, because they exist in the mind, do not cease to be non-existences; rather, they only cease to be non-existents. So it is better to say: Since distinction is an affirmative attribute that requires the subsistence of the described, whoever affirms mental existence judges the distinction of non-existences and external non-existents, because they have mental subsistence, and whoever denies it judges the absence of distinction due to the absence of subsistence at all. This objection, along with the explanation of the branching in the mentioned manner, is mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid, except his statement: 'It cannot be applied to the distinction of non-existences etc.' And I say: As for the answer to the refutation that the matter is the opposite, it is that his intention [is that] non-existents and non-existences and the concept of absolute non-existent and absolute non-existence, if they existed in the mind, then the distinction obtained there is affirmed for the existent, not for the absolute non-existent which has no existence. His statement: '(Affirmed for the existent)' i.e., existence has an entry in distinction, for if it were not for it, distinction would be negated, so it is not objected that we do not grant that it is for existence necessarily, because the absence of conditions is distinct from the absence of something else without the two forms obtained from them, except that the locus of distinction is the intellect. His statement: '(Not for the absolute non-existent)' i.e., insofar as it is non-existent, even if it is affirmed for the essence of the non-existent. And this corresponds to what is in Ilahiyyat al-Shifa' and al-Tahsil concerning how can a judgment be imposed on the non-existent? The meaning of our saying: 'The non-existent is such-and-such' - the description of its being such-and-such is obtained for the non-existent, i.e., existent for it. So that description is either existent in itself or non-existent. If it is existent, then the non-existent has an existent attribute, so the described is necessarily existent, so the non-existent is existent. If the attribute is non-existent, then how can the non-existent be in itself existent for something? For that which is not existent in itself is impossible to be existent for something. End of quote. And their statement that non-existents are distinct means that external non-existents are distinct in the mind, which is not incompatible with the negation of distinction for the absolute non-existent. Thus what the author of al-Maqasid said is refuted: that the matter is the opposite of what the author of al-Mawaqif said, because the philosophers who affirm mental existence say distinction, and the majority of theologians who deny it say no distinction, and yet it cannot be applied to the distinction of non-existences, for it cannot be said: that distinction, if it is because they exist in the mind, they do not cease to be non-existences, but rather cease to be non-existents. As for the first, because of the two statements; and as for the second, because the discussion is about the distinction of non-existents insofar as they are non-existents, and if non-existences exist in the mind, they are not non-existents. Likewise, the corruption of what the commentator of al-Tajrid mentioned is apparent: that the best in the manner of branching is to say: Since distinction is an affirmative attribute that requires the subsistence of the affirmed, whoever affirms mental existence judges the distinction of non-existences and external non-existents because they have mental subsistence, and whoever denies it judges... ...from the difference of the author's statement of what is the truth in this issue, and that the dispute about distinction should be a branch of the dispute about mental existence, even if they did not make it so, and his intention is not that they only differed about the distinction of the non-existent based on their difference in mental existence, although the commentator's words in the Seventh Observatory of al-Wahda wa al-Kathra suggest that Abu Ali mentioned in his Wujudiyyat al-Imkan that if it were not existential, there would be no difference between its possibility and no possibility for it due to the lack of distinction between non-existences, from which it is understood that the philosophers do not affirm the distinction of non-existences in accordance with what the author mentioned, unless it is established that what Abu Ali mentioned is argumentative speech. And as for his statement that it cannot be applied to non-existences, it is that the difference in the distinction of non-existences is not insofar as they are non-existences, but insofar as they are non-existents, and the commentator indicated this by his saying: 'As for non-existents which include non-existences, there is dispute about their distinction. On the assumption of affirming mental existence, non-existences would be distinct, but not insofar as they are non-existences, but insofar as they are existents in the mind, and it does not harm us that they do not cease, by mental existence, to be non-existences; rather, it suffices us that they cease to be non-existents.' So ponder, for it is subtle.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a lengthy dialectical discussion about whether non-existents (المعدومات) can be distinct from one another. The author, al-Jurjani, begins by stating that the issue is a branch of the dispute about mental existence (الوجود الذهني). He explains that distinction between non-existents can only occur in the intellect, because in the external world they have no subsistence (ثبوت) to be described by anything. If, however, the distinction in the intellect implies that they have existence in the mind, then no absolute non-existent would be conceived—everything conceived would have some mental existence. The commentator then clarifies that the dispute is not restricted to possible non-existents or to external distinction. He addresses an objection that the differentiation of non-existents (like the difference between privations) does not depend on the intellect, because the differences in the implications of non-existences (مقتضيات الأعدام) are not conditional on intellection; even if no intellect existed, those differences would remain. The author notes that he has already answered this objection elsewhere. Then a later objection from some post-classical scholars is presented: the relation is actually the opposite of what the author claims, because the philosophers who affirm mental existence affirm distinction, while the theologians who deny mental existence deny it. Therefore, the dispute cannot be applied to non-existences (العدمات) in the same way, because if non-existences exist in the mind, they do not cease to be non-existences but merely cease to be non-existents. The objection recommends saying that distinction requires subsistence, so those who affirm mental existence judge distinction for non-existences and external non-existents, while those who deny it judge no distinction. The author responds by clarifying that the intended point is that the dispute *should* be a branch of the mental existence dispute, even if scholars did not actually make it so. He also distinguishes between non-existents (معدومات) and non-existences (عدمات): when non-existences are considered as existing in the mind, they are no longer non-existents (as such), so the distinction applies to them as mental existents, not as non-existences. He quotes Ibn Sina's Ilahiyyat al-Shifa' and al-Tahsil to argue that a judgment cannot be attributed to a non-existent unless that judgment itself has existence. Ultimately, the author concludes that the truth is that the dispute about distinction should be a branch of the mental existence dispute, and that the distinction of non-existents in the mind is not incompatible with the negation of distinction for the absolute non-existent. The passage ends with an encouragement to ponder, as it is subtle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if non-existences exist in the mind, they cease to be non-existences, so the distinction is irrelevant to non-existents.
Correction: Al-Jurjani clarifies that non-existences become mental existents, so the distinction applies to them as existents, not as non-existents. The debate is about non-existents (معدومات) which include non-existences, but they are considered as they are in the mind.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, how do non-existences (عدمات) become distinct?
A) They are distinct by their very nature as non-existences.
B) They become distinct because they are considered as mental existents.
C) They are distinct only in the external world.
D) They are not distinct at all.

Key Takeaway
The distinction of non-existents is a branch of the mental existence debate because non-existences are only distinct in the mind as mental existents, not as pure nothings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your past sins that no longer exist in external reality, do you treat them as real entities in your mind, causing you unnecessary shame or guilt? How can distinguishing between the sin as a past event and the mental concept of it help you find peace?
?
Al-Jurjani calls for careful reflection, saying 'ponder, for it is subtle.' Is there a theological topic you have accepted without deep thought? What is one such topic you will commit to studying carefully this week, even if it challenges your assumptions?
?
The objector in this lesson made a mistake by not defining his terms carefully. In your own life, when have you miscommunicated or misunderstood something because you failed to distinguish between similar concepts? How can you practice greater precision in your speech and understanding today?

Are Non-Existents Distinct? The Role of Mental Existence
States the key proposition that distinction requires existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the distinction of non-existents depends on mental existence, and how this argument underpins a key debate between philosophers and theologians.

Core Idea
Non-existents are distinct only in the mind, not in themselves.

This lesson addresses a classic debate in Islamic theology and philosophy: can non-existents be considered distinct from each other? The author of al-Mawaqif argues that distinction requires existence, so non-existents are distinct only if they have mental existence. We'll examine the core proposition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic matn and its translation. Notice the key terms: 'non-existents' (al-ma'dumat), 'privations' (al-'adamat), and 'absolute non-existent' (al-ma'dum al-mutlaq).
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the central claim
The passage states that distinction is affirmed for the existent, not for the absolute non-existent. Ask: What does this imply about non-existents in general?
3
Understand the role of existence
The commentary explains that existence is necessary for distinction. If something has no existence at all (neither external nor mental), it cannot be distinguished from anything else.
This sets up a conditional: if non-existents are distinct, they must have some form of existence.
4
Consider the objection and response
The passage anticipates an objection: what about privations (e.g., the absence of conditions) that seem distinct without existence? The response is that such distinction occurs only in the mind.
This ties the argument to the broader debate about mental existence.
5
Reflect on the broader debate
Consider how this argument divides philosophers (who affirm mental existence and thus allow for distinction of non-existents) from theologians (who deny mental existence and thus deny distinction).
The author implies that the disagreement about distinction is fundamentally a disagreement about mental existence.

قوله : (لا للمعدوم (المطلق أي من حيث إنه معدوم وإن كان ثابتاً لذات المعدوم، وهذا هو المطابق لما في إلهيات الشفاء والتحصيل من أنه كيف يوجب على المعدوم حكم ؟ ومعنى قولنا : أن المعدوم كذا إن وصف كونه كذا حاصل للمعدوم أي موجود ،له، فذلك الوصف لا يخلو، إما أن يكون في نفسه موجوداً أو معدوماً، فإن كان موجوداً، فيكون للمعدوم صفة موجودة فالموصوف بها موجود لا محالة فالمعدوم موجود، وإن كانت الصفة معدومة، فكيف يكون المعدوم في نفسه موجوداً لشيء؟ . فإن ما لا يكون موجوداً في نفسه يستحيل أن يكون موجوداً لشيء انتهى، وما قالوا من أن المعدومات متمايزة فمرادهم أن المعدومات الخارجية متمايزة في الذهن وهو غير مناف لنفي التمايز عن المعدوم المطلق، فاندفع ما قاله صاحب المقاصد : من أن الأمر على عكس ما قال صاحب المواقف، لأن الحكماء المثبتين للوجود الذهني قائلون بالتمايز وجمهور المتكلمين النافين له قائلون بعدم التمايز، ومع ذلك لا يمكن إجراؤه في تمايز ،العدمات، إذ لا يمكن أن يقال : أن ذلك التمايز إذا كان لكونها موجودة في الذهن لم تخرج عن كونها إعداماً، بل عن كونها معدومات. أما الأول فلما القولين وأما الثاني فلان الكلام في تمايز المعدومات من حيث إنها معدومات وإذا كانت الأعدام موجودة في الذهن لم تكن معدومات، وكذا ظهر فساد ما ذكره شارح التجريد من أن الأولى في وجه التفريع أن يقال : لما كان التمايز وصفاً ثبوتياً يستدعي ثبوت المثبت له فمن أثبت الوجود الذهني حكم بتمايز الأعدام، والمعدومات الخارجية لما لها من الثبوت الذهني ومن نفاه حكم
