أصلاً ( وإلا تصور ما هو معدوم مطلقاً لا وجود له خارجاً ولا ذهناً مع أنه متصف بالامتياز فالمعدومات متمايزة .
The First Observatory - The Fifth Section: Are non-existents (al-ma'dumat) distinct or not? Non-existents, privations (al-'adamat), and the concept of the absolute non-existent (al-ma'dum al-mutlaq) and absolute privation (al-'adam al-mutlaq) were existing in the mind; so the distinction occurring there is affirmed for the existent, not for the absolute non-existent which has no existence. He said: (Affirmed for the existent) i.e., existence has an entry in distinction, for if it were not for it, distinction would be negated. So it is not objected: We do not concede its being for existence, because necessarily the privation of conditions is distinct from the privation of other than them, not the two forms arising from them, except that the locus of distinction is the mind. He said: (Not for the non-existent (absolute) i.e., insofar as it is non-existent, even if it is affirmed for the essence of the non-existent. And this corresponds to what is in the Ilahiyyat of al-Shifa' and al-Tahsıl about how can a judgment be imposed on the non-existent? The meaning of our saying: 'The non-existent is such' is that the description of its being such is obtained for the non-existent, i.e., exists for it. So that description is either existent in itself or non-existent. If it is existent, then the non-existent has an existent attribute, so the described is necessarily existent, so the non-existent is existent. If the attribute is non-existent, then how can the non-existent in itself exist for something? For what is not existent in itself is impossible to be existent for something — end. And what they said that non-existents are distinct means that external non-existents are distinct in the mind, and this does not conflict with negating distinction for the absolute non-existent. So what the author of al-Maqasid said is repelled: that the matter is the opposite of what the author of al-Mawaqif said, because the philosophers who affirm mental existence say distinction, and the majority of theologians who negate it say no distinction. Nevertheless, it cannot be applied to the distinction of privations (al-'adamat), because it cannot be said that distinction, if it is because they are existent in the mind, does not remove them from being privations, but from being non-existents. As for the first, because of the two statements; as for the second, because the discussion is about the distinction of non-existents insofar as they are non-existents, and if privations are existent in the mind, they are not non-existents. Similarly, the corruption of what the commentator of al-Tajrid mentioned is apparent: that the best in the manner of derivation is to say: Since distinction is a positive attribute requiring the positivity of that to which it is attributed, whoever affirms mental existence judges the distinction of privations, and external non-existents because of their mental positivity, and whoever negates it judges no distinction due to lack of positivity. From what has passed of the disagreement of the author, the clarification of what is correct in this issue appears: that the disagreement in distinction should be a branch of the disagreement in mental existence, even if they did not make it so. And he does not mean that they only differed in the distinction of the non-existent based on their disagreement in mental existence, even if the speech of the commentator in the seventh section of the observatory of unity and multiplicity suggests that Abu Ali mentioned in the existentiality of possibility that if it were not existential, there would be no difference between its possibility and its impossibility due to lack of distinction between privations. So it is understood that the philosophers do not affirm the distinction of privations in accordance with what the author mentioned, unless it is proven that what Abu Ali mentioned is a compelling statement. As for his saying: 'It cannot be applied to privations,' it is that the disagreement in the distinction of privations is not insofar as they are privations, but insofar as they are non-existents. The commentator indicated this by saying: As for non-existents which include privations, there is disagreement about their distinction. So on the supposition of affirming mental existence, privations are distinct, but not insofar as they are privations, but insofar as they are existents in the mind. And it does not harm us that by mental existence they do not cease to be privations; rather, it suffices us that they cease to be non-existents. So reflect, for it is subtle. Originally (Otherwise, conceive what is absolutely non-existent with no existence externally or mentally, while it is described by distinction, so non-existents are distinct.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a detailed examination of whether non-existents (al-ma'dumat) can be considered distinct from each other. The author begins by distinguishing between non-existents and privations (al-'adamat) and introduces the concept of the absolute non-existent (al-ma'dum al-mutlaq), which has no existence at all. He argues that distinction requires existence, so if a non-existent is distinguished, that distinction must be based on its mental existence, not on its absolute non-existence. The commentary then explains a statement from the matn: 'Affirmed for the existent' means that existence plays a role in distinction; without existence, there would be no distinction. A potential objection is raised: could not one say that the privation of conditions is distinct from the privation of other things without invoking existence? The author responds that such distinction occurs only in the mind. The passage then quotes from Avicenna's al-Shifa' and al-Tahsıl, arguing that if a description applies to a non-existent, the description itself must be either existent or non-existent. If it is existent, then the described must be existent, which is contradictory; if it is non-existent, then something non-existent cannot be attributed to anything. Thus, the description of a non-existent as distinct is problematic. The author clarifies that when philosophers say non-existents are distinct, they mean external non-existents are distinct in the mind, which does not contradict the denial of distinction for the absolute non-existent. He then refutes the author of al-Maqasid, who claimed the opposite of al-Mawaqif's position, by noting that philosophers affirm mental existence and so can speak of distinction, while most theologians deny mental existence and thus deny distinction. However, this does not apply to privations because if they are existent in the mind, they are no longer privations but rather mental existents. The passage further criticizes a commentator on al-Tajrid for misconstruing the issue. Ultimately, the author concludes that the disagreement about distinction should depend on the disagreement about mental existence, even though it is not always framed that way. He ends with an argument that if one does not accept this, then consider a non-existent with no existence at all—it cannot be described as distinct, so non-existents must be distinct only in the mind.

معدوم مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that non-existents (e.g., different absences) are distinct in themselves, even without any existence.
Correction: The passage argues that distinction requires existence. If non-existents are distinct, it is only because they have mental existence; the absolute non-existent cannot be distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the absolute non-existent not be distinct?
A) Because it has no existence
B) Because it is a privation
C) Because it is conceived in the mind
D) Because distinction requires existence

Key Takeaway
Non-existents are distinct only in the mind, because distinction presupposes existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you held a strong belief about something that turned out to be purely mental—a worry, a hope, a prejudice. Did you treat that mental object as if it had real existence? This lesson teaches that distinction requires existence. What distinction have you been making that might only exist in your mind? Commit to re-examining one such distinction today.
?
The absolute non-existent has no distinction. Yet we often argue about things that are not real. Consider an argument you had recently. Was the subject of the argument something that actually exists, or was it a mental construct? How would acknowledging its non-existence change your stance?
?
This lesson says that distinction (tamayyuz) is a positive attribute that must belong to an existent. In your own spiritual life, are you making distinctions among things (e.g., sins, virtues, people) based on mental categories rather than reality? How can you ground your judgments in what truly exists?

The Third Argument: Necessity vs Possibility of Non-Existent Essences
Presents the third argument that non-existents cannot be fixed without contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against the fixity of non-existents: if non-existents had fixity, they would be either necessary (implying multiplicity) or possible (implying origination), both of which are problematic.

Core Idea
Non-existent things cannot be fixed because that would imply either multiple necessary beings or temporal origination.

The debate over whether the non-existent is a thing hinges on its 'fixity' (taqarrur) in the state of non-existence. The third argument attacks this fixity by showing that any fixed non-existent must be either necessary or possible—both options are unacceptable. Let us examine this argument as presented in the commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage that presents the third argument. Notice how it sets up two possibilities for the fixity of non-existents: necessary or possible.
2
Identify the two branches
The argument says if non-existents are fixed, their fixity is either necessary (wajib) or possible (mumkin). Understand why necessity leads to multiplicity of the Necessary Being, and possibility leads to origination (huduth).
The conclusion is that both are false, so non-existents cannot be fixed.
3
Analyze the response about the definition of the Necessary
Notice the reply: 'al-wajib ma yajibu wujuduhu, la ma yajibu taqarruruhu' (the necessary is that whose existence is necessary, not that whose fixity is necessary). This distinguishes between existence and fixity.
This response attempts to save the argument from the objection that the Necessary Being could have fixed non-existents.
4
Reflect on the polemical nature
Understand that this argument is often considered ilzami (polemical) against the Mu'tazila who assert the eternity of fixity. The commentary emphasizes that the proof shows non-eternity of fixity, not necessarily non-existence.

The First Observatory - The Sixth Aim: Is the non-existent a thing or not? That which is established in the state of non-existence is either necessary of establishment, so it would be necessary while it was assumed possible, and it would also necessitate multiplicity of the Necessary (first), or it is possible of establishment, and every possible is originated, so those essences would be originated preceded by negation and non-fixity, and that is the required. So it was said: The necessary is that whose existence is necessary, not that whose establishment is necessary, which is more general than existence. The Fourth Aspect: That non-existence is a negative attribute (i.e., an attribute that is negated, not positive, because it is the removal of existence), and the described by a negative attribute is negated (i.e., negated, not fixed), just as the described by a positive attribute (i.e., a positive attribute) is positive (i.e., fixed, not negated). So the non-existent described by non-existence is negated. Al-Amidi said: 'This approach, though many theologians such as Muhammad al-Shahrastani and others have hovered around its meaning, yet as established and refined, we have not found it for anyone else.' The learned: His saying: 'But possible of establishment, so it is in need of an efficient cause for its establishment, and since it is established that the agent is free [to choose], it becomes originated, because everything emanating from a free agent is originated. So the objection by His attributes is not valid.' His saying: 'And that is the required' because it is established that non-existents have no fixity in themselves; it is only from the agent. So the objection of the author of al-Maqasid that the required is the non-establishment of them and the necessary implication of the proof is the non-eternity of their establishment is not valid. And there is no need for what was said: 'The argument is polemical, and the Mu'tazilites assert the eternity of their establishment.' His saying: 'The necessary is that whose existence is necessary.' For if it is said: 'Just as multiplicity of what is necessary of existence is impossible, so multiplicity of what is necessary as an attribute of His attributes is impossible, because it necessitates His being necessary of existence,' I say: That is in an attribute whose being described by it is posterior to existence. But regarding fixity, no, because it is prior to existence, so it is permissible that what is necessary of fixity be possible of existence. His saying: 'A negative attribute' — apparently the addition is explicative, i.e., an attribute whose essence is negation, because it is the removal of existence. And on the interpretation of it as negation, it is appropriate to say 'non-fixed' rather than 'non-positive', whether it is intended as what does not have negation included in its concept or as what is existent, for the inference from the non-fixity of the attribute to the non-fixity of the described is stronger and more evident than the inference from its non-fixity in one of the two meanings to his non-fixity. His saying: 'Hovered over its meaning' — in al-Qamus: 'hāma fī al-amr' means he persisted. So the word 'over' is in the sense of 'in' or by implication of superiority. His saying: 'And it is extremely weak' with kasra on 'inna' as a conjunct to his saying 'al-Amidi said', so it is from the words of the author. His saying: 'We do not accept that the described [by negation] is negated' can be refuted by saying that if it were fixed, the fixity of that attribute would be necessary. His saying: 'The third: the essences established [in non-existence] etc.' He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: 'This proof, despite its dependence on every possible of fixity being originated in the sense of preceded by negation, does not negate that essences are fixed without existence. Rather, its conclusion is that their fixity in non-existence is preceded by their negation. And you are aware that the proof is polemical, so it suffices.' (And it is extremely precise and good, but it is extremely weak) and ugly, because we do not accept that the described by a negative attribute is negated, due to the possibility of the existent being described by a negation (i.e., a negative attribute that has no fixity in itself, like the description of Zayd by blindness). As for his saying 'just as the described by a positive attribute is positive', it is an analogical argument without a commonality between the compared and the standard, and with the evident difference between them, because the fixity of a thing for another is a branch of the fixity of that other in itself, so it is not permissible for the non-existent to be described by a positive attribute; rather, the described by it must be fixed in itself. And the non-existence of a thing from another is not a branch of the non-existence of that other in itself, so it is permissible for the existent to be described by a negative attribute, so it is not necessary that the described by it be non-existent in itself. The Fifth Aspect: If non-existents fixed in non-existence were distinct by their essences, then every two different things would be, and their establishment in the described externally, so it would not be a negative attribute, just as the attributes of genera fixed for the possible non-existent are fixed according to them, unlike the description of existents by negative attributes, for it does not require their existence. Unless it is said: The necessary is their fixity in the described, and the negated is what has no fixity in itself. As for the answer that what is meant is that the described by a negative attribute of itself is negated, for non-existence is the negation of that thing itself, unlike other negative attributes, for they are not the negation of itself but the negation of an attribute of it, like blindness, for it is the negation of sight, not the negation of the essence of the blind, it is nothing, because those who affirm the fixity of the non-existent do not acknowledge that non-existence is the negation of the thing in itself, but rather the negation of the attribute of existence from it, while the thing is fixed in itself. His saying: 'And the non-existence [of a thing from another] etc.' means that being described by a negative attribute, i.e., what has negation included in its concept, is not a real description, for it is in reality a statement of the non-existence of the subject of the negation from a thing, and the non-existence of a thing from another does not necessitate its non-existence in itself. So what was said: 'The approach is incomplete because the discussion is about being described by a negative attribute, not about the negation of description, so it must be said: And the description of a thing by a negative attribute is not a branch of its non-existence in itself' — it is nothing. His saying: 'And it is extremely weak, since we do not accept etc.' It is answered that the meaning of al-Amidi's statement is that the described by a negative attribute of itself is negated, and by a positive attribute of itself is positive. And the described by blindness, i.e., the negation of sight, is not Zayd's essence, for example, but rather his sight itself, i.e., his sight described as being non-existent, so it is also described by the negation of itself and is negated. So his statement is extremely precise. And one may say: We do not accept that the described by non-existence is described by the negation of itself; rather, it is described by the negation of its existence added to it, and that is apparent. And we do not accept that the described by the negation of its existence is negated in the sense of not being fixed, for that is the point of dispute. Yes, it is negated in the sense of not existing, but that does not help, because the discussion is about the negation of fixity, and the claim is its generality from existence. Moreover, his saying 'just as the described by a positive attribute is positive' suggests absoluteness in the compared as in the standard, so let it be considered. His saying: 'The fifth: If they were distinct etc.' It can be said, by analogy with what he will mention about unity, that distinctness requires the condition of non-existence. Consider.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a continuous commentary presenting and discussing multiple arguments (wujuh) concerning whether the non-existent (al-ma'dum) has any reality or fixity. The discussion is part of the larger topic of al-Maqsad al-Sadis (the Sixth Aim) in the First Observatory. The commentary addresses the third, fourth, and fifth arguments, with objections and replies. The commentator first outlines a proof (likely the third argument) that if non-existents had fixity, they would be either necessary or possible; necessity is impossible due to the principle of tawhid, and possibility implies origination (huduth). The response clarifies that the necessary is defined by existence, not fixity. Then the fourth argument is introduced: 'adam (non-existence) is a negative attribute (sifat nafy). The described by negation is itself negated, just as the described by affirmation is affirmed. Al-Amidi notes this approach is unique. The commentary then addresses objections: if non-existents were fixed, they would need an efficient cause; the free agent causes origination. It refutes the objection that this conflicts with divine attributes. It also explains that the proof is polemical (ilzami) against the Mu'tazila who assert eternal fixity. The commentator discusses the meaning of 'sifat nafy', suggesting it is better phrased as 'non-fixed' rather than 'non-positive'. A later section returns to the third argument, citing Sharh al-Maqasid: the proof that every possible of fixity is originated (preceded by negation) does not deny that essences are fixed without existence, but only that their fixity in non-existence is preceded. Then the commentary critiques the fourth argument, noting that an existent can be described by a negative attribute (e.g., blindness) without being negated in itself. The analogy with positive attributes fails due to the asymmetry: positive attributes require a fixed subject, while negative attributes do not. The fifth argument then considers whether non-existents would be distinct by their essences; if they were, their distinction would be a positive attribute, contradicting the claim that 'adam is negative. The objection that the description by negation of existence does not entail non-fixity is rejected because the dispute is about fixity, not existence. The commentary ends with a reference to a later discussion on unity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Necessary Being is defined by necessary fixity, which would allow non-existents to have necessary fixity without implying multiplicity.
Correction: The Necessary is defined by necessary existence, not fixity. Fixity is broader and includes non-existents; necessary existence is specific to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, if a non-existent had necessary fixity, what would follow?
A) It would be possible.
B) It would be originated.
C) It would imply multiple necessary beings.
D) It would be identical to existence.

Key Takeaway
The third argument uses a dichotomy to show that non-existents cannot have fixity: if their fixity were necessary, it would compromise tawhid; if possible, it would entail origination, contradicting the claim of eternal fixity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything other than Allah is contingent and originated, then what does that mean for the things you hold onto most tightly—your reputation, your wealth, your relationships? Are you willing to see them as ultimately non-fixed, dependent on Allah's will every moment?
?
The argument shows that assuming eternal fixity for created things leads to logical contradictions. Is there an area of your life where you act as if your current state—good or bad—is eternally fixed, forgetting that only Allah has necessary existence? How would your actions change if you truly believed everything else is originated and passing?
?
The distinction between necessary existence and necessary fixity is subtle but crucial. Are you careful to not ascribe the kind of permanence that belongs only to Allah to anything else—even your own understanding of yourself? What would it take to let go of that comfortable illusion of fixity and live in total reliance on the One who alone is Necessary?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
5 min
The Fourth Argument: 'Adam as a Negative Attribute
Presents the fourth argument that non-existence is a negative attribute, so the described is negated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument which states that non-existence is a negative attribute, and therefore the described (the non-existent) is itself negated, just as a positive attribute affirms the subject.

Core Idea
If non-existence is a negative attribute, then the non-existent must be negated (non-fixed), not a real thing.

The fourth argument shifts focus to the nature of 'adam (non-existence) itself. It claims that 'adam is a negative attribute (sifat nafy), and just as a positive attribute requires a positive (fixed) subject, a negative attribute requires a negated (non-fixed) subject. This directly challenges the Mu'tazilite view that non-existents have positive fixity. Let us examine this argument as presented by al-Amidi and the subsequent commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth argument passage
Read the Arabic text that introduces 'adam as a negative attribute and the analogy with positive attributes. Note the structure: 'adam is sifat nafy, so the described is manfi.
2
Understand the analogy
The argument draws an analogy: just as a positive attribute (sifat ithbatiyya) makes the described positive (muthbit), a negative attribute makes the described negated (manfi). So the non-existent described by 'adam is negated, i.e., not fixed.
This is a key move: it equates description by negation with the negation of the subject itself.
3
Appreciate al-Amidi's remark
Al-Amidi says this approach, though touched upon by earlier scholars, was not found in such a refined form. This indicates the argument's novelty and importance.
4
Consider the initial defense
The commentary defends the argument by clarifying that if non-existents were fixed, they would need a cause, and the free agent causes origination. Also, it responds to an objection about divine attributes.
This defense shows the argument is polemical (ilzami) against the Mu'tazila.

مختار تكون محدثة لأن كل صادر عن الفاعل المختار ،محدث، فلا يرد النقض بصفاته تعالى . قوله : (وهو المطلوب) لأنه ثبت أن المعدومات ليس لها ثبوت في أنفسها إنما هو من الفاعل، فلا يرد ما أورده صاحب المقاصد من أن المطلوب عدم ،تقررها واللازم من الدليل عدم أزلية تقررها، ولا يحتاج إلى ما قيل : إن الحجة إلزامية والمعتزلة قائلون بأزلية تقررها . قوله : ( الواجب ما يجب وجوده فإن قيل : كما يمتنع تعدد ما يجب وجوده يمتنع تعدد ما يجب صفة من صفاته، لأنه يستلزم كونه واجب الوجود، قلت ذلك في صفة يتأخر الاتصاف به عن الوجود، وأما في الثبوت فكلا لكونه مقدماً على الوجود، فيجوز أن يكون ما يجب ثبوته
ممكناً وجوده .
قوله : (صفة نفي الظاهر أن الإضافة بيانية أي صفة حقيقته النفي لأنه رفع الوجود وعلى تقدير تأويله بالنفي، فاللائق أن يقال : غير ثابتة لا غير ثبوتية سواء أريد بها ما ليس السلب داخلاً مفهومه أو ما هو موجود فإن الاستدلال بعدم ثبوت الصفة على عدم ثبوت الموصوف أقوى، وأظهر من الاستدلال بعدم ثبوتها لأحد المعنيين على عدم ثبوته . قوله : (حوم) على (معناه في القاموس حوم في الأمر استدام فكلمة على بمعنى في أو
بتضمين معنى الاستعلاء.

المصنف .
قوله: (لا) نسلم أن المتصف إلخ يمكن دفعه بأنه لو كان ثابتاً لزم ثبوت تلك الصفة
قوله : ( الثالث الذوات المتقررة إلخ ) قال في شرح المقاصد : هذا الدليل مع ابتنائه على كون كل ممكن الثبوت محدثاً بمعنى المسبوق بالنفي لا ينفي كون الذوات ثابتة بدون الوجود، بل غايته أن ثبوتها في العدم مسبوق بنفيها وأنت خبير بأن الدليل إلزامي فيتم .
( وهو في غاية الإحكام والحسن وإنه في غاية الضعف ) والقبح ( إذ لا نسلم أن المتصف بصفة النفي نفي لجواز اتصاف الموجود بالسلب ( أي بالصفة السلبية التي لا ثبوت لها في نفسها كاتصاف زيد بالعمى ( وأما قوله كما أن الموصوف بصفة الإثبات إثبات فقياس) تمثيلي (من غير جامع) بين المقيس والمقيس عليه (مع ظهور الفرق ( بينهما لأن ثبوت الشيء لغيره فرع على ثبوت ذلك الغير في نفسه، فلا يجوز أن يتصف المعدوم بصفة ،ثبوتية، بل لا بد أن يكون الموصوف بها ثابتاً في نفسه، وليس انتفاء الشيء من غيره فرعاً عن انتفاء ذلك الغير في نفسه، فجاز أن يتصف الموجود بصفة سلبية، فلا يجب أن يكون الموصوف بها منتفياً في نفسه. 
الوجه الخامس) المعدومات الثابتة في العدم لو) تباينت لذواتها كان كل شيئين مختلفين
،
وتقررها في الموصوف في الخارج، فلا يكون صفة نفي كما أن صفات الأجناس الثابتة للمعدوم الممكنة ثابتة عندهم بخلاف اتصاف الموجودات بصفات السلب فإنه لا يستدعي وجودها ، اللهم إلا أن يقال : اللازم ثبوتها في الموصوف والمنفي ما لا ثبوت له في نفسه، وأما الجواب بأن المراد أن الموصوف بصفة نفي نفسه منفي، فإن العدم نفي نفس ذلك الشيء بخلاف الصفة السلبية الأخرى، فإنها ليست نفي نفسه بل نفي صفة من صفاته كالعمى، فإنه نفي البصر لا نفي ذات الأعمى، فليس بشيء لأن القائلين بثبوت المعدوم لا يعترفون بأن العدم نفي الشيء في نفسه، بل نفي صفة الوجود عنه والشيء ثابت في نفسه.
قوله : ( وليس انتفاء إلخ) يعني أن الاتصاف بالصفة السلبية أي ما يكون السلب داخلاً في مفهومه ليس باتصاف حقيقي، فإنه في الحقيقة عبارة عن انتفاء مدخول السلب عن شيء، وانتفاء الشيء عن غيره لا يقتضي انتفاءه في نفسه فما قيل: إن التقريب غير تام لأن الكلام في الاتصاف بالصفة السلبية، لا في سلب الاتصاف، فالواجب أن يقال وليس اتصاف شيء بالصفة السلبية فرع انتفائه في نفسه ليس بشيء.
قوله : ( وإنه في غاية الضعف إذ لا نسلم إلخ) أجيب عنه بأن معنى كلام الآمدي أن الموصوف بصفة نفي نفسه منفي، وبصفة إثبات نفسه مثبت والموصوف بالعمى أعني نفي البصر، ليس ذات زيد مثلاً بل نفس بصره أي بصره متصف بأنه عديم، فهو أيضاً موصوف بنفي نفسه ومنفي فكلامه في غاية الإحكام ولقائل أن يقول : لا نسلم أن المتصف بالعدم متصف بصفة نفي نفسه، بل هو متصف بصفة نفي وجوده الزائد عليه وهو ظاهر ، و ولا نسلم أن المتصف بصفة نفي وجوده منفي بمعنى أنه غير ثابت فإنه المتنازع فيه نعم هو منفي بمعنى أنه غير موجود لكنه لا يفيد لأن الكلام في نفي الثبوت، والمدعى عمومه من الوجود على أن قوله : كما أن الموصوف بصفة الإثبات إثبات يشعر بالإطلاق في المقيس كما في المقيس عليه فليتأمل. قوله : ( الخامس لو تباينت إلخ يمكن أن يقال : قياساً على ما سيذكره في الوحدة اقتضاء التباين بشرط العدم تأمل.
The First Observatory - The Sixth Aim: Is the non-existent a thing or not? That which is established in the state of non-existence is either necessary of establishment, so it would be necessary while it was assumed possible, and it would also necessitate multiplicity of the Necessary (first), or it is possible of establishment, and every possible is originated, so those essences would be originated preceded by negation and non-fixity, and that is the required. So it was said: The necessary is that whose existence is necessary, not that whose establishment is necessary, which is more general than existence. The Fourth Aspect: That non-existence is a negative attribute (i.e., an attribute that is negated, not positive, because it is the removal of existence), and the described by a negative attribute is negated (i.e., negated, not fixed), just as the described by a positive attribute (i.e., a positive attribute) is positive (i.e., fixed, not negated). So the non-existent described by non-existence is negated. Al-Amidi said: 'This approach, though many theologians such as Muhammad al-Shahrastani and others have hovered around its meaning, yet as established and refined, we have not found it for anyone else.' The learned: His saying: 'But possible of establishment, so it is in need of an efficient cause for its establishment, and since it is established that the agent is free [to choose], it becomes originated, because everything emanating from a free agent is originated. So the objection by His attributes is not valid.' His saying: 'And that is the required' because it is established that non-existents have no fixity in themselves; it is only from the agent. So the objection of the author of al-Maqasid that the required is the non-establishment of them and the necessary implication of the proof is the non-eternity of their establishment is not valid. And there is no need for what was said: 'The argument is polemical, and the Mu'tazilites assert the eternity of their establishment.' His saying: 'The necessary is that whose existence is necessary.' For if it is said: 'Just as multiplicity of what is necessary of existence is impossible, so multiplicity of what is necessary as an attribute of His attributes is impossible, because it necessitates His being necessary of existence,' I say: That is in an attribute whose being described by it is posterior to existence. But regarding fixity, no, because it is prior to existence, so it is permissible that what is necessary of fixity be possible of existence. His saying: 'A negative attribute' — apparently the addition is explicative, i.e., an attribute whose essence is negation, because it is the removal of existence. And on the interpretation of it as negation, it is appropriate to say 'non-fixed' rather than 'non-positive', whether it is intended as what does not have negation included in its concept or as what is existent, for the inference from the non-fixity of the attribute to the non-fixity of the described is stronger and more evident than the inference from its non-fixity in one of the two meanings to his non-fixity. His saying: 'Hovered over its meaning' — in al-Qamus: 'hāma fī al-amr' means he persisted. So the word 'over' is in the sense of 'in' or by implication of superiority. His saying: 'And it is extremely weak' with kasra on 'inna' as a conjunct to his saying 'al-Amidi said', so it is from the words of the author. His saying: 'We do not accept that the described [by negation] is negated' can be refuted by saying that if it were fixed, the fixity of that attribute would be necessary. His saying: 'The third: the essences established [in non-existence] etc.' He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: 'This proof, despite its dependence on every possible of fixity being originated in the sense of preceded by negation, does not negate that essences are fixed without existence. Rather, its conclusion is that their fixity in non-existence is preceded by their negation. And you are aware that the proof is polemical, so it suffices.' (And it is extremely precise and good, but it is extremely weak) and ugly, because we do not accept that the described by a negative attribute is negated, due to the possibility of the existent being described by a negation (i.e., a negative attribute that has no fixity in itself, like the description of Zayd by blindness). As for his saying 'just as the described by a positive attribute is positive', it is an analogical argument without a commonality between the compared and the standard, and with the evident difference between them, because the fixity of a thing for another is a branch of the fixity of that other in itself, so it is not permissible for the non-existent to be described by a positive attribute; rather, the described by it must be fixed in itself. And the non-existence of a thing from another is not a branch of the non-existence of that other in itself, so it is permissible for the existent to be described by a negative attribute, so it is not necessary that the described by it be non-existent in itself. The Fifth Aspect: If non-existents fixed in non-existence were distinct by their essences, then every two different things would be, and their establishment in the described externally, so it would not be a negative attribute, just as the attributes of genera fixed for the possible non-existent are fixed according to them, unlike the description of existents by negative attributes, for it does not require their existence. Unless it is said: The necessary is their fixity in the described, and the negated is what has no fixity in itself. As for the answer that what is meant is that the described by a negative attribute of itself is negated, for non-existence is the negation of that thing itself, unlike other negative attributes, for they are not the negation of itself but the negation of an attribute of it, like blindness, for it is the negation of sight, not the negation of the essence of the blind, it is nothing, because those who affirm the fixity of the non-existent do not acknowledge that non-existence is the negation of the thing in itself, but rather the negation of the attribute of existence from it, while the thing is fixed in itself. His saying: 'And the non-existence [of a thing from another] etc.' means that being described by a negative attribute, i.e., what has negation included in its concept, is not a real description, for it is in reality a statement of the non-existence of the subject of the negation from a thing, and the non-existence of a thing from another does not necessitate its non-existence in itself. So what was said: 'The approach is incomplete because the discussion is about being described by a negative attribute, not about the negation of description, so it must be said: And the description of a thing by a negative attribute is not a branch of its non-existence in itself' — it is nothing. His saying: 'And it is extremely weak, since we do not accept etc.' It is answered that the meaning of al-Amidi's statement is that the described by a negative attribute of itself is negated, and by a positive attribute of itself is positive. And the described by blindness, i.e., the negation of sight, is not Zayd's essence, for example, but rather his sight itself, i.e., his sight described as being non-existent, so it is also described by the negation of itself and is negated. So his statement is extremely precise. And one may say: We do not accept that the described by non-existence is described by the negation of itself; rather, it is described by the negation of its existence added to it, and that is apparent. And we do not accept that the described by the negation of its existence is negated in the sense of not being fixed, for that is the point of dispute. Yes, it is negated in the sense of not existing, but that does not help, because the discussion is about the negation of fixity, and the claim is its generality from existence. Moreover, his saying 'just as the described by a positive attribute is positive' suggests absoluteness in the compared as in the standard, so let it be considered. His saying: 'The fifth: If they were distinct etc.' It can be said, by analogy with what he will mention about unity, that distinctness requires the condition of non-existence. Consider.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a continuous commentary presenting and discussing multiple arguments (wujuh) concerning whether the non-existent (al-ma'dum) has any reality or fixity. The discussion is part of the larger topic of al-Maqsad al-Sadis (the Sixth Aim) in the First Observatory. The commentary addresses the third, fourth, and fifth arguments, with objections and replies. The commentator first outlines a proof (likely the third argument) that if non-existents had fixity, they would be either necessary or possible; necessity is impossible due to the principle of tawhid, and possibility implies origination (huduth). The response clarifies that the necessary is defined by existence, not fixity. Then the fourth argument is introduced: 'adam (non-existence) is a negative attribute (sifat nafy). The described by negation is itself negated, just as the described by affirmation is affirmed. Al-Amidi notes this approach is unique. The commentary then addresses objections: if non-existents were fixed, they would need an efficient cause; the free agent causes origination. It refutes the objection that this conflicts with divine attributes. It also explains that the proof is polemical (ilzami) against the Mu'tazila who assert eternal fixity. The commentator discusses the meaning of 'sifat nafy', suggesting it is better phrased as 'non-fixed' rather than 'non-positive'. A later section returns to the third argument, citing Sharh al-Maqasid: the proof that every possible of fixity is originated (preceded by negation) does not deny that essences are fixed without existence, but only that their fixity in non-existence is preceded. Then the commentary critiques the fourth argument, noting that an existent can be described by a negative attribute (e.g., blindness) without being negated in itself. The analogy with positive attributes fails due to the asymmetry: positive attributes require a fixed subject, while negative attributes do not. The fifth argument then considers whether non-existents would be distinct by their essences; if they were, their distinction would be a positive attribute, contradicting the claim that 'adam is negative. The objection that the description by negation of existence does not entail non-fixity is rejected because the dispute is about fixity, not existence. The commentary ends with a reference to a later discussion on unity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that non-existence is a positive attribute that can belong to a fixed subject.
Correction: The argument shows that 'adam is a negative attribute, and a negative attribute cannot inhere in a fixed subject; rather, it negates the subject itself.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what does it mean to say 'adam is a negative attribute (sifat nafy)?
A) It is a real attribute that exists in the non-existent.
B) It is the removal of existence and has no positive content.
C) It is analogous to a positive attribute like knowledge.
D) It proves that non-existents are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument uses the nature of 'adam as a negative attribute to conclude that the non-existent described by it is itself negated (non-fixed), challenging the Mu'tazilite view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that non-existents have no fixity in themselves. Do you live as though your own existence is self-sufficient, or do you genuinely acknowledge that every moment of your being is given by Allah? What would change if you truly internalized this dependency?
?
If non-existence is merely a negative attribute, then everything real comes from the agent. When you plan for the future, do you act as if those future possibilities are fixed realities, or do you hold them with the humility that only Allah brings them into being? How can you train yourself to rely on Him alone?
?
The argument refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that non-existents have eternal fixity. Does your heart ever fall into a similar trap, treating your own thoughts or desires as eternally fixed realities rather than contingent creations? How can you remind yourself daily that nothing exists outside of Allah's will?

Logic and Technical Terms

7 min
Objections to the Fourth Argument and the Fifth Argument on Distinctness
Critiques the fourth argument and presents the fifth argument on distinctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections to the fourth argument (the asymmetry of positive and negative attributes) and the fifth argument that distinctness among non-existents would imply a positive attribute, contradicting the negative nature of 'adam.

Core Idea
Negative attributes do not require a fixed subject, and distinctness among non-existents would imply positive fixity.

The previous argument that 'adam is a negative attribute faced strong objections. Critics pointed out that an existent can be described by a negative attribute (like blindness) without being negated—this breaks the analogy. Moreover, if non-existents were distinct from each other, that distinctness would be a positive attribute, contradicting the claim that they have no fixity. Let us explore these objections and the fifth argument that addresses them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections to the fourth argument
Read the Arabic passage that critiques the fourth argument. Notice the example of an existent (Zayd) described by blindness (a negative attribute) without being negated.
2
Understand the asymmetry
The objection highlights that positive attributes require a fixed subject, but negative attributes do not: a thing can lack an attribute without losing its own existence. Thus, the analogy fails.
The commentator further mentions the reply that the described by a negative attribute of itself (like 'adam) is negated, but other negative attributes (like blindness) only negate a part, not the whole.
3
Examine the fifth argument
Read the fifth argument: if non-existents were distinct by their essences, that distinctness would be a positive attribute (since it is real). But 'adam is supposed to be purely negative, so this is contradictory.
The argument uses the principle that distinction (tabayun) is a real relation that requires fixity.
4
Reflect on the overall debate
Understand that these arguments and counter-arguments form a sophisticated dialectic. The fifth argument attempts to show that even if non-existents were fixed, their distinctness would force positive attributes, undermining the claim that they are merely negative.

( وهو في غاية الإحكام والحسن وإنه في غاية الضعف ) والقبح ( إذ لا نسلم أن المتصف بصفة النفي نفي لجواز اتصاف الموجود بالسلب ( أي بالصفة السلبية التي لا ثبوت لها في نفسها كاتصاف زيد بالعمى ( وأما قوله كما أن الموصوف بصفة الإثبات إثبات فقياس) تمثيلي (من غير جامع) بين المقيس والمقيس عليه (مع ظهور الفرق ( بينهما لأن ثبوت الشيء لغيره فرع على ثبوت ذلك الغير في نفسه، فلا يجوز أن يتصف المعدوم بصفة ،ثبوتية، بل لا بد أن يكون الموصوف بها ثابتاً في نفسه، وليس انتفاء الشيء من غيره فرعاً عن انتفاء ذلك الغير في نفسه، فجاز أن يتصف الموجود بصفة سلبية، فلا يجب أن يكون الموصوف بها منتفياً في نفسه. 
الوجه الخامس) المعدومات الثابتة في العدم لو) تباينت لذواتها كان كل شيئين مختلفين
،
وتقررها في الموصوف في الخارج، فلا يكون صفة نفي كما أن صفات الأجناس الثابتة للمعدوم الممكنة ثابتة عندهم بخلاف اتصاف الموجودات بصفات السلب فإنه لا يستدعي وجودها ، اللهم إلا أن يقال : اللازم ثبوتها في الموصوف والمنفي ما لا ثبوت له في نفسه، وأما الجواب بأن المراد أن الموصوف بصفة نفي نفسه منفي، فإن العدم نفي نفس ذلك الشيء بخلاف الصفة السلبية الأخرى، فإنها ليست نفي نفسه بل نفي صفة من صفاته كالعمى، فإنه نفي البصر لا نفي ذات الأعمى، فليس بشيء لأن القائلين بثبوت المعدوم لا يعترفون بأن العدم نفي الشيء في نفسه، بل نفي صفة الوجود عنه والشيء ثابت في نفسه.
قوله : ( وليس انتفاء إلخ) يعني أن الاتصاف بالصفة السلبية أي ما يكون السلب داخلاً في مفهومه ليس باتصاف حقيقي، فإنه في الحقيقة عبارة عن انتفاء مدخول السلب عن شيء، وانتفاء الشيء عن غيره لا يقتضي انتفاءه في نفسه فما قيل: إن التقريب غير تام لأن الكلام في الاتصاف بالصفة السلبية، لا في سلب الاتصاف، فالواجب أن يقال وليس اتصاف شيء بالصفة السلبية فرع انتفائه في نفسه ليس بشيء.
قوله : ( وإنه في غاية الضعف إذ لا نسلم إلخ) أجيب عنه بأن معنى كلام الآمدي أن الموصوف بصفة نفي نفسه منفي، وبصفة إثبات نفسه مثبت والموصوف بالعمى أعني نفي البصر، ليس ذات زيد مثلاً بل نفس بصره أي بصره متصف بأنه عديم، فهو أيضاً موصوف بنفي نفسه ومنفي فكلامه في غاية الإحكام ولقائل أن يقول : لا نسلم أن المتصف بالعدم متصف بصفة نفي نفسه، بل هو متصف بصفة نفي وجوده الزائد عليه وهو ظاهر ، و ولا نسلم أن المتصف بصفة نفي وجوده منفي بمعنى أنه غير ثابت فإنه المتنازع فيه نعم هو منفي بمعنى أنه غير موجود لكنه لا يفيد لأن الكلام في نفي الثبوت، والمدعى عمومه من الوجود على أن قوله : كما أن الموصوف بصفة الإثبات إثبات يشعر بالإطلاق في المقيس كما في المقيس عليه فليتأمل. قوله : ( الخامس لو تباينت إلخ يمكن أن يقال : قياساً على ما سيذكره في الوحدة اقتضاء التباين بشرط العدم تأمل.
The First Observatory - The Sixth Aim: Is the non-existent a thing or not? That which is established in the state of non-existence is either necessary of establishment, so it would be necessary while it was assumed possible, and it would also necessitate multiplicity of the Necessary (first), or it is possible of establishment, and every possible is originated, so those essences would be originated preceded by negation and non-fixity, and that is the required. So it was said: The necessary is that whose existence is necessary, not that whose establishment is necessary, which is more general than existence. The Fourth Aspect: That non-existence is a negative attribute (i.e., an attribute that is negated, not positive, because it is the removal of existence), and the described by a negative attribute is negated (i.e., negated, not fixed), just as the described by a positive attribute (i.e., a positive attribute) is positive (i.e., fixed, not negated). So the non-existent described by non-existence is negated. Al-Amidi said: 'This approach, though many theologians such as Muhammad al-Shahrastani and others have hovered around its meaning, yet as established and refined, we have not found it for anyone else.' The learned: His saying: 'But possible of establishment, so it is in need of an efficient cause for its establishment, and since it is established that the agent is free [to choose], it becomes originated, because everything emanating from a free agent is originated. So the objection by His attributes is not valid.' His saying: 'And that is the required' because it is established that non-existents have no fixity in themselves; it is only from the agent. So the objection of the author of al-Maqasid that the required is the non-establishment of them and the necessary implication of the proof is the non-eternity of their establishment is not valid. And there is no need for what was said: 'The argument is polemical, and the Mu'tazilites assert the eternity of their establishment.' His saying: 'The necessary is that whose existence is necessary.' For if it is said: 'Just as multiplicity of what is necessary of existence is impossible, so multiplicity of what is necessary as an attribute of His attributes is impossible, because it necessitates His being necessary of existence,' I say: That is in an attribute whose being described by it is posterior to existence. But regarding fixity, no, because it is prior to existence, so it is permissible that what is necessary of fixity be possible of existence. His saying: 'A negative attribute' — apparently the addition is explicative, i.e., an attribute whose essence is negation, because it is the removal of existence. And on the interpretation of it as negation, it is appropriate to say 'non-fixed' rather than 'non-positive', whether it is intended as what does not have negation included in its concept or as what is existent, for the inference from the non-fixity of the attribute to the non-fixity of the described is stronger and more evident than the inference from its non-fixity in one of the two meanings to his non-fixity. His saying: 'Hovered over its meaning' — in al-Qamus: 'hāma fī al-amr' means he persisted. So the word 'over' is in the sense of 'in' or by implication of superiority. His saying: 'And it is extremely weak' with kasra on 'inna' as a conjunct to his saying 'al-Amidi said', so it is from the words of the author. His saying: 'We do not accept that the described [by negation] is negated' can be refuted by saying that if it were fixed, the fixity of that attribute would be necessary. His saying: 'The third: the essences established [in non-existence] etc.' He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: 'This proof, despite its dependence on every possible of fixity being originated in the sense of preceded by negation, does not negate that essences are fixed without existence. Rather, its conclusion is that their fixity in non-existence is preceded by their negation. And you are aware that the proof is polemical, so it suffices.' (And it is extremely precise and good, but it is extremely weak) and ugly, because we do not accept that the described by a negative attribute is negated, due to the possibility of the existent being described by a negation (i.e., a negative attribute that has no fixity in itself, like the description of Zayd by blindness). As for his saying 'just as the described by a positive attribute is positive', it is an analogical argument without a commonality between the compared and the standard, and with the evident difference between them, because the fixity of a thing for another is a branch of the fixity of that other in itself, so it is not permissible for the non-existent to be described by a positive attribute; rather, the described by it must be fixed in itself. And the non-existence of a thing from another is not a branch of the non-existence of that other in itself, so it is permissible for the existent to be described by a negative attribute, so it is not necessary that the described by it be non-existent in itself. The Fifth Aspect: If non-existents fixed in non-existence were distinct by their essences, then every two different things would be, and their establishment in the described externally, so it would not be a negative attribute, just as the attributes of genera fixed for the possible non-existent are fixed according to them, unlike the description of existents by negative attributes, for it does not require their existence. Unless it is said: The necessary is their fixity in the described, and the negated is what has no fixity in itself. As for the answer that what is meant is that the described by a negative attribute of itself is negated, for non-existence is the negation of that thing itself, unlike other negative attributes, for they are not the negation of itself but the negation of an attribute of it, like blindness, for it is the negation of sight, not the negation of the essence of the blind, it is nothing, because those who affirm the fixity of the non-existent do not acknowledge that non-existence is the negation of the thing in itself, but rather the negation of the attribute of existence from it, while the thing is fixed in itself. His saying: 'And the non-existence [of a thing from another] etc.' means that being described by a negative attribute, i.e., what has negation included in its concept, is not a real description, for it is in reality a statement of the non-existence of the subject of the negation from a thing, and the non-existence of a thing from another does not necessitate its non-existence in itself. So what was said: 'The approach is incomplete because the discussion is about being described by a negative attribute, not about the negation of description, so it must be said: And the description of a thing by a negative attribute is not a branch of its non-existence in itself' — it is nothing. His saying: 'And it is extremely weak, since we do not accept etc.' It is answered that the meaning of al-Amidi's statement is that the described by a negative attribute of itself is negated, and by a positive attribute of itself is positive. And the described by blindness, i.e., the negation of sight, is not Zayd's essence, for example, but rather his sight itself, i.e., his sight described as being non-existent, so it is also described by the negation of itself and is negated. So his statement is extremely precise. And one may say: We do not accept that the described by non-existence is described by the negation of itself; rather, it is described by the negation of its existence added to it, and that is apparent. And we do not accept that the described by the negation of its existence is negated in the sense of not being fixed, for that is the point of dispute. Yes, it is negated in the sense of not existing, but that does not help, because the discussion is about the negation of fixity, and the claim is its generality from existence. Moreover, his saying 'just as the described by a positive attribute is positive' suggests absoluteness in the compared as in the standard, so let it be considered. His saying: 'The fifth: If they were distinct etc.' It can be said, by analogy with what he will mention about unity, that distinctness requires the condition of non-existence. Consider.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a continuous commentary presenting and discussing multiple arguments (wujuh) concerning whether the non-existent (al-ma'dum) has any reality or fixity. The discussion is part of the larger topic of al-Maqsad al-Sadis (the Sixth Aim) in the First Observatory. The commentary addresses the third, fourth, and fifth arguments, with objections and replies. The commentator first outlines a proof (likely the third argument) that if non-existents had fixity, they would be either necessary or possible; necessity is impossible due to the principle of tawhid, and possibility implies origination (huduth). The response clarifies that the necessary is defined by existence, not fixity. Then the fourth argument is introduced: 'adam (non-existence) is a negative attribute (sifat nafy). The described by negation is itself negated, just as the described by affirmation is affirmed. Al-Amidi notes this approach is unique. The commentary then addresses objections: if non-existents were fixed, they would need an efficient cause; the free agent causes origination. It refutes the objection that this conflicts with divine attributes. It also explains that the proof is polemical (ilzami) against the Mu'tazila who assert eternal fixity. The commentator discusses the meaning of 'sifat nafy', suggesting it is better phrased as 'non-fixed' rather than 'non-positive'. A later section returns to the third argument, citing Sharh al-Maqasid: the proof that every possible of fixity is originated (preceded by negation) does not deny that essences are fixed without existence, but only that their fixity in non-existence is preceded. Then the commentary critiques the fourth argument, noting that an existent can be described by a negative attribute (e.g., blindness) without being negated in itself. The analogy with positive attributes fails due to the asymmetry: positive attributes require a fixed subject, while negative attributes do not. The fifth argument then considers whether non-existents would be distinct by their essences; if they were, their distinction would be a positive attribute, contradicting the claim that 'adam is negative. The objection that the description by negation of existence does not entail non-fixity is rejected because the dispute is about fixity, not existence. The commentary ends with a reference to a later discussion on unity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the fourth argument is unanswerable because non-existence is purely negative.
Correction: The objection shows that negative attributes can apply to existents without negating them, so the argument's analogy fails.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection to the fourth argument presented in this passage?
A) The non-existent cannot be described by any attribute.
B) An existent can be described by a negative attribute without being negated, so the analogy fails.
C) Positive attributes and negative attributes are identical.
D) The fourth argument proves too much.

Key Takeaway
The objections reveal that negative attributes do not require a non-existent subject, and the fifth argument shows that distinctness among non-existents would introduce positive attributes, undermining the claim that 'adam is purely negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever unconsciously assign Him positive, creaturely attributes? How does this lesson's distinction between negative and positive attributes challenge you to refine your understanding of tawḥīd?
?
The fifth argument shows that even a seemingly neutral concept like 'distinctness' can imply positiveness. In your own life, what 'neutral' assumptions might actually contradict the pure oneness of God? Be specific.
?
If non-existents cannot have positive attributes, what does that mean for your daily concerns about things that do not exist yet? How should this affect your trust in Allah's power over the unseen?

The Second Proof: The Possible Non-Existent is Established
Al-Jurjani proves that the possible non-existent is established through logical inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument (the second proof) by which al-Jurjani establishes that the possible non-existent is an established thing (thabit) through the relationship of generality and distinctness.

Core Idea
The concept of non-existent must be a thing, or else logic collapses.

This passage presents a sophisticated logical proof that the possible non-existent—something that could exist but does not—has a kind of reality as a concept. Al-Jurjani argues through the relationship of generality between 'non-existent' and 'negated' to show that the concept of non-existent is distinct and therefore established. Before reading the dense text, focus on the structure of conditional reasoning and the careful distinctions between terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'If... then...'.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Notice the 'if...then' structure and how it leads to a contradiction. Ask yourself what the assumption is and what follows from it.
The contradiction shows that the assumption cannot be false.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The passage includes a possible objection that the establishment of the possible non-existent is irrelevant to the generality claim. Read the response carefully to see how the definition of 'negated' resolves the objection.
The answer clarifies the terminology used in the proof.
4
Trace the logical steps
Use the guided breakdown to follow each step of the argument from the premise to the contradiction and then the objection and answer.
This will help you see the underlying logic clearly.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this argument is important for the kalam concept of non-existents. What does it establish about the possible non-existent?
Think about the distinction between conceptual and extramental existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني لو كان) المعدوم الممكن ثابتاً) كان المعدوم المطلق (أعم) مطلقاً ( من المنفي) لشموله الثابت والمنفي معاً ( فيكون ) مفهوم المعدوم مطلقاً متميزاً عنه) أي عن مفهوم المنفي ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن متميزاً عنه ( لكان) المفهوم ( العام عين) المفهوم الخاص) وهو محال (فيكون ) مفهوم المعدوم أمراً ( ثابتاً لأن كل متميز عن غيره ) ثابت عندكم وإنه ) يعني مفهوم المعدوم ( صادق على المنفي) أي على ما صدق عليه المنفي (و) كل ما يصدق عليه صفة ثبوتية فهو ثابت فالمنفي ثابت هذا ،خلف وما يقال : ( من ( أن المعدوم الممكن ثابت عندهم ( لا) كل معدوم فيصدق ) حينئذ ( بعض المعدوم ثابت فلا يلزم من صدقه أي صدق المعدوم . قوله : (لو كان المعدوم الممكن ثابتاً إلخ) قيل لا دخل للثبوت في الملازمة إذ على تقدير عدم الثبوت فالأعمية ثابتة إذ للمعدوم فردان الممكن والممتنع وللمنفي فرد واحد وهو الممتنع وجوابه أن المراد بيان العموم على وفق ما اصطلحوا عليه من أن المنفي ما لا ثبوت له محالاً كان أو ممكناً، كالخياليات فالتعرض لثبوت الممكن المعدوم في اللزوم مما لا بد منه، إذ لو لم يكن له ثبوت لصدق أن كل معدوم منفي بالمعنى المذكور فلا يثبت عموم المعدوم منه .
The second point (الوجه الثاني): If the possible non-existent (المعدوم الممكن) were established (ثابت), then the absolute non-existent (المعدوم المطلق) would be absolutely more general (أعم) than the negated (المنفي) because it encompasses both the established and the negated. Then the concept of absolute non-existent would be distinct from it, i.e., from the concept of the negated. Otherwise, i.e., if it were not distinct from it, the general concept would be identical to the particular concept, which is impossible. So the concept of non-existent would be an established thing, because everything distinct from another is established according to you, and it—i.e., the concept of non-existent—is true of the negated, i.e., of what the negated is true of. And everything of which a positive attribute is true is established, so the negated is established—this is a contradiction. And what is said: that the possible non-existent is established among them (but not every non-existent), so then some non-existent is established, and the truth of 'non-existent' does not entail the truth of 'every non-existent'. His saying: (If the possible non-existent were established, etc.), it is said that establishment has no role in the implication, because on the assumption of non-establishment, the greater generality is still established, for non-existent has two individuals: the possible and the impossible, while the negated has only one individual: the impossible. Its answer: The intention is to explain generality according to their convention that the 'negated' is that which has no establishment, whether impossible or possible, like imaginary things. So mentioning the establishment of the possible non-existent in the implication is necessary, because if it had no establishment, it would be true that every non-existent is negated in the mentioned sense, and thus the generality of non-existent would not be proven from it.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof (الوجه الثاني) that the possible non-existent is an established thing (ثابت). The argument proceeds by conditional proof: If the possible non-existent were established, then the absolute non-existent (المعدوم المطلق) would be more general than the negated (المنفي), because non-existent would then include both the established (the possible non-existent) and the negated (the impossible). Thus the concept of non-existent would be distinct from the concept of negated. If they were not distinct, the general concept would be identical to the particular, which is impossible. Therefore, the concept of non-existent is established (since everything distinct is established). Now, this concept applies to the negated (the impossible). Whatever has a positive attribute (i.e., being a concept that applies) is established, so the negated itself would be established, which is a contradiction (since the negated is supposed to lack establishment). Hence the assumption that the possible non-existent is not established leads to contradiction; therefore the possible non-existent must be established. The gloss addresses an objection: Establishment is irrelevant to the generality because even without establishment, one could argue that non-existent has two individuals (possible and impossible) and negated only one (impossible). The response clarifies that the terminology used here defines 'negated' as anything without any establishment (including both impossible and possible non-existents like imaginary beings). So it is necessary to posit the establishment of the possible non-existent to make the argument work; otherwise, all non-existent would be negated and the distinction would collapse.

Key Terms
معدوم
ثابت
منفي
أعم
خاص
ممكن
ممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that all non-existents (including impossibles) are established things.
Correction: The proof specifically concerns possible non-existents, not impossible ones. The argument shows that the concept of non-existent must be a thing, but that does not mean impossible non-existents have any extramental reality.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the second proof?
A) The possible non-existent is not established.
B) The absolute non-existent is identical to the negated.
C) The possible non-existent is established (ثابت).
D) The negated is more general than the non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The possible non-existent, though non-actual, must be considered an established concept in order for logical distinctions between non-existent and negated to hold.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about something you plan to do tomorrow (e.g., a specific good deed), do you treat it as 'nothing' because it doesn't exist yet? According to this lesson, that possible future action is 'established' as a concept. How would your intentionality (niyya) change if you truly believed that Allah establishes even your possible future choices as real in His knowledge?
?
This argument hinges on the impossibility of the general being identical to the particular. In your own life, do you sometimes confuse general categories (like 'all people') with particular instances (like 'me'), excusing yourself from accountability by hiding behind generalities? Where specifically have you done that, and what would it look like to face the particular truth about yourself?
?
The proof shows that even non-existents must have some 'established' reality for logic to function. How does this affect your view of things you consider 'meaningless' or 'nothing' in your spiritual life (e.g., a fleeting thought, a forgotten sin)? Will you now treat them as more real and consequential?

The Core Ilzam Argument and the Syllogistic Objection
The commentator refutes an objection about syllogistic form and presents the core ilzam argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refined ilzam argument that distinction (tamayyuz) implies fixity (thubut), and why the objection about the syllogism's major premise being particular misses the point.

Core Idea
Distinction implies fixity, so the non-existent must be a fixed thing.

The commentator begins by addressing a common objection: that the intended syllogism fails because its major premise is particular. He shows that this objection misses the real argument, which is an ilzam (forcing) based on the Mu'tazila's own admission that distinction entails thubut. This lesson unpacks that refined argument and explains why the formal syllogistic objection is irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the commentator dismisses the objection as missing the intended meaning.
The passage begins with an objection, then moves to the ilzam argument.
2
Identify the ilzam argument
Find the statement that distinction (tamayyuz) requires thubut (fixity). Understand that this is the core of the forced contradiction.
3
Understand why the syllogistic objection fails
The commentator explains that the objection about the major premise being particular is based on a misunderstanding. The ilzam argument does not rely on that syllogism; it relies on the Mu'tazila's own recognition of distinction implying fixity.
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
