على المنفي ثبوته إذ يصير الاستدلال هكذا) المنفي معدوم وبعض المعدوم ثابت وأنه لا ينتج لكون الكبرى في الشكل الأول جزئية، فإنه بمعزل مما قدمناه من التحرير، وإنما خذلهم ذلك القول أنهم لم يحوموا على المراد، ولم يتفطنوا لأن قصدهم) أي قصد المستدلين بالوجه الثاني (الإلزام ) أي إلزام المعتزلة بما هم معترفون به من أن التميز يقتضي الثبوت وتوضيحه أن تحرير المصنف متعلق بمفهوم المعدوم، وأنه على تقدير كونه أعم من مفهوم المنفي يلزم أن يكون متميزاً عنه، فيكون أمراً ثابتاً، فيلزم أن يكون ما صدق عليه المنفي ثابتاً لاتصافه بأمر ثبوتي هو مفهوم المعدوم، وحينئذ لا يتجه عليه أصلاً ما قالوه : من أن الكبرى في الشكل الأول جزئية، وهناك تقرير آخر متعلق بما صدق عليه مفهوم المعدوم، وهو أن يقال : على تقدير كونه أعم من المنفي لا يكون ما صدق عليه المعدوم نفياً محضاً، وإلا لم يكن بينهما فرق وإذا لم يكن نفياً محضاً، كان ثابتاً فيصدق المنفي معدوم والمعدوم ثابت فيرد عليه أنه ليس جميع ما صدق عليه مفهوم المعدوم نفياً محضاً، بل بعضه نفي محض هو المعدوم الممتنع وبعضه ثابت هو المعدوم الممكن، وحينئذ تصير الكبرى في ذلك القياس جزئية، واعلم أن الأظهر على تحرير المصنف أن يقال : على تقدير كونه أعم من المنفي كان مفهوم المنفي متميزاً عنه فيكون ثابتاً وقد اتصف به ما صدق عليه من أفراده، فيكون أيضاً ثابتاً، وأما ما يقال من أن المعدوم ليس عندهم أعم من المنفي فمردود بما نقل عنهم من أنهم يطلقون المعدوم على المنفي أيضاً، وحينئذ إما أن يكون مساوياً له أو أخص منه مطلقاً ، أو من وجه أو أعم وعلى التقادير، فالمطلوب حاصل كما لا يخفى (للمثبت) أي للذي يثبت كون المعدوم ثابتاً. قوله : (ليس عندهم أعم بل هو مباين له لاختصاصه بالممكن، فلا يصح الصغرى أعني كل منفي معدوم . قوله : ( يطلقون المعدوم) بالمعنى المقابل للموجود على ما صدق عليه المنفي أيضاً، أي كما يطلقون لفظ المنفي عليه، فلا يكونان متباينين فاندفع ما قيل : إنه يجوز أن يكون الإطلاق بالاشتراك اللفظي . قوله : ( وحينئذ إما أن يكون إلخ لانتفاء التباين وعدم الاتحاد في المفهوم لفرض صدق المعدوم على الثابت وهذا التردد بالنظر إلى مجرد صدق المعدوم على المنفي من غير ملاحظة حال المنفي، وأما إذا لوحظ حاله فأعمية المعدوم متعين كما في المتن. قوله فالمطلوب (حاصل) أي المطلوب الأصلي وهو عدم ثبوت المعدوم، إذ يرتب على التقديرين الأولين القياس ،هكذا كل معدوم منفي ولا شيء من المنفي بثابت فلا شيء من المعدوم بثابت، وعلى التقديرين الآخرين كل منفي معدوم، أو بعض المنفي معدوم، وكل معدوم منفي ولا شيء من المنفي بثابت فلا شيء من المعدوم بثابت. قوله : ( من أنهم يطلقون المعدوم على المنفي أيضاً ) أي كما يطلقون المنفي على المنفي، أي يطلقون لفظ المنفي على ذاته فاندفع توهم ركاكة الترديد بقوله : وحينئذ إما أن يكون مساوياً إلخ بناء على توهم أن معنى أيضاً كما يطلقون المعدوم على الثابت، إذ حينئذ لا احتمال للمساواة على أن معنى قوله : وحينئذ حين الإطلاق من غير اعتبار مدلول لفظ أيضاً، أو نقول : إنه توسيع للدائرة لا ترديد، ومثله مقبول شائع في كلامهم وقد يورد على جواب الشارح عن القيل أن إطلاق المعدوم على المنفي يحتمل أن يكون باشتراك اللفظ، بأن يوضع بوضع آخر بإزاء المنفي لا باعتبار اتصاف المنفي بمفهوم المعدوم الثابت على الفرض حتى يلزم ثبوت المنفي لا يقال : الإطلاق على المنفي والثابت معاً، فلا اشتراك لفظياً بناء على عدم عموم المشترك لانا نقول : يجوز أن يكون الإطلاق باعتبار المسمى بهذا اللفظ على أن هذا التوجيه على تقدير تمامه يستدعي صرف كلمة أيضاً إلى ذلك المعنى الموهم لركاكة الترديد، والأظهر أن يجاب بأن الاشتراك خلاف الأصل هذا والأقرب أن يقال : في دفع قيل ذلك القائل أنه لو سلم عدم إطلاقهم المعدوم على المنفي بالاشتراك المعنوي لا شك في أن معنى العدم عندهم سلب الوجود، ومعنى النفي سلب الثبوت ولا شك في عموم الأول لأن نقيض الأخص أعم وبه يتم المقصود كما لا يخفى . قوله : ((فالمطلوب (حاصل) أراد به أصل المطلوب، وهو ثبوت ذات المعدوم لثبوت
upon the negated its being established, for the reasoning becomes thus: The negated is non-existent, and some non-existent is established, and it does not yield a conclusion because the major premise in the first figure is particular. But this is far from what we have previously presented of the refinement. Rather, that statement failed them because they did not circle around the intended meaning, and they did not realize that their intention—i.e., the intention of those who argue by the second way—is the ilzam (forcing), i.e., forcing the Mu'tazila by what they themselves acknowledge, that distinction (tamayyuz) requires thubut (fixity/being). Its clarification is that the author's refinement concerns the concept of the non-existent (ma'dum), and that on the assumption that it is more general than the concept of the negated (manfi), it follows that it is distinguished from it, so it becomes a fixed thing, so it follows that what is true of the negated is fixed because it is characterized by a fixed thing, namely the concept of the non-existent. And then what they said—that the major premise in the first figure is particular—does not apply to it at all. There is another presentation concerning what is true of the concept of the non-existent, which is to say: On the assumption that it is more general than the negated, what is true of the non-existent is not pure negation; otherwise there would be no difference between them. And if it is not pure negation, then it is fixed, so it is true that the negated is non-existent and the non-existent is fixed. But it is objected that not everything true of the concept of the non-existent is pure negation; rather, some of it is pure negation—the impossible non-existent—and some of it is fixed—the possible non-existent. And then the major premise in that syllogism becomes particular. Know that the more apparent on the author's refinement is to say: On the assumption that it is more general than the negated, the concept of the negated is distinguished from it, so it is fixed, and what is true of its individuals is characterized by it, so it is also fixed. As for the claim that the non-existent is not more general than the negated in their view, it is refuted by what is transmitted from them, that they apply the term 'non-existent' to the negated as well. Then it is either equal to it, or more specific absolutely, or more specific in some respect, or more general. And on all these estimations, the desired conclusion is obtained, as is not hidden. (For the affirmer) i.e., for the one who affirms that the non-existent is fixed. His statement: (It is not more general in their view, but rather it is disparate from it because it is specific to the possible, so the minor premise—namely, every negated is non-existent—is not valid). His statement: (They apply the term 'non-existent') in the sense opposite to 'existent' to what is true of the negated as well, i.e., just as they apply the term 'negated' to it, so they are not disparate. Thus what was said—that the application could be by verbal equivocation—is repelled. His statement: (Then it is either... etc.) because of the negation of disparity and the lack of unity in concept due to the assumption that the non-existent is true of the fixed. This alternation is with respect to the mere truth of 'non-existent' for the negated without considering the state of the negated. But if its state is considered, the non-existent being more general is determined, as in the text. His statement: (The desired conclusion is obtained) i.e., the original desired conclusion, which is the non-fixity of the non-existent, for upon the first two estimations the syllogism proceeds thus: Every non-existent is negated, and nothing of the negated is fixed, so nothing of the non-existent is fixed. And upon the other two estimations: Every negated is non-existent, or some negated is non-existent, and every non-existent is negated, and nothing of the negated is fixed, so nothing of the non-existent is fixed. His statement: (That they apply the term 'non-existent' to the negated as well) i.e., just as they apply the term 'negated' to the negated, i.e., they apply the term 'negated' to its essence. Thus the suspicion of weakness in the alternation in his statement 'Then it is either equal... etc.' is repelled, based on the suspicion that the meaning of 'also' is 'as they apply the term non-existent to the fixed', for then there is no possibility of equality. For the meaning of his statement 'Then' is at the time of application without regard to the signification of the word 'also'. Or we say: it is an expansion of the circle, not an alternation, and the like is accepted and common in their speech. And it may be objected to the commentator's answer to the critic that the application of 'non-existent' to the negated could be by verbal equivocation, in that it is placed by another positing in place of the negated, not by virtue of the negated being characterized by the concept of the non-existent that is fixed on the assumption, so that the fixity of the negated would follow. It cannot be said: The application is to both the negated and the fixed together, so there is no verbal equivocation, based on the non-generality of the homonym. For we say: It is permissible that the application is by virtue of the named by this word, such that this justification, if complete, requires diverting the word 'also' to that meaning that suggests weakness in the alternation. The more apparent is to answer that equivocation is contrary to the default. And the closest is to say in repelling the critic's statement: Even if their application of 'non-existent' to the negated by semantic sharing is not conceded, there is no doubt that the meaning of non-existence ('adam) in their view is the negation of existence, and the meaning of negation (nafy) is the negation of fixity (thubut), and there is no doubt about the generality of the former because the contradictory of the more specific is more general. And by this the purpose is achieved, as is not hidden. His statement: (The desired conclusion is obtained) he intended by it the original desired conclusion, which is the fixity of the essence of the non-existent due to the fixity
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a complex dialectical refinement of the argument about whether the non-existent (ma'dum) is something (thabit). The commentator begins by addressing an objection: some claimed that the intended syllogism is invalid because its major premise in the first figure is particular (not universal). The commentator dismisses this objection as missing the point, because the real argument is an ilzam (forcing) of the Mu'tazila, based on their own recognition that distinction (tamayyuz) entails thubut (fixity/being). The proper formulation, according to the author (al-Iji?), is that the concept of ma'dum, if assumed more general than the concept of manfi (the negated), must be distinct from manfi, and thus is a fixed thing. Consequently, the individuals that are manfi are described by this fixed concept, so they too are fixed. The commentator then presents another formulation that avoids making the major premise particular: the more apparent approach is that if ma'dum is more general than manfi, then manfi (the negated) is distinct from it, so manfi is fixed. The claim that ma'dum is not more general than manfi in the Mu'tazila's view is refuted by citing that they apply the term 'ma'dum' to manfi as well. Given that, he analyzes the logical relations: ma'dum could be equal, more specific, or more general than manfi; in all cases the conclusion that something is fixed follows, but the original desired conclusion (that ma'dum is not fixed) is actually obtained by the syllogism only on the first two estimations. The commentator then clarifies that the alternation (taqdīrāt) is merely a rhetorical expansion, not a real logical equivocation, and that the use of 'also' (ayḍan) does not imply weakness. Finally, he addresses a possible objection about verbal equivocation (ishtirāk lafẓī): the application of 'ma'dum' to manfi could be by homonymy, not by conceptual sharing. The commentator refutes this by pointing out that verbal equivocation is contrary to the default principle, and moreover, even without semantic sharing, the meanings of 'adam (negation of existence) and nafy (negation of thubut) are such that 'adam is more general (since its contradictory—existence—is more specific than thubut). This ensures the argument's validity. The passage thus systematically defends the argument that the non-existent is not a thing, while dealing with logical and linguistic objections.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection that the syllogism fails because the major premise is particular is a valid refutation.
Correction: The commentator shows that the objection misses the real ilzam argument, which does not depend on that syllogism.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the objection about the major premise being particular is irrelevant?
A) Because the argument is not a syllogism at all.
B) Because the real argument is an ilzam based on distinction implying fixity, not that syllogism.
C) Because the major premise is actually universal.
D) Because the Mu'tazila accept the particular premise.

Key Takeaway
The ilzam argument forces the Mu'tazila to admit that distinction implies fixity, and the formal syllogistic objection about particularity does not refute it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you raise objections against Allah's commands or decree, are you, like the objectors in this passage, missing the real point? What technical 'syllogism' are you hiding behind to avoid the heart of your accountability? Will you drop the excuse today and submit?
?
The argument shows that even the non-existent (like a past sin you regret) is 'fixed' in the sense that it is distinguishable and known to Allah. If your sin is fixed in His knowledge, how does that affect your repentance? Are you trying to erase it from your mind, or are you willing to let His fixity of it drive you to sincere change?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
8 min
Alternative Formulation and Logical Possibilities
The commentator offers an alternative formulation and analyzes the logical relations between the terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative formulation of the argument that avoids the particularity issue and the logical possibilities between the concepts of ma'dum and manfi.

Core Idea
Even if ma'dum is more general, the major premise does not become particular; moreover, the logical relations between ma'dum and manfi always yield the desired result.

The commentator presents another formulation of the argument that does not rely on the objection's premise. This formulation focuses on what is true of the concept of the non-existent. He then addresses the claim that ma'dum is not more general than manfi, showing that even if we grant that, the various logical possibilities still lead to the conclusion. This lesson explores that alternative and the analysis of the relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative formulation
Notice how this formulation avoids the particularity objection by focusing on the concept of the non-existent itself, not on its individuals.
2
Understand the objection to this formulation
The objection is that not all non-existent is pure negation; some is possible and thus fixed, making the major premise particular. The commentator then gives the more apparent refinement.
3
Analyze the logical possibilities
The commentator examines whether ma'dum is equal, more specific, or more general than manfi. See how each case leads to the conclusion that the non-existent is not fixed (the original desired conclusion).
This step is key for the learning objective.

There is another presentation concerning what is true of the concept of the non-existent, which is to say: On the assumption that it is more general than the negated, what is true of the non-existent is not pure negation; otherwise there would be no difference between them. And if it is not pure negation, then it is fixed, so it is true that the negated is non-existent and the non-existent is fixed. But it is objected that not everything true of the concept of the non-existent is pure negation; rather, some of it is pure negation—the impossible non-existent—and some of it is fixed—the possible non-existent. And then the major premise in that syllogism becomes particular. Know that the more apparent on the author's refinement is to say: On the assumption that it is more general than the negated, the concept of the negated is distinguished from it, so it is fixed, and what is true of its individuals is characterized by it, so it is also fixed. As for the claim that the non-existent is not more general than the negated in their view, it is refuted by what is transmitted from them, that they apply the term 'non-existent' to the negated as well. Then it is either equal to it, or more specific absolutely, or more specific in some respect, or more general. And on all these estimations, the desired conclusion is obtained, as is not hidden. (For the affirmer) i.e., for the one who affirms that the non-existent is fixed. His statement: (It is not more general in their view, but rather it is disparate from it because it is specific to the possible, so the minor premise—namely, every negated is non-existent—is not valid). His statement: (They apply the term 'non-existent') in the sense opposite to 'existent' to what is true of the negated as well, i.e., just as they apply the term 'negated' to it, so they are not disparate. Thus what was said—that the application could be by verbal equivocation—is repelled. His statement: (Then it is either... etc.) because of the negation of disparity and the lack of unity in concept due to the assumption that the non-existent is true of the fixed. This alternation is with respect to the mere truth of 'non-existent' for the negated without considering the state of the negated. But if its state is considered, the non-existent being more general is determined, as in the text. His statement: (The desired conclusion is obtained) i.e., the original desired conclusion, which is the non-fixity of the non-existent, for upon the first two estimations the syllogism proceeds thus: Every non-existent is negated, and nothing of the negated is fixed, so nothing of the non-existent is fixed. And upon the other two estimations: Every negated is non-existent, or some negated is non-existent, and every non-existent is negated, and nothing of the negated is fixed, so nothing of the non-existent is fixed. His statement: (That they apply the term 'non-existent' to the negated as well) i.e., just as they apply the term 'negated' to the negated, i.e., they apply the term 'negated' to its essence. Thus the suspicion of weakness in the alternation in his statement 'Then it is either equal... etc.' is repelled, based on the suspicion that the meaning of 'also' is 'as they apply the term non-existent to the fixed', for then there is no possibility of equality. For the meaning of his statement 'Then' is at the time of application without regard to the signification of the word 'also'. Or we say: it is an expansion of the circle, not an alternation, and the like is accepted and common in their speech. And it may be objected to the commentator's answer to the critic that the application of 'non-existent' to the negated could be by verbal equivocation, in that it is placed by another positing in place of the negated, not by virtue of the negated being characterized by the concept of the non-existent that is fixed on the assumption, so that the fixity of the negated would follow. It cannot be said: The application is to both the negated and the fixed together, so there is no verbal equivocation, based on the non-generality of the homonym. For we say: It is permissible that the application is by virtue of the named by this word, such that this justification, if complete, requires diverting the word 'also' to that meaning that suggests weakness in the alternation. The more apparent is to answer that equivocation is contrary to the default. And the closest is to say in repelling the critic's statement: Even if their application of 'non-existent' to the negated by semantic sharing is not conceded, there is no doubt that the meaning of non-existence ('adam) in their view is the negation of existence, and the meaning of negation (nafy) is the negation of fixity (thubut), and there is no doubt about the generality of the former because the contradictory of the more specific is more general. And by this the purpose is achieved, as is not hidden. His statement: (The desired conclusion is obtained) he intended by it the original desired conclusion, which is the fixity of the essence of the non-existent due to the fixity
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a complex dialectical refinement of the argument about whether the non-existent (ma'dum) is something (thabit). The commentator begins by addressing an objection: some claimed that the intended syllogism is invalid because its major premise in the first figure is particular (not universal). The commentator dismisses this objection as missing the point, because the real argument is an ilzam (forcing) of the Mu'tazila, based on their own recognition that distinction (tamayyuz) entails thubut (fixity/being). The proper formulation, according to the author (al-Iji?), is that the concept of ma'dum, if assumed more general than the concept of manfi (the negated), must be distinct from manfi, and thus is a fixed thing. Consequently, the individuals that are manfi are described by this fixed concept, so they too are fixed. The commentator then presents another formulation that avoids making the major premise particular: the more apparent approach is that if ma'dum is more general than manfi, then manfi (the negated) is distinct from it, so manfi is fixed. The claim that ma'dum is not more general than manfi in the Mu'tazila's view is refuted by citing that they apply the term 'ma'dum' to manfi as well. Given that, he analyzes the logical relations: ma'dum could be equal, more specific, or more general than manfi; in all cases the conclusion that something is fixed follows, but the original desired conclusion (that ma'dum is not fixed) is actually obtained by the syllogism only on the first two estimations. The commentator then clarifies that the alternation (taqdīrāt) is merely a rhetorical expansion, not a real logical equivocation, and that the use of 'also' (ayḍan) does not imply weakness. Finally, he addresses a possible objection about verbal equivocation (ishtirāk lafẓī): the application of 'ma'dum' to manfi could be by homonymy, not by conceptual sharing. The commentator refutes this by pointing out that verbal equivocation is contrary to the default principle, and moreover, even without semantic sharing, the meanings of 'adam (negation of existence) and nafy (negation of thubut) are such that 'adam is more general (since its contradictory—existence—is more specific than thubut). This ensures the argument's validity. The passage thus systematically defends the argument that the non-existent is not a thing, while dealing with logical and linguistic objections.

Key Takeaway
The commentator shows that whether ma'dum is more general, more specific, or equal to manfi, the argument for the fixity of the non-existent can be constructed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, when you think about something that does not exist (like a future goal or a past mistake), do you treat it as a 'pure negation' (nothing at all) or as something 'fixed' in your mind that influences your actions? What changes if you see it as fixed?
?
The commentator shows that even if one objection seems to weaken the argument, the conclusion still stands. Is there a challenge to your faith that you have allowed to linger as a 'paralyzing objection'? What would it mean to face it with the same patient analysis of all possibilities?
?
If the 'non-existent' can be classified into impossible and possible, how do you determine which of your desires are impossible and which are merely possible? Are you dismissing possibilities too quickly, or chasing impossibilities? How does this affect your relationship with your Lord's decree?

Refuting the Charge of Verbal Equivocation
The commentator refutes the verbal equivocation objection and provides a final argument based on the generality of 'adam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the objection that the term 'non-existent' is applied to the negated by verbal equivocation, and how he argues that 'adam is more general than nafy.

Core Idea
Verbal equivocation is contrary to the default, and even if not, 'adam is logically more general than nafy.

A possible objection is that the Mu'tazila might apply the word 'ma'dum' to the negated by homonymy, not by conceptual sharing. The commentator provides two responses: first, verbal equivocation is contrary to the default principle; second, even without semantic sharing, the meanings of 'adam (negation of existence) and nafy (negation of thubut) are such that 'adam is more general, which suffices for the argument. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the commentator's first answer
Notice the objection: the application of 'ma'dum' to manfi could be by verbal equivocation. The commentator says this is contrary to the default and that the word 'also' (aydan) is not problematic.
2
Understand the second, stronger answer
Even if we do not concede semantic sharing, the meanings of 'adam and nafy show that 'adam is more general because its contradictory (existence) is more specific than thubut.
3
See how this completes the argument
With this, the commentator argues that the purpose is achieved: the non-existent is fixed due to the fixity of its essence.
Connects to the key takeaway.

Commentary
This passage is a complex dialectical refinement of the argument about whether the non-existent (ma'dum) is something (thabit). The commentator begins by addressing an objection: some claimed that the intended syllogism is invalid because its major premise in the first figure is particular (not universal). The commentator dismisses this objection as missing the point, because the real argument is an ilzam (forcing) of the Mu'tazila, based on their own recognition that distinction (tamayyuz) entails thubut (fixity/being). The proper formulation, according to the author (al-Iji?), is that the concept of ma'dum, if assumed more general than the concept of manfi (the negated), must be distinct from manfi, and thus is a fixed thing. Consequently, the individuals that are manfi are described by this fixed concept, so they too are fixed. The commentator then presents another formulation that avoids making the major premise particular: the more apparent approach is that if ma'dum is more general than manfi, then manfi (the negated) is distinct from it, so manfi is fixed. The claim that ma'dum is not more general than manfi in the Mu'tazila's view is refuted by citing that they apply the term 'ma'dum' to manfi as well. Given that, he analyzes the logical relations: ma'dum could be equal, more specific, or more general than manfi; in all cases the conclusion that something is fixed follows, but the original desired conclusion (that ma'dum is not fixed) is actually obtained by the syllogism only on the first two estimations. The commentator then clarifies that the alternation (taqdīrāt) is merely a rhetorical expansion, not a real logical equivocation, and that the use of 'also' (ayḍan) does not imply weakness. Finally, he addresses a possible objection about verbal equivocation (ishtirāk lafẓī): the application of 'ma'dum' to manfi could be by homonymy, not by conceptual sharing. The commentator refutes this by pointing out that verbal equivocation is contrary to the default principle, and moreover, even without semantic sharing, the meanings of 'adam (negation of existence) and nafy (negation of thubut) are such that 'adam is more general (since its contradictory—existence—is more specific than thubut). This ensures the argument's validity. The passage thus systematically defends the argument that the non-existent is not a thing, while dealing with logical and linguistic objections.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'ma'dum' might be applied to manfi by verbal equivocation, so the argument fails.
Correction: Equivocation is contrary to default, and even if not, the meanings of 'adam and nafy show 'adam is more general, preserving the argument.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the relationship between the meanings of 'adam (non-existence) and nafy (negation)?
A) They are synonymous.
B) 'Adam is more general than nafy.
C) Nafy is more general than 'adam.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
The obstacle of verbal equivocation is overcome by appealing to default and the logical generality of 'adam over nafy, thereby securing the fixity of the non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a theological argument based on a suspicion of verbal ambiguity, without examining whether the deeper concepts are clear? This passage demonstrates that even if a term could be ambiguous, the underlying logical relationships can still be sound. Will you now commit to looking past surface-level word confusion in your own study of aqeedah?
?
The commentator appeals to 'al-asl' (the default) that equivocation is not the norm. What defaults do you hold about your understanding of Allah's attributes? Could you articulate and defend them with logical reasoning as this commentator does, or are you relying on assumptions that might be challenged?

Logic and Technical Terms

The Non-Existent: Objection and Rebuttal by Distinction of Impossibilities
Objection and rebuttal with examples of impossibilities, substances, and composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author rebuts the objection that the non-existent lacks determination by pointing to the agreed-upon distinction among impossibilities, including imaginary things, substances, and composition.

Core Idea
Impossibilities are distinct, so the non-existent can be determined.

In the debate on whether the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing, one objection is that it has no determination or intellectual reference. The author responds by turning the opponent's own concepts against them: even impossibilities, which both sides agree have no subsistence, are distinguishable from each other. This lesson presents the objection, the rebuttal, and the detailed examples from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objection: 'لا تعين له في نفسه ولا إشارة عقلاً إليه'. Note that the non-existent is claimed to have no determination or intellectual reference.
2
Identify the rebuttal method
The author answers with 'النقض بما وافقونا على أنه منفي كالممتنعات'. The rebuttal (al-naqḍ) uses things both sides agree are negated (manfī), i.e., impossibilities.
3
Examine the examples of impossibilities
The passage lists 'كشريك الباري', 'اجتماع الضدين', and 'الخياليات' like a sea of mercury, a mountain of ruby, and a two-headed man. These are distinct despite having no subsistence.
The commentary explains that 'imaginary things' are possible things composed by imagination.
4
Consider substances and accidents
The text notes that according to opponents, substances and accidents are fixed in non-existence without qualification. Existence itself is distinct and has no subsistence in non-existence.
5
Analyze composition
Composition (tarkīb) is distinct but not realized in non-existence because it involves parts joining, which is inconceivable in non-existence.

The First Observatory - The Sixth Aim: Is the non-existent a thing or not? It has no determination in itself, and no intellectual reference to it. The answer to this aspect is the rebuttal by what they agreed with us that it is negated, like the impossibilities. For some of them, like a partner of God, are distinct from others; like the joining of opposites, and imaginary things like a sea of mercury, a mountain of ruby, and a two-headed man. For some of them are distinct from others, and they have no subsistence by agreement, because they consist of substances qualified by composition, colors, and specific forms. According to them, what is fixed in non-existence are the substances and accidents, without the substances being qualified by accidents there. And existence itself is distinct from non-existence and other things, and it has no subsistence in non-existence by agreement and necessarily. And composition: its quiddity is distinct from others, and it is not realized in the state of non-existence by agreement, because it consists of the gathering of parts and the joining of some to others, and their contact in a specific manner, and that is not conceivable in the state of non-existence, but rather in the state of existence. His saying: (and imaginary things) meaning possible things that the imagination has composed from perceived matters. His saying: (by agreement) meaning between the proponents of the subsistence of the non-existent and its deniers. His saying: (the substances etc.) meaning the simple substances, for there is no composition in non-existence, and the accidents which qualify things in the external world. So the meaning of their saying 'the possible non-existents are fixed' and their saying 'what is fixed in non-existence from every type are infinite individuals, the simples, which are the type of simple substance and all other types of accidents.' And it follows from them the claim that accidents subsist by themselves in the state of subsistence, and perhaps they do not object to that, just as one does not object to a single thing being both substance and accident according to the two existences. For their dispute arose only from the denial of mental existence and the affirmation of its rulings for things in the external world. Hence, some of them said: 'There is a non-existent man riding a non-existent horse, on his head a colored cap, in his hand a non-existent sword, engaging in non-existent combat,' based on the permissibility of imagining that. The philosophers. His saying: (and existence itself) meaning without the quiddities being qualified by it. His saying: (in non-existence etc.) meaning in the state of non-existence of the quiddities, so it does not contradict the generalization that will come from his saying: 'neither in existence nor in non-existence nor in other than them.' For the intended meaning is that it is not a locus for the subsistence of the states of any of the mentioned matters. And existence in terms of being a state is included in that generalization. Here is the explanation of the rebuttal by it in terms of its essence, disregarding whether it is a state, existent, or non-existent, so reflect, for some observers have erred in it. His saying: (and that is not conceivable) based on the necessity of sophistry from the permissibility of the non-existent being qualified by movements. His saying: (that it is negated, the meaning of negation according to them is the negation of subsistence, so there is no harm in conjoining the imaginary things with the impossibilities. His saying: (and according to them that which is fixed etc.) Apparently this is the saying of all who affirm the subsistence of the non-existent. What we will mention at the end of the sixth aim, that all agreed that after knowledge that the world ... indicates that it is the saying of some, unless it is interpreted as we will mention there.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer within the debate on whether the non-existent (maʿdūm) is a thing. The objector claims that the non-existent has no determination (taʿayyun) in itself and no intellectual reference (ishāra ʿaqliyya) to it. The answer is a rebuttal (al-naqḍ) based on what both sides agree upon: that the non-existent is negated (manfī), like impossibilities (mumtaniʿāt). The author argues that even impossibilities — such as a partner of God (sharīk al-Bārī), the coexistence of opposites, or imaginary things (khiyāliyyāt) like a sea of mercury, a mountain of ruby, and a two-headed man — are distinct from each other. Despite having no subsistence (thubūt) by agreement, they are distinguishable. The commentary explains that 'imaginary things' are possible things composed by the imagination from sensory elements, and 'by agreement' means by both proponents and deniers of the subsistence of the non-existent. The main text then states that according to the opponents, what is fixed in non-existence are the substances (dhawāt al-jawāhir) and accidents (aʿrāḍ), without the substances being qualified by accidents there. Existence itself is distinct from non-existence and has no subsistence in non-existence by agreement and necessity. The composition (tarkīb) is also distinct but not realized in non-existence. The commentary expands on these points: 'substances' means simple substances (jawhar fard), and 'accidents' are those that qualify things in the external world. The opponents' claim that non-existents are fixed leads to the implication that accidents subsist by themselves in the state of subsistence, which they may accept. The dispute originated from denying mental existence. An example is given of a non-existent man riding a non-existent horse, etc., to illustrate that such things can be imagined. The passage then delves into further commentary on specific phrases, clarifying that existence itself is not a state (ḥāl) but is used in rebuttal, and that the generalization later will cover all cases. The commentary warns that some observers have erred in understanding this point. Overall, this lesson establishes the method of rebuttal by turning the opponent's own assumptions against them.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rebuttal is merely hypothetical and not based on actual agreement.
Correction: The rebuttal is grounded in what both sides agree upon: that impossibilities are negated (manfi) and yet distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the basis of the rebuttal (al-naqḍ) against the claim that the non-existent has no determination?
A) The impossibility of distinguishing impossibilities.
B) The agreement that impossibilities are distinct from each other.
C) The necessity of existence for distinction.
D) The rejection of imaginary things.

Key Takeaway
The determination of the non-existent is established by the very distinction among impossibilities that both sides accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you dismiss false beliefs like shirk, do you reduce them to vague nothings, or do you clearly distinguish their specific falsehood? How does this distinction sharpen your commitment to tawhid today?
?
The passage uses agreed-upon premises to refute an objection. Is there a belief you hold that you have never challenged? What would happen if you turned its own logic against it?
?
You constantly imagine things that do not exist—like a perfect future or a past mistake. How does the reality that these are distinct non-existents call you to anchor your mind in what truly exist: Allah and His signs?

Existence Cannot Be Fixed in Non-Existence
Arguing that existence and attributes are not fixed in non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence and attributes cannot be fixed in non-existence without leading to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Existence and non-existence cannot coexist; attributing existence to a non-existent is contradictory.

In this passage, the author continues the rebuttal against those who claim the non-existent is a thing. He argues that existence and attributes are not fixed in non-existence, because establishing existence in a state of non-existence would force existence and non-existence to coexist, which is impossible. The commentary then refines the argument, distinguishing existence from other attributes and addressing an objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Observe how the author builds his argument.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that existence and attributes are distinct and not fixed in non-existence. Find this claim in the text.
3
Understand the consequence
If existence were fixed in non-existence, it would necessitate the conjunction of existence and non-existence. Why is this impossible?
This is the core logical contradiction.
4
Note why existence is singled out
The author explains that existence is specified because its non-fixity in non-existence is agreed upon by necessity, unlike other attributes.
This addresses a potential objection.
5
Consider the response to al-Maqāṣid
The commentary replies to an objection: existence and attributes have no state of non-existence, so the opponent's principle does not apply.
This clarifies the scope of the argument.

The First Observatory - The Sixth Aim: Is the non-existent a thing or not? 207 Existence and attributes: they are distinct and are not fixed according to you in non-existence. It is as if he specified existence in mention despite its inclusion in attributes, because its being fixed in non-existence is negated by agreement and necessity. For if the existence of the non-existent were to be established in its state of non-existence, it would necessitate the conjunction of existence and non-existence. Then the rebuttal by attributes is only directed... and motions, and that leads to the school of sophistry. And some of them said concerning qualification and distinguished that sophistry only follows if we say that external effects and rulings are arranged in the state of non-existence. In it [the rebuttal], qualification by sensible accidents is among the external effects. His saying: (in non-existence) meaning in the state of non-existence of that which is qualified by them. His saying: (and it is as if he specified existence etc.) meaning that existence, even if it is included in attributes, the rebuttal by it is directed against the deniers of the state (ḥāl) in terms of it being a state, but in terms of its specificity, the rebuttal is directed by it against both groups, so it has an advantage over others. That is because it may be said: existence is qualified by distinction in the state of annihilation of the quiddities qualified by it, so its subsistence in the state of their annihilation is necessitated, and it entails the existence of the non-existent in its state of non-existence, whether one says that existence is a state or not, unlike other attributes. His saying: (existence of the non-existent) meaning the specific existence by which the non-existent is qualified in its state of non-existence, i.e., the non-existence of the non-existent. His saying: (it necessitates the conjunction of existence etc.) necessarily, because subsistence, existence, and other attributes have no state of non-existence at all. So from where is their subsistence in non-existence necessitated? For existence is only in the non-existent because it is an abstracted matter. His saying: (then the rebuttal etc.) is a response to what the author of al-Maqāṣid raised, that the opponent's principle is none other than that every known thing is fixed in the external world; if it is existent, then in existence; if it is non-existent, then in non-existence; or if it is neither existent nor non-existent, then in that state. And existence and other attributes have no state of non-existence at all, so from where is their subsistence in non-existence necessitated? His saying: (and they are not fixed according to you in non-existence) If you say: more appropriate to his saying later, 'and they are not fixed according to you neither in existence nor in non-existence nor in other than them,' he omitted his saying 'in non-existence.' So what is the reason for mentioning it? I say: since the rebuttal concerns the deniers of the state (ḥāl), and they say that they are non-existents, this restriction was more appropriate. As for what he will mention, it is an additional generalization intended to harmonize with the speech of al-Maqāṣid who raised his saying 'then the rebuttal etc.' as a response, as reflection upon it indicates. His saying: (and it is as if he specified existence etc.) It is said: the outcome of this apology is that what is meant by 'existence' in the preceding is the existence of the non-existent, by the context of his saying: 'for if the existence of the non-existent were established etc.', and it is not a state. The more apparent from the context is that it is that which has attained necessity and the agreement of all on the negation of the subsistence of the existence of the non-existent, even if its being a state is assumed by virtue of the subsistence of existence with the existent in general. As for other attributes, there is no necessity in the negation of their subsistence, nor even agreement of all, even if the agreement of the deniers of the state is realized. His saying: (it necessitates the conjunction of existence and non-existence) It is said: they affirm the subsistence of the essences of accidents in non-existence from...
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage continues the rebuttal (al-naqḍ) within the debate on the non-existent, now focusing on existence (al-wujūd) and attributes (al-aḥwāl). The main line argues that existence and attributes are distinct (mutamāyiza) and are not fixed (thābita) in non-existence according to the opponent. The author notes that existence is singled out despite being included in attributes, because its being fixed in non-existence is negated by agreement and necessity. The argument is that if existence were established in the state of non-existence, it would lead to the conjunction of existence and non-existence (ijtimāʿ al-wujūd wa l-ʿadam), which is impossible. Then the rebuttal by attributes is introduced. The commentary expands on several points: it discusses how some say that qualification by sensible accidents is an external effect, and that sophistry follows only if external effects are considered. The commentary explains that 'in non-existence' means in the state of non-existence of the things qualified. It then analyzes why existence is specified: because it has an advantage over other attributes. Even if existence is considered a state (ḥāl), it can be used to rebut both the deniers of the state and others, since existence qualified by distinction in the state of annihilation of quiddities would entail its subsistence. The commentary clarifies that 'existence of the non-existent' means the specific existence that qualifies the non-existent in its state of non-existence. The necessity of the conclusion is explained: subsistence, existence, and other attributes have no state of non-existence, so their subsistence in non-existence cannot be necessitated. The passage then responds to an objection from Sāḥib al-Maqāṣid about the opponent's principle that everything known is fixed in the external world. The author argues that existence and other attributes have no state of non-existence, so the question of their subsistence in non-existence does not arise. The commentary further discusses why 'in non-existence' was specified, and then addresses the issue of generalization later. Finally, it mentions that the opponent affirms the subsistence of the essences of accidents in non-existence, though the passage cuts off. Overall, this lesson clarifies the logical implications of attributing existence to non-existents and defends the rebuttal by careful distinctions.

Checkpoint
What does the author say would happen if the existence of the non-existent were established in its state of non-existence?
A) It would lead to the conjunction of existence and non-existence.
B) It would prove the non-existent is a thing.

D) It would have no logical consequence.

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be attributed to non-existents without logical contradiction, as it would force existence and non-existence to coexist.

?

?
If you truly internalized the impossibility of existence and non-existence coexisting, how would it change the way you approach doubts about God's existence or attributes?

The Second Argument: Possibility as a Positive Attribute
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the argument that possibility is a positive attribute of the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that possibility is a positive attribute of the non-existent fails, and why possibility is instead a mental consideration.

Core Idea
Possibility is not a real attribute but a mental construct.

This lesson examines the second argument used by those who claim the non-existent is a thing. They argue that possibility is a positive attribute, so whatever possesses it must be established. Al-Jurjani refutes this by denying that possibility is a real attribute, showing it is purely conceptual. Understanding this distinction helps clarify the nature of attributes and existence in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Note the structure: an argument followed by its refutation.
The passage is dense; focus on the flow from premise to conclusion.
2
Identify the argument's logic
The argument has three steps: (1) the non-existent is possible, (2) possibility is a positive attribute, (3) therefore the non-existent is established. Trace these steps and see why each is claimed.
The phrase 'صفة ثبوتية' is key—it means a real, affirmative attribute.
3
Understand the refutation
Al-Jurjani's answer is to deny the second premise: possibility is not a positive attribute but a mental consideration (أمر اعتباري). Note that he also mentions it is refuted by the same counterexamples as the first argument.
Think of 'mental consideration' as something that exists only in the mind, not externally.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: if possibility were real, it would force us to posit non-existent things as having some reality. By showing it is merely conceptual, the argument against the non-existent being a thing is preserved.

الممكن حتى نصدق به (و) عليكم ثانياً ( بيان كونه مقتضياً للثبوت ) حال العدم، فإنا من وراء المنع في المقامين الوجه ( الثاني المعدوم متصف بالإمكان ) لأن كلامنا في المعدوم الممكن (وأنه) أي الإمكان صفة) ثبوتية كما سيأتي تقريره في المرصد الثالث ( فكان المتصف به ثبوتياً ) أي ثابتاً لما مر من أن اتصاف غير الثابت بالصفة الثبوتية محال ) وجوابه منع كون الإمكان (ثبوتياً) بل هو أمر اعتباري (كما سيأتي ) في ذلك المرصد أيضاً على أنه منقوض ببعض ما نقض به الوجه الأول
The possible [entity] so that we believe in it, and [you have] secondly a demonstration that it necessitates affirmation [ثبوت] in the state of non-existence, for we are beyond prevention in both positions. The second [argument]: the non-existent [المعدوم] is characterized by possibility [إمكان] because our discussion is about the possible non-existent, and that possibility is a positive attribute [صفة ثبوتية] as will come in the third observatory, so the one characterized by it is affirmatively existing [ثبوتياً], i.e., established, because of what has passed that the characterization of what is not established by a positive attribute is impossible. Its answer: preventing that possibility is positive; rather it is a mental consideration [أمر اعتباري] as will come in that observatory also, and that it is refuted by some of what refuted the first argument.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument from proponents of the view that the non-existent (المعدوم) is a thing (شيء). The argument states: The non-existent in question is a possible entity (المعدوم الممكن). Everything possible is characterized by possibility (الإمكان). Possibility is a positive attribute (صفة ثبوتية) as will be established later. Therefore, the one characterized by a positive attribute must itself be positively established (ثابت). But the non-existent is characterized by possibility, so it is established, i.e., it is a thing. The author (al-Jurjani) gives the answer by denying that possibility is a positive attribute. Rather, possibility is a mental consideration (أمر اعتباري) that has no external reality. Hence the argument fails. He also notes that this argument is also refuted by the same counterexamples that refuted the first argument.

Key Terms
المعدوم
الإمكان
صفة ثبوتية
أمر اعتباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'possibility' is a real attribute existing in the external world.
Correction: The text clarifies that possibility is merely a mental consideration (أمر اعتباري) with no external reality.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the argument that the non-existent is characterized by possibility fail?
A) Because the non-existent cannot be characterized by anything.
B) Because possibility is not a positive attribute but a mental consideration.
C) Because possibility is not applicable to the non-existent.
D) Because the first argument was stronger.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is not a real attribute but a mental consideration, so the argument that the non-existent must be established fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that a possible future event, like a sin you may commit, is not a real attribute of your soul right now. Yet you often fear or obsess over future possibilities as if they are already real. Will you, starting today, refuse to let a mere mental consideration steal your peace and distract you from what is actually present?
?
The people of the argument falsely elevated possibility to a real attribute. In your life, do you treat your own thoughts about what might happen—whether good or bad—as if they carry real weight, influencing your emotions and actions? This lesson calls you to distinguish between mental constructs and reality. Can you commit to checking your fears and hopes against the truth that only what Allah has decreed is real, and everything else is merely a consideration in your mind?
?
If possibility is only a mental consideration, then your ability to change is also a mental construct until you act. Are you using the mental consideration of 'I could change' as an excuse to delay actual repentance? This lesson forces you to ask: Are you treating your potential to do good as if it already makes you righteous? Commit to one concrete action today that turns a mere possibility into a real act of obedience.

Answering the Otherness Objection
Al-Jurjani presents and answers the objection that the non-existent is other than God, so both must be things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani addresses the objection that otherness between God and the non-existent implies that the non-existent is a thing, and the two possible rebuttals.

Core Idea
Otherness does not automatically make the non-existent a thing.

This passage examines a key objection to the classical position that non-existent possibles are not 'things'. The opponent argues that since the non-existent is other than God, both must be things. Al-Jurjani shows how this argument reduces to earlier ones and offers two lines of response: counterexample or denying otherness as a positive attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: objection, reduction, answer.
The passage uses technical terms like 'غيرية' and 'تغاير'.
2
Identify the core objection
The opponent says: the non-existent (in pre-eternity) is not God, so it is other than Him. Two things that are other must be two things (شيئان), because otherness only applies between two things. Therefore the non-existent is a thing.
This relies on the concept of 'غيرية' (otherness) as a relation that requires two relata.
3
Understand how the objection reduces to earlier arguments
Al-Jurjani notes this objection collapses into either the first argument (distinctness implies each is a thing) or the second argument (otherness as a positive attribute).
This shows the objection is not novel.
4
Learn the two possible answers
The answer is either: (1) counterexample – there are cases where otherness does not imply real existence, or (2) deny that otherness is a positive attribute; it may be a mere relational concept.
The second answer is the preferred one in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهم شبه غيرهما أي غير الوجهين المذكورين منها ما يعود إليهما نحو أنه أي المعدوم الممكن في الأزل ليس الله فهو غيره، والغيران شيئان إذ لا يتصور التغاير إلا بين شيئين وهذا راجع إما إلى الأول إذ حاصله أن كل واحد من الغيرين متمايز عن الآخر، وإما إلى الثاني بأن يقال : كل من الغيرين متصف بالغيرية التي هي صفة ثبوتية، فجوابه إما النقض أو منع كون الغيرية صفة ثبوتية
And they have other doubts besides these two, i.e., other than the two mentioned arguments. Among them is what returns to them, such as: the possible non-existent in pre-eternity is not God, so it is other than Him, and two things that are other are two things, for otherness [تغاير] is only conceivable between two things. And this returns either to the first [argument], because its gist is that each of the two others is distinct from the other, or to the second, in that it is said: each of the two others is characterized by otherness [غيرية] which is a positive attribute. So its answer is either counterexample [نقض] or preventing that otherness is a positive attribute.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the proponents that the non-existent possible entity in pre-eternity (al-azal) is not God, so it is other (غير) than God. If two things are other, then they must be two things (شيئان), because otherness (تغاير) can only exist between two things. Hence the non-existent is a thing. The author notes that this argument can be reduced to either the first argument (distinctness implies each is a thing) or the second argument (otherness as a positive attribute). The answer is twofold: either counterexample—mentioning cases where otherness does not imply external existence—or denying that otherness is a positive attribute; otherness may be a relational concept without external reality.

Key Terms
الغير
الغيرية
التغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Otherness between two entities always implies both are real, existing things.
Correction: Otherness can be a merely conceptual or relational distinction without requiring external existence, as the non-existent is 'other' than God only in the mind, not in reality.

Checkpoint
What is the author's answer to the objection based on otherness?
A) That otherness is impossible between the non-existent and God.
B) That the non-existent is not truly other than God.
C) Either by counterexample or by denying that otherness is a positive attribute.
D) That otherness is a positive attribute that requires existence.

Key Takeaway
The objection that otherness implies thing-ness is answered by showing it reduces to earlier arguments and by denying otherness as a positive attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that something you felt or imagined was real simply because it was distinct from God? In what specific area of your life have you given conceptual 'otherness' the status of a real thing, and what would it look like to deny it that status today?
?
When you think about your own existence as a creature distinct from the Creator, do you secretly believe that your 'otherness' gives you a kind of independent reality? How would your heart change if you truly accepted that your otherness is not a positive attribute but merely a relational concept that vanishes the moment you consider God's absolute reality?
?
The objection claims that otherness requires both things to be real. But what if the only truly real thing is God, and everything else exists only as a shadow or a sign of Him? Are you willing to live as if your entire being is nothing but a contingent relation—a 'not-God' that has no standing except through Him? What would you have to let go of to make that shift?

The Third Objection: Perception Parallel to Knowledge
Al-Jurjani refutes the perception-knowledge analogy for non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that attempts to prove non-existents are things by analogy with knowledge, and why it is refuted by a counterexample.

Core Idea
Not everything known can be sensed—analogy breaks down.

Theologians used an interesting analogy: if knowledge can grasp non-existents, then sensation—being a type of knowledge—should also grasp them. But you can know what you cannot sense. This lesson unpacks that objection and its simple yet powerful rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objection builds an analogy: sensation is a type of knowledge; if knowledge can be about non-things, then sensation should also be able to perceive non-things.
This is a common strategy—using an accepted premise to force a conclusion.
2
Understand the reply: 'it returns to the first'
Al-Jurjani notes this argument is just a variant of the first objection. Recognizing this helps to apply the same counterexample strategy.
The first argument likely used a similar move; check your earlier notes.
3
Identify the refutation: counterexample (naqd)
The answer is simply 'counterexample'—meaning we can produce a case where the premise holds but the conclusion fails. For example, we can know a past event without sensing it now.
This shows the analogy is not universal, so the conclusion doesn't follow.
4
Reflect on the lesson for kalam methodology
This passage demonstrates how theologians use logical disanalogy and counterexamples to block unwanted conclusions.
Counterexample (naqd) is a common tool in disputation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و نحو أن الإدراك أي الإحساس علم أي نوع منه، فلو جاز أن يكون لنا معلوم هو ليس بشيء فليجز أن يكون لنا مدرك أي محسوس ليس بشيء وهذا راجع إلى الأول وجوابه النقض
And similar [to the above] is that perception [إدراك], i.e., sensation [إحساس], is a type of knowledge [علم]. So if it is permissible for us to have a known thing [معلوم] that is not a thing, then let it be permissible for us to have a perceived thing [مدرك], i.e., sensed [محسوس], that is not a thing. And this returns to the first [argument], and its answer is counterexample [نقض].
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection: Sensation (الإحساس) is a kind of knowledge (العلم). Knowledge can be about something that is not a thing (i.e., the non-existent is known). By analogy, sensation should also be able to perceive what is not a thing. But sensation only perceives existents, so the non-existent must be a thing. The author responds that this argument reduces to the first argument (which was probably refuted by counterexample). The answer is simply to give a counterexample (نقض), showing that the analogy is false. For instance, we can know something without it being externally existent, but we cannot sense it without existence.

Key Terms
الإدراك
الإحساس
العلم
النقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because both knowledge and perception are cognitive acts, they have the same conditions for intentional objects, but the author shows they differ: knowledge can be of non-existents, perception cannot.
Correction: Knowledge includes nonexistent objects, but sensation requires existence. The analogy fails because sensation is a specific subtype of knowledge with additional constraints.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the argument that if we can know a non-existent, we can also sense it?
A) He accepts the analogy but adds a condition.
B) He denies that sensation is a type of knowledge.
C) He gives a counterexample, showing the analogy breaks down.
D) He argues that the non-existent cannot be known either.

Key Takeaway
The objection that sensation must parallel knowledge fails because a counterexample shows that not everything known is sensed, so the analogy is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know truths about the unseen (Allah, angels, resurrection) that you have never perceived. Yet, do your worries and actions reflect that you treat these as more certain than what you sense? What is one specific change you can make today to live by that knowledge?
?
The passage shows that a single counterexample (naqd) can invalidate an entire analogy. Is there a false analogy you rely on to justify a shortcut in your deen—like 'just this once doesn't matter' or 'everyone does it so it's okay'? How will you dismantle that lie today?

Three Proofs for the Reality of the Non-Existent
Three proofs for the reality of the non-existent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational proofs used to establish that the non-existent (al-maʿdūm) has a reality (thubūt) and the responses to these proofs.

Core Idea
The non-existent is not absolutely nothing — it has a fixed essence in God's knowledge.

The question of whether the non-existent is a thing was a central debate in classical kalām. Before discussing divine attributes, theologians needed to clarify the ontological status of potential things. This passage presents three arguments for the reality of the non-existent, along with their refutations. Understanding these arguments helps grasp how kalām thinkers approached the relationship between existence and essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice how each proof is structured as a conditional argument followed by a response.
The passage uses technical terms like 'thubūt' (fixity) and 'naqḍ' (refutation).
2
Identify the three arguments
The passage presents three distinct proofs: (1) from distinction of two things, (2) from intention to create, (3) from perception as a type of knowledge. For each, identify the premise and the conclusion.
Each proof concludes that the non-existent must be a thing (shaw’).
3
Examine the responses
For each proof, the text offers a response, either by refutation (naqḍ) or by denying a premise. Understand why the responses are considered valid within the kalām framework.
The commentator adds nuance, especially regarding the common property (jāmiʿ) in analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السادس : المعدوم شيء أم لا

إلا بين شيئين وهذا راجع إما إلى الأول إذ حاصله أن كل واحد من الغيرين متمايز عن الآخر، وإما إلى الثاني بأن يقال : كل من الغيرين متصف بالغيرية التي هي صفة ثبوتية، فجوابه إما النقض أو منع كون الغيرية صفة ثبوتية ) ونحو أن القصد إلى إيجاد غير المعين ممتنع) فلو لم تكن الذوات الممكنة ثابتة في العدم ومتعينة متميزة فيه لم يتصور من الفاعل القصد إلى إيجادها، فإن ما ليس بمتعين في نفسه لم يتميز القصد إليه عن القصد إلى غيره، فلم يكن هو بكونه مقصوداً بذلك القصد أولى من غيره، ومحصوله أنه متعين متميز، فيكون ثابتاً فقد رجع إلى الوجه الأول، والجواب كالجواب فإن التميز في علم الفاعل كاف في القصد (و) نحو ( أن الإدراك ) أي الإحساس ) علم ) أي نوع منه ، فلو جاز أن يكون لنا معلوم هو ليس بشيء ( فليجز) أن يكون لنا ( مدرك ) أي محسوس (ليس بشيء وهذا راجع إلى الأول وجوابه النقض

قوله : ( راجع إما إلى الأول إلخ ) فإن الحكم على الغيرين بأنهما شيئان أي ثابتان إما لأجل تعددهما وإما لأجل الاتصاف بالغيرية.
قوله : ( فقد رجع إلخ) لما كان التعين عين التميز ، أو مستلزماً له أرجعه إلى الوجه الأول، وإن كان يمكن رجوعه إلى الثاني بأن التعين صفة ثبوتية.
قوله : ( فإن التميز إلخ) تعليل لمقدمة مطوية في التشبيه فإن الجواب بالنقض لا شبهة فيه، والجواب يمنع كون التعين والتميز مقتضياً للثبوت فيه خفاء إزالة بأن التميز في علم الفاعل كاف، وهو لا يقتضي الثبوت الخارجي، وبعض الناظرين لماخفي عليه معنى الفاء غيره إلى الواو، وجعله جواباً آخر، وحمل قوله كالجواب على النقض ولا يخفى سماجته .

قوله : (أو منع كون الغيرية صفة (ثبوتية وإن قال به مشايخنا القائلون : بأن الصفات لا ولا غيره كما سيجيء في موضعه، وإنما لم يجب بمنع أنه غيره بناء على أن الغيرين موجودان ينفك أحدهما عن الآخر في الوجود، أو في الحيز لأن مرادهم الغيرية بحسب اللغة . قوله : (فقد رجع إلى الوجه الأول وإن حمل قولهم على أن التعين صفة ثبوتية يرجع إلى الثاني إلا أن التعيين عند المتكلمين أمر اعتباري كما سيجيء .

 قوله : ( النقض بالمستحيل أي إبطال الملازمة المدلول عليها بالشرطية بالمستحيل، فإنه معلوم ليس بثابت ولا يمكن إدراكه بالحواس، فقد تحقق لنا معلوم ليس بشيء مع عدم جواز كونه مدركاً بالحواس سمي إبطال الملازمة نقضاً لاستلزامه نقض كون المعلومية علة للثبوت، وبما ذكرنا اندفع ما قيل : إنه لادخل لعدم كونه مدركاً بالحواس في نقض كون المعلومية علة كما لا يخفى . قوله : (وأيضا ما ذكره إلخ) أي فيما ذكره من القياس الاستثنائي تمثيل خال عن الجامع أي الأمر المشترك المؤثر في الحكم، وذلك لأن الملازمة المدلول عليها بالشرطية مبينة بالتمثيل أي بقياس المعلوم المتعقل على المحسوس بجامع المعلومية المشار إليه بقوله : الإدراك علم لا نسلم وجود الجامع فإن الإحساس يخالف التعقل في الأحكام ألا ترى تخالفهما في اقتضاء الوجود وعدمه فليجز تخالفهما في اقتضاء الثبوت ،وعدمه وبما حررنا لك من أنه منع اندفع الشكوك الموردة هاهنا من أن التخالف بالنوع لا ينافي وجود الجامع، وأنه يشعر بأنه لولا التخالف بالنوع بأن يكونا متحدين بالنوع يحصل الجامع بينهما، وأن اللازم مما ذكره عدم كون المعلومية جامعاً، وهو لا يستلزم خلو التمثيل عن الجامع مطلقاً، وأن التنوير المذكور لا يثبت المخالفة بالنوع لأن مدارها على توهم كونه مدعياً لإثبات الخلو عن الجامع كما لا يخفى .
The First Observatory - The Sixth Section: Is the non-existent a thing or not?

Except between two things, and this refers either to the first, since its conclusion is that each one of the two others is distinct from the other, or to the second, by saying: each of the two others is qualified by otherness (al-ghayriyya) which is a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya). So the answer to it is either by refutation (al-naqḍ) or by denying that otherness is a positive attribute. And like that: the intention (al-qaṣd) to bring into existence a non-specific (non-existent) is impossible. For if the possible essences (al-dhāt al-mumkina) were not fixed (thābita) in non-existence and determined and distinct in it, then the agent's intention to bring them into existence would not be conceivable. For that which is not determined in itself, the intention towards it is not distinguished from the intention towards another, so it is not more entitled to be intended by that intention than another. Its sum is that it is determined and distinct, so it is fixed (thābit). So it has returned to the first aspect, and the answer is like the answer: for distinction (al-tamayyuz) in the knowledge of the agent is sufficient for intention. And (also) like that: perception (al-idrāk) i.e. sensation (al-iḥsās) is knowledge (ʿilm), i.e. a type of it. So if it is permissible that we have a known thing (maʿlūm) that is not a thing, then let it be permissible that we have a perceived thing (mudrak) i.e. sensed that is not a thing. And this refers to the first, and its answer is refutation (al-naqḍ).

His saying: (refers either to the first etc.): The judgment on the two others that they are two things i.e. two fixed things is either because of their multiplicity or because of being qualified by otherness.
His saying: (so it has returned etc.): Since determination (al-taʿayyun) is identical to distinction (al-tamayyuz) or entails it, he referred it to the first aspect, although it could be referred to the second because determination is a positive attribute.
His saying: (for distinction etc.): An explanation of a premise hidden in the analogy. For the answer by refutation is without doubt, and the answer prevents that determination and distinction necessitate fixity. There is a subtlety removed by saying that distinction in the knowledge of the agent is sufficient, and it does not entail external fixity. Some of the investigators, because the meaning of the particle fa was hidden from them, changed it to wāw and made it another answer, and carried his saying 'like the answer' as refutation, and its absurdity is not hidden.
His saying: (i.e. a type of it because knowledge is divided into sensation, imagination, estimation, and intellection. His saying: (so if it is permissible etc.): That is, since sensation is a type of knowledge, the known i.e. the intelligible is like the sensed in being known. So if it is permissible that etc. His saying: (and this refers to the first): Because the inference in the first is by being known through its entailment of distinction, and here the inference is by being known without mediation. Its formulation is: every possible non-existent is known, and every known is fixed, because if it were not fixed, it would be permissible that we have a known that is not fixed, and if that were so, then let it be permissible that we have a sensed that is not fixed, because the known is like the sensed in requiring fixity, due to the commonality of being known. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false.
His saying: (or denying that otherness is a positive attribute): Even if our masters who say that attributes are neither [something] nor other than it, as will come in its place, said it. And he did not answer by denying that it is other, based on the fact that the two others are existing, one separated from the other in existence or in place, because their meaning of otherness is linguistic.
His saying: (so it has returned to the first aspect): Even if one carries their saying that determination is a positive attribute, it returns to the second, except that determination according to the theologians is a relative matter (amr iʿtibārī), as will come.

His saying: (refutation by the impossible): i.e. invalidating the implication indicated by the conditional by the impossible. For it is known and not fixed and cannot be perceived by the senses. So we have realized a known thing that is not a thing, together with the impossibility of its being perceived by the senses. It is called invalidating the implication a refutation because it entails invalidating that being known is a cause for fixity. And by what we have mentioned, what was said is repelled: that the fact that it is not perceived by the senses has no part in invalidating that being known is a cause, as is not hidden.
His saying: (and also what he mentioned etc.): i.e. in what he mentioned from the exceptional syllogism is an analogy devoid of the common property (al-jāmiʿ), i.e. the shared effective factor in the judgment. This is because the implication indicated by the conditional is based on analogy, i.e. by comparing the intellectually known to the sensed by the commonality of being known, indicated by his saying: perception is knowledge. We do not accept the existence of the common property, for sensation differs from intellection in rulings. Do you not see their difference in requiring existence and non-existence? So let it be permissible for them to differ in requiring fixity and non-fixity. And by what we have set forth for you from denial, the doubts raised here are repelled: that difference in type does not negate the existence of the common property, and that it indicates that were it not for difference in type, they would be united in type and the common property would obtain between them, and that the necessary consequence of what he mentioned is that being known is not a common property, and that does not entail that the analogy is devoid of a common property absolutely, and that the mentioned clarification does not prove difference in type because its basis is the supposition that he claims to prove its being devoid of the common property, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a composite of three proofs that the non-existent (al-maʿdūm) is a thing (shayʾ), each with responses. The first proof argues from the distinction between two things (al-ghayrayn): if two things are distinct, each must be a thing. The response either refutes the implication or denies that 'otherness' is a positive attribute. The second proof argues from intention (al-qaṣd): if essences were not fixed in non-existence, the agent could not intend to create them. The response is that distinction in God's knowledge is sufficient for intention. The third proof argues from perception (al-idrāk): since perception is a type of knowledge, if a non-thing can be known, it could also be perceived. The response is that the impossible (al-mustaḥīl) is known but not perceived. The commentary explains each point, noting that the first two proofs reduce to the same principle and that the response to the third is by refutation (naqḍ). The commentator also addresses a subtlety: the common property (al-jāmiʿ) between intellection and sensation is denied, so the analogy fails.

القصد

تعين
صفة ثبوتية
الغيرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The non-existent is absolutely nothing with no reality whatsoever.
Correction: The non-existent has a fixed essence (thubūt) in non-existence, distinct from other essences, which makes intention and distinction possible. It is not absolutely nothing.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument (from distinction), why must the non-existent be a thing?
A) Because it is perceived
B) Because it is intended by the agent
C) Because it is distinct from another thing
D) Because it is known

Key Takeaway
The non-existent has a fixed essence (thubūt) in God's knowledge, which is why intention and distinction are possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every possible action you could ever do is already known to God and fixed in His knowledge (whether you do it or not), would that make you more intentional about your choices? How would you live differently today knowing that nothing you decide is outside His knowledge?
?
Your own non-existence before you were born was a 'fixed essence' in God's knowledge. Does that change how you see your own coming into existence? Are you grateful that He intended you specifically, not just any possible person?
?
When you fail to do something good, is it because you think that possibility was 'nothing' until you did it? Or do you recognize that even your missed opportunities were real in God's knowledge, and that He knows exactly what you could have been? How does that accountability press on your heart right now?
