Defining 'Thing': Ash'ari vs Mu'tazila Views
Defines the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions on 'shay'' and discusses the implication for the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila definitions of 'thing' and the theological implications for the impossible.

Core Idea
A 'thing' is either what exists or what is known—and that choice has deep consequences.

In Islamic theology, the simple term 'thing' (shay') becomes a battleground for fundamental ontology. The Ash'aris restrict it to existents, while the Mu'tazila broaden it to the known, forcing them to confront the status of the impossible. This passage lays out those sides and the resulting objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the passage and spot how the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila define 'shay''. The Ash'aris equate it with existent, while the Mu'tazila equate it with known.
Notice that each definition has consequences for what can be called a 'thing'.
2
Understand the consequence for the impossible
For the Mu'tazila, since the impossible is known, it would logically be called a 'thing'. Understand why this is problematic.
The Ash'ari definition avoids this problem entirely.
3
Consider the Bahshamiyya escape
Read the exception: some Mu'tazila (Bahshamiyya) claim the impossible is known only by analogy, not direct knowledge. Reflect on how this attempts to solve the problem.
This shows the debate's depth and the flexibility of theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن المعدوم شيء أم لا، فإنه بحث معنوي ( الشيء عندنا الموجود ) أي لفظ الشيء عند الأشاعرة يطلق على الموجود فقط، وكل شيء عندهم موجود وكل موجود شيء. وقال الجاحظ والبصرية من المعتزلة: ( هو المعلوم ويلزمهم المستحيل ) أي يلزمهم إطلاق لفظ الشيء على المستحيل لأنه معلوم، إلا أن يقولوا: المستحيل لا يعلم إلا على سبيل التشبيه والتمثيل كما ذهب إليه البهشمية.
Whether the non-existent is a thing? That is a conceptual discussion. (The thing with us is the existent) meaning: the word 'thing' according to the Ash'aris applies only to the existent, and every thing with them is existent and every existent is a thing. And al-Jahiz and the Basrans from the Mu'tazila said: (It is the known, and the impossible is necessitated for them), i.e., they are necessitated to apply the word 'thing' to the impossible because it is known, unless they say: the impossible is not known except by way of analogy and similitude, as the Bahshamiyya held.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines the core issue of whether a non-existent (ma‘dum) is a 'thing' (shay'). It states that according to the Ash‘aris, the term 'shay'' applies only to the existent (mawjud); everything existent is a thing and vice versa. It then presents the view of al-Jahiz and the Basran Mu‘tazila that 'shay'' means 'the known' (ma‘lum), which forces them to apply the term 'thing' to the impossible (mustahil) since it is known, unless they adopt the Bahshamiyya position that the impossible is known only by way of analogy (tashbīh wa tamthīl). The passage thus frames the debate and the first objection.

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'thing' in kalam carries weighty ontological implications, linking language to reality and divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

What might be the hidden theological commitments in your own language? Take time today to examine one concept you use frequently and trace its implications.

The Verbal Dispute Over 'Thing' (Shay')
Lists views and concludes verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main positions on the term 'thing' (shay') in early Islamic theology and why the dispute is considered verbal.

Core Idea
The dispute over 'thing' is about language, not reality—a classic verbal disagreement.

This passage surveys four distinct theological schools on the meaning of 'shay'. After listing them, the author concludes that the conflict is merely verbal—a disagreement over word usage—and that the true criterion is linguistic convention, not rational proof. This lesson guides you through each view and the reasoning behind the verbal dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify each school's position
Carefully read the Arabic and translation. Note how each school defines 'shay''—as eternal, contingent, body, or existent.
Notice the labels: Nashshai, Jahmiyya, Hisham, Basran Mu'tazila.
2
Understand the author's classification of the dispute
The author states the dispute is verbal (lafẓī), meaning it's about word usage, not about a real difference in theology. Reflect on why he says this.
The passage says 'the truth is what language and transmitted usage support'.
3
Analyze the argument for linguistic criterion
The author argues that reason has no role in establishing languages; only linguistic convention matters. He gives evidence from common usage: people accept 'the existent is a thing' and reject 'it is not a thing'.
This is a key methodological principle in kalam.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this shows that some theological disputes reduce to definitions. Understanding this can prevent unnecessary divisions.
The Ash'ari position is mentioned as close to the Basran view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الناشي أبو العياش هو القديم وللحادث مجاز، وقالت الجهمية هو الحادث، وقال هشام بن الحكم هو الجسم، وقال أبو الحسين البصري والنصيبيني من معتزلة البصرة هو حقيقة في الموجود ومجاز في المعدوم، وهذا قريب من مذهب الأشاعرة، والنزاع لفظي متعلق بلفظ الشيء وأنه على ماذا يطلق، والحق ما ساعد عليه اللغة والنقل، إذ لا مجال للعقل في إثبات اللغات، والظاهر معنا، فإن أهل اللغة في كل عصر يطلقون لفظ الشيء على الموجود، حتى لو قيل عندهم الموجود شيء تلقوه بالقبول، ولو قيل: ليس بشيء قابلوه بالإنكار ولا يفرقون في إطلاق لفظ الشيء بين أن يكون.
And al-Nashshai Abu al-'Ayyash said: (The thing is) the eternal, and for the contingent it is metaphorical. And the Jahmiyya said: It is the contingent. And Hisham bin al-Hakam said: It is the body. And Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Nasibini from the Mu'tazila of Basra said: It is literal for the existent and metaphorical for the non-existent. And this is close to the school of the Ash'aris. And the dispute is verbal, pertaining to the word 'thing' and to what it is applied. And the truth is what language and transmitted usage support, for there is no room for reason in establishing languages. And the apparent (evidence) is with us, for the people of language in every age apply the word 'thing' to the existent, such that if it is said among them 'the existent is a thing', they accept it, and if it is said 'it is not a thing', they reject it, and they do not differentiate in applying the word 'thing' between being.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage enumerates the positions of various schools on the meaning of 'shay''. Al-Nashshai holds that it is literal for the eternal (qadīm) and metaphorical for the contingent (ḥādith). The Jahmiyya hold it is literal for the contingent. Hisham bin al-Hakam holds it is literal for the body (jism). Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Nasibini from the Basran Mu'tazila hold it is literal for the existent and metaphorical for the non-existent, which is close to the Ash'ari view. The passage then declares that the dispute is verbal (lafẓī), revolving around the term 'shay'', and that the truth is determined by language (lugha) and transmitted usage (naql), not reason. It argues that language users in every age apply 'shay'' to the existent, accepting 'the existent is a thing' and rejecting 'it is not a thing', without distinguishing different types of existents.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these are arbitrary definitions, but each reflects a theological commitment about ontology and God's attributes.
Correction: The passage shows that despite different definitions, the dispute is verbal; the real criterion is linguistic usage, not theology per se.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the term 'thing' is a verbal one, decided by linguistic convention, not rational argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in debates about religious matters, do you ever stop to check whether the disagreement is merely verbal? How often do you assume a difference in belief when it's actually a difference in terminology? Commit to asking 'What do you mean by that word?' before arguing.
?
The passage says 'there is no room for reason in establishing languages.' Reflect on a time you tried to rationalize a linguistic usage in theology. Were you imposing reason where language should decide? Are there other areas where you rely on reason over transmitted usage? What would it look like to submit to the linguistic conventions of revelation?
?
If the dispute over 'thing' is verbal, how many other theological disagreements might also be verbal? How might that change the way you perceive those who differ from you? Can you think of a specific disagreement you hold strongly that might be more about words than substance? What would it mean to let go of that verbal attachment for the sake of unity?

The Dispute over 'Thing' – Opinions and Verbal Nature
Al-Jurjani enumerates opinions on applying 'thing' and declares the dispute verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various opinions on whether the non-existent is called a 'thing' and why al-Jurjani considers the dispute to be verbal.

Core Idea
The dispute over 'thing' is about word usage, not reality.

Al-Jurjani begins by cataloguing the positions of different Islamic sects on whether the term 'thing' (شيء) applies to the non-existent. After listing these views, he asserts that the disagreement is merely verbal, concerning the conventional meaning of the word rather than any real metaphysical difference. This sets the stage for his argument that language and revelation settle the issue.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of opinions
Read the passage carefully, noting each sect's view on the application of 'thing'. Pay attention to the distinctions between literal and metaphorical usage.
The Bahshamiyya, al-Nashi', Jahmiyya, Hisham, and Basran Mu'tazila are mentioned.
2
Identify the verbal dispute claim
Notice that after listing the opinions, al-Jurjani states that the dispute is verbal (النزاع لفظي). Understand what this means: the disagreement is about the conventional meaning of the word, not about real existence.
This is a key methodological point in kalam.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does labeling a dispute as 'verbal' matter? How does it change how we approach the evidence? Think about how this approach can resolve many theological disagreements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السادس : المعدوم شيء أم لا
إطلاق البشيء على المستحيل لأنه معلوم، إلا أن يقولوا : المستحيل لا يعلم إلا على سبيل التشبيه والتمثيل كما ذهب إليه البهشمية ( و ) قال ( الناشي أبو العياش هو القديم وللحادث مجاز و قالت : ( الجهمية هو الحادث و قال (هشام) بن الحكم هو الجسم (و) قال ( أبو الحسين البصري ( والنصيبيني) من معتزلة البصرة ( هو حقيقة في الموجود ومجاز في المعدوم) وهذا قريب من مذهب الأشاعرة ) والنزاع لفظي ) متعلق بلفظ الشيء وأنه على ماذا يطلق ) والحق ما ساعد عليه اللغة والنقل، إذ لا مجال للعقل في إثبات اللغات ) والظاهر معنا ) فإن أهل اللغة في كل عصر يطلقون لفظ الشيء على الموجود ، حتى لو قيل عندهم الموجود شيء تلقوه بالقبول، ولو قيل : ليس بشيء قابلوه بالإنكار ولا يفرقون في إطلاق لفظ الشيء بين أن يكون الموجود قديماً أو حادثاً جسماً أو عرضاً ( ونحو : خلقتك من قبل ولم تك شيئاً [مريم: 9] ينفي إطلاقه ) بطريق الحقيقة ( على المعدوم ) لأن الحقيقة لا يصح نفيها، فيبطل به قول الجاحظ (و) قوله : والله على كل شيء قدير [الممتحنة: 7] ينفي اختصاصه بالقديم لأن القدرة إنما تتعلق بالحادث دون القديم والأصل في الإطلاق الحقيقة، فيبطل به قول أبي العياش الناشي (و) قوله : ( ولا تقولن لشيء إني فاعل ذلك ) ينفي اختصاصه بالجسم فيبطل به قول هشام ( و) قول لبيد ( ألا كل شيء ما خلا الله باطل ) ينفي (اختصاصه بالحادث لأن الأصل في الاستثناء أن يكون متصلاً، فيبطل به قول الجهمية
The First Observatory - The Sixth Objective: Is the non-existent a thing or not?
Applying 'thing' to the impossible because it is known, unless they say: the impossible is not known except by way of analogy and representation, as the Bahshamiyya held. And al-Nashi' Abu al-'Ayyash said: it is the eternal, and for the temporal it is metaphorical. And the Jahmiyya said: it is the temporal. And Hisham ibn al-Hakam said: it is the body. And Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Nusaybi from the Mu'tazila of Basra said: it is literal regarding the existent and metaphorical regarding the non-existent. And this is close to the Ash'ari school. And the dispute is verbal, pertaining to the word 'thing' and to what it is applied. And the truth is what language and transmitted evidence support, for there is no room for reason in establishing languages. And the apparent is with us, for the people of language in every era apply the word 'thing' to the existent. Even if one says among them 'the existent is a thing' they accept it, and if one says 'it is not a thing' they reject it. And they do not distinguish in applying the word 'thing' between whether the existent is eternal or temporal, substance or accident. And like His saying: 'I created you before when you were nothing' (Maryam 9) negates its application, by way of literalness, to the non-existent, because a literal meaning cannot be negated. Thereby the saying of al-Jahiz is refuted. And His saying: 'And Allah over all things is capable' (al-Mumtahina 7) negates its restriction to the eternal, because ability is only connected to the temporal, not the eternal, and the principle in application is literalness. Thereby the saying of Abu al-'Ayyash al-Nashi' is refuted. And His saying: 'And never say of a thing, I will do that' negates its restriction to the body. Thereby the saying of Hisham is refuted. And the saying of Labid: 'Indeed, everything except God is false' negates its restriction to the temporal because the principle in exception is that it be connected. Thereby the saying of the Jahmiyya is refuted.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, classification, and proof. It begins by defining the various opinions regarding whether the non-existent can be called a 'thing' (شَيْء). Al-Jurjani lists the views of several sects: the Bahshamiyya say it applies to the impossible because it is known (with an exception regarding knowledge of the impossible); al-Nashi' says it is literally the eternal and metaphorically the temporal; the Jahmiyya say it is only the temporal; Hisham ibn al-Hakam says it is only the body; and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Nusaybi from the Basran Mu'tazila say it is literal for the existent and metaphorical for the non-existent, which is close to the Ash'ari position. Al-Jurjani then classifies the dispute as verbal (نزاع لفظي)—that is, it concerns only the conventional usage of the term, not a real metaphysical difference. He then states the correct position: it is what language and transmitted texts support, not reason, because reason has no role in establishing languages. He provides evidence: in every era, language users apply 'thing' only to what exists; they accept it when said and reject it when denied. Then he gives four scriptural examples: verse from Maryam about creation from nothing refutes al-Jahiz (who applied 'thing' to the non-existent); verse about Allah's power over all things refutes al-Nashi' (who restricted it to the eternal); verse about not saying 'I will do that' of a thing refutes Hisham (who restricted it to bodies); and the poetic line of Labid that everything other than God is false refutes the Jahmiyya (who restricted it to the temporal). The argument follows the principle that original usage is literal unless evidence forces a metaphorical interpretation, and these texts would be meaningless if 'thing' could apply to non-existents in the same way.

معدوم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think the debate is about whether non-existents have any reality at all.
Correction: Al-Jurjani clarifies it's about the word 'thing' and its conventional usage, not a real metaphysical divide.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, what kind of dispute is the disagreement over whether the non-existent is a 'thing'?
A) A real metaphysical dispute
B) A verbal dispute about word usage
C) A dispute about the existence of God

Key Takeaway
The various opinions on whether the non-existent is a 'thing' are ultimately a verbal dispute about the word's usage, not a real conflict about existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How deeply do you rely on linguistic conventions versus the realities they describe? Will you examine your own assumptions about words and meanings more carefully from now on?

Proofs from Language and Revelation that 'Thing' Applies Only to the Existent
Al-Jurjani gives linguistic and scriptural arguments for 'thing' applied only to the existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani uses linguistic usage and Qur'anic verses to prove that 'thing' is literally applied only to the existent.

Core Idea
Language and revelation show that 'thing' refers only to what exists.

Having established that the dispute is verbal, al-Jurjani now provides evidence from linguistic practice and four scriptural proofs. He argues that people naturally apply 'thing' to existents, and that the Qur'an and poetry consistently use it that way. These proofs refute the views that restrict 'thing' to specific categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the passage carefully, noting the linguistic argument first, then the four scriptural proofs. Identify which view each proof refutes.
2
Understand the linguistic argument
Al-Jurjani argues that common usage across all eras applies 'thing' only to the existent. People accept 'the existent is a thing' and reject 'it is not a thing'. This shows the literal meaning is the existent.
This argument is based on convention, not reason.
3
Analyze the scriptural proofs
Go through each verse or poetic line: (1) Maryam 9 refutes al-Jahiz, (2) al-Mumtahina 7 refutes al-Nashi', (3) the verse about saying 'I will do that' refutes Hisham, (4) Labid's poetry refutes the Jahmiyya. Understand the logic behind each refutation.
The principle is that literal meaning cannot be negated, and these texts would be problematic if 'thing' applied to non-existents.
4
Synthesize the overall proof
Consider how these proofs together support the Ash'ari position that 'thing' is literal for the existent, whether eternal or temporal, substance or accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السادس : المعدوم شيء أم لا
إطلاق البشيء على المستحيل لأنه معلوم، إلا أن يقولوا : المستحيل لا يعلم إلا على سبيل التشبيه والتمثيل كما ذهب إليه البهشمية ( و ) قال ( الناشي أبو العياش هو القديم وللحادث مجاز و قالت : ( الجهمية هو الحادث و قال (هشام) بن الحكم هو الجسم (و) قال ( أبو الحسين البصري ( والنصيبيني) من معتزلة البصرة ( هو حقيقة في الموجود ومجاز في المعدوم) وهذا قريب من مذهب الأشاعرة ) والنزاع لفظي ) متعلق بلفظ الشيء وأنه على ماذا يطلق ) والحق ما ساعد عليه اللغة والنقل، إذ لا مجال للعقل في إثبات اللغات ) والظاهر معنا ) فإن أهل اللغة في كل عصر يطلقون لفظ الشيء على الموجود ، حتى لو قيل عندهم الموجود شيء تلقوه بالقبول، ولو قيل : ليس بشيء قابلوه بالإنكار ولا يفرقون في إطلاق لفظ الشيء بين أن يكون الموجود قديماً أو حادثاً جسماً أو عرضاً ( ونحو : خلقتك من قبل ولم تك شيئاً [مريم: 9] ينفي إطلاقه ) بطريق الحقيقة ( على المعدوم ) لأن الحقيقة لا يصح نفيها، فيبطل به قول الجاحظ (و) قوله : والله على كل شيء قدير [الممتحنة: 7] ينفي اختصاصه بالقديم لأن القدرة إنما تتعلق بالحادث دون القديم والأصل في الإطلاق الحقيقة، فيبطل به قول أبي العياش الناشي (و) قوله : ( ولا تقولن لشيء إني فاعل ذلك ) ينفي اختصاصه بالجسم فيبطل به قول هشام ( و) قول لبيد ( ألا كل شيء ما خلا الله باطل ) ينفي (اختصاصه بالحادث لأن الأصل في الاستثناء أن يكون متصلاً، فيبطل به قول الجهمية
The First Observatory - The Sixth Objective: Is the non-existent a thing or not?
Applying 'thing' to the impossible because it is known, unless they say: the impossible is not known except by way of analogy and representation, as the Bahshamiyya held. And al-Nashi' Abu al-'Ayyash said: it is the eternal, and for the temporal it is metaphorical. And the Jahmiyya said: it is the temporal. And Hisham ibn al-Hakam said: it is the body. And Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Nusaybi from the Mu'tazila of Basra said: it is literal regarding the existent and metaphorical regarding the non-existent. And this is close to the Ash'ari school. And the dispute is verbal, pertaining to the word 'thing' and to what it is applied. And the truth is what language and transmitted evidence support, for there is no room for reason in establishing languages. And the apparent is with us, for the people of language in every era apply the word 'thing' to the existent. Even if one says among them 'the existent is a thing' they accept it, and if one says 'it is not a thing' they reject it. And they do not distinguish in applying the word 'thing' between whether the existent is eternal or temporal, substance or accident. And like His saying: 'I created you before when you were nothing' (Maryam 9) negates its application, by way of literalness, to the non-existent, because a literal meaning cannot be negated. Thereby the saying of al-Jahiz is refuted. And His saying: 'And Allah over all things is capable' (al-Mumtahina 7) negates its restriction to the eternal, because ability is only connected to the temporal, not the eternal, and the principle in application is literalness. Thereby the saying of Abu al-'Ayyash al-Nashi' is refuted. And His saying: 'And never say of a thing, I will do that' negates its restriction to the body. Thereby the saying of Hisham is refuted. And the saying of Labid: 'Indeed, everything except God is false' negates its restriction to the temporal because the principle in exception is that it be connected. Thereby the saying of the Jahmiyya is refuted.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, classification, and proof. It begins by defining the various opinions regarding whether the non-existent can be called a 'thing' (شَيْء). Al-Jurjani lists the views of several sects: the Bahshamiyya say it applies to the impossible because it is known (with an exception regarding knowledge of the impossible); al-Nashi' says it is literally the eternal and metaphorically the temporal; the Jahmiyya say it is only the temporal; Hisham ibn al-Hakam says it is only the body; and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and al-Nusaybi from the Basran Mu'tazila say it is literal for the existent and metaphorical for the non-existent, which is close to the Ash'ari position. Al-Jurjani then classifies the dispute as verbal (نزاع لفظي)—that is, it concerns only the conventional usage of the term, not a real metaphysical difference. He then states the correct position: it is what language and transmitted texts support, not reason, because reason has no role in establishing languages. He provides evidence: in every era, language users apply 'thing' only to what exists; they accept it when said and reject it when denied. Then he gives four scriptural examples: verse from Maryam about creation from nothing refutes al-Jahiz (who applied 'thing' to the non-existent); verse about Allah's power over all things refutes al-Nashi' (who restricted it to the eternal); verse about not saying 'I will do that' of a thing refutes Hisham (who restricted it to bodies); and the poetic line of Labid that everything other than God is false refutes the Jahmiyya (who restricted it to the temporal). The argument follows the principle that original usage is literal unless evidence forces a metaphorical interpretation, and these texts would be meaningless if 'thing' could apply to non-existents in the same way.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that reason can determine the meaning of words.
Correction: Al-Jurjani asserts that language is established by convention, not reason, so we must look to linguistic and scriptural evidence.

Checkpoint
Which view is refuted by the verse 'And Allah over all things is capable' (al-Mumtahina 7)?
A) The view that 'thing' applies to the non-existent
B) The view that 'thing' is restricted to the eternal
C) The view that 'thing' is restricted to bodies
D) The view that 'thing' is restricted to the temporal

Key Takeaway
Through linguistic custom and four revelatory texts, al-Jurjani demonstrates that 'thing' in its literal sense applies only to the existent, whether eternal or temporal, substance or accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about ‘things’ in your daily life—your plans, possessions, problems—do you unconsciously affirm that they are real, while forgetting that everything besides God is ultimately false? What would change if you truly felt that ‘all things’ are false except Him?
?
Al-Jurjani shows that the word ‘thing’ cannot apply to the non-existent. Have you ever built your life around an ‘existent’ that will one day cease to exist? How does the truth that only God is truly and eternally existent reshape where you place your hope?
?
The verse ‘Nor say of a thing, I will do that’ reminds you that every ‘thing’ you plan is subject to God’s will. When you make your next decision, will you attach it with ‘insha’Allah’ as a reality check, or will you let the words fall as if you are in control?

The Mu'tazili Definition of Non-Existent Possible Things as Entities
Al-Jurjani reports the Mu'tazili definition that possible non-existents are essences before existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili claim that possible non-existents have a fixed reality as essences before they come into existence.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila held that non-existents are fixed realities, not mere nothing.

The Mu'tazila are known for their distinctive view that non-existent possible things have a kind of reality. In this passage, al-Jurjani reports their definition, explaining that they consider these non-existents as 'essences, entities, and realities' that are fixed externally apart from existence. This lesson unpacks that claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own phrasing come through.
Notice that the passage begins by contextualising this as the 'second investigation'.
2
Identify the three terms
The Mu'tazila describe possible non-existents with three terms: ذوات, أعيان, وحقائق. Find them in the Arabic and note their English equivalents.
These three synonyms emphasise objective reality.
3
Understand the key clarification
The commentary explains that 'thing' here means fixed, established, and realized externally apart from the attribute of existence. Grasp the distinction between fixedness and existence.
This is the core of the Mu'tazili position.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث ( الثاني في تعريفات المعتزلة على القول بأن المعدوم شيء ) أي ثابت متقرر متحقق في الخارج عن صفة الوجود كما مر ( قالوا : المعدومات الممكنة قبل وجودها ذوات وأعيان وحقائق) وتأثير الفاعل في منفكاً
The second investigation: On the definitions of the Mu'tazila on the saying that the non-existent is a thing. That is, it is fixed, established, and realized externally apart from the attribute of existence, as has passed. They said: The possible non-existents before their existence are essences, entities, and realities. And the effect of the agent in [؟] separate.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a definition. It presents the Mu'tazili position that non-existent possible things are 'things' in the sense of having a fixed reality before they are brought into existence. The sharh clarifies that they are 'fixed, established, and realized externally apart from the attribute of existence'—meaning they have a kind of objective reality that is not identical with existence. The Mu'tazila call them 'essences, entities, and realities' (ذوات وأعيان وحقائق). The final phrase 'and the effect of the agent in separate' is incomplete but likely refers to the agent's effect being distinct from the essence of the possible thing. This position is foundational to the Mu'tazili theory of causal efficacy and their views on divine attributes and human acts.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: In reality, they assert a distinct mode of reality ('fixedness') that is not existence.

Checkpoint

B) Essences, entities, and realities.
C) Mere concepts in the mind.
D) Non-existent with no reality.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila affirm that possible non-existents have a fixed reality as essences before they are created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila say that possible things before they exist are already fixed essences in Allah's knowledge. Think of one thing you desperately hope for but haven't seen yet. Do you treat that thing as already real in Allah's decree, or as a mere wish? How would viewing it as a fixed essence change your trust in Allah today?
?
This lesson teaches that non-existents are not nothing—they have a reality apart from existence. Does your heart rest in the knowledge that Allah's knowledge of all things is complete, even before they happen? Or do you live as if some outcomes are uncertain? What step will you take this week to strengthen your certainty in Allah's perfect knowledge?

Refuting al-Jahiz and al-Nashi' on 'Thing'
Author refutes al-Jahiz and al-Nashi' with two Qur'anic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses Qur'anic verses to refute the claims that 'thing' applies only to the existent or only to the eternal.

Core Idea
A thing can be non-existent and originated.

This lesson begins the author's defense of the view that 'shay'' (thing) in its real sense includes the non-existent possible. He first addresses two restrictive positions: that 'thing' means only the existent (al-Jahiz) or only the eternal (al-Nashi'). Using clear Qur'anic evidence, he demonstrates that these restrictions are unfounded.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the structure: each refutation follows a pattern—(1) verse, (2) explanation, (3) opponent's claim nullified.
The passage uses 'فيبطل به قول فلان' to indicate the refutation.
2
Identify the two opponents
Spot al-Jahiz and Abu al-Ayyash al-Nashi' and their respective restrictions. Write down their claims.
3
Analyze the first refutation
Look at the verse 'I created you before when you were nothing'. How does it show that 'thing' includes the non-existent? Think about what 'nothing' refers to.
The non-existent thing was known to Allah.
4
Analyze the second refutation
Now look at the verse 'Allah is over all things competent'. How does it refute the claim that 'thing' is only for the eternal? Consider the scope of 'all things'.
Includes originated, so not exclusive to eternal.
5
Reflect on the principle
The author states 'the principle in unrestricted usage is reality'. What does this mean? How does it support the refutation?
Unrestricted language defaults to real meaning until context restricts.

The First Observatory - Sixth Section: Is the Non-Existent a Thing or Not? It is correct to deny it, the existent whether eternal or originated, substance or accident (and similar to: 'I created you before when you were nothing' [Maryam: 9] negates its application) in the way of reality (to the non-existent) because the reality does not invalidate the saying of al-Jahiz (and) His saying: 'And Allah is over all things competent' [al-Mumtahina: 7] negates its exclusivity to the eternal because ability is only connected to the originated, not the eternal, and the principle in unrestricted usage is reality, so it invalidates the saying of Abu al-Ayyash al-Nashi' (and) His saying: 'And do not say of anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow"' negates its exclusivity to the body, so it invalidates the saying of Hisham (and) the saying of Labid: 'Verily, everything besides Allah is false' negates (its exclusivity to the originated) because the principle in exception is that it is connected, so it invalidates the saying of the Jahmiyya. His saying: (and similar to 'I created you' etc.) is a refutation of the opponent's claims after establishing his claim. His saying: (because the reality etc.) i.e., the word according to its real meaning cannot be denied of what that meaning applies to. His saying: (only connects to the originated etc.) So the meaning of the verse does not hold otherwise, if it meant the existent in meaning, and the verse would be of the type of general specified; and as for that it is not derived from the verse, for then his power over possible non-existents becomes valid and does not harm us. His saying: (negates its exclusivity to the body) since the act of the servant is an accident, and some say: it negates its exclusivity to the existent as well because what will be done is non-existent; this is refuted because it is non-existent at the time of the statement, not absolutely. So the meaning is: do not say of the existent by His will in its appointed time: 'I will do that tomorrow' unless you say 'if Allah wills'. His saying: (negates its exclusivity to the eternal) If you say: the noble verse indicates the negation of exclusivity of the thing to the existent as well, because Allah is able over possible non-existents as well; and similarly it indicates the negation of its application to the eternal, not just the negation of its exclusivity to it; and each of these contradicts the original claim. I say: the two indications are weak; as for the first, the utmost that follows is that ability over the non-existent is not derived from this verse; as for the second, its utmost is that the word 'thing' is general and some of it is specified, and that is permissible. Yes, if it is specified as not having a meaning for the eternal? Mutamanna'. His saying: (negates its exclusivity to the body) It contains that the apparent meaning of the verse negates exclusivity to the existent as well, for the complete verse is 'And do not say of anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow" except if Allah wills' [al-Kahf: 23-24]; and that which will be done is non-existent now, and carrying it metaphorically invalidates the inference about non-exclusivity to the body; and it can be said: it does not follow from the verse that the term 'thing' is applied to what will be done before it is done, so contemplate.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a proof by linguistic preponderance. The author argues that the term 'shay'' (thing) in Arabic, when used unrestrictedly, applies to both existent and non-existent, as shown by Qur'anic verses. He refutes four restrictive positions: (1) that 'shay'' only applies to the existent (al-Jahiz) is refuted by the verse 'I created you when you were nothing' where 'nothing' indicates non-existent thing; (2) that it applies only to the eternal (Abu al-Ayyash) is refuted by 'Allah is over all things competent' which includes originated things; (3) that it applies only to bodies (Hisham) is refuted by 'Do not say of anything, "I will do it"' which includes actions (accidents); (4) that it applies only to originated things (Jahmiyya) is refuted by Labid's line 'Everything besides Allah is false' which includes the eternal except Allah. The commentary then addresses objections to each refutation, explaining why the verses are valid proofs and defending the interpretation against counterarguments. The overall conclusion is that 'shay'' in its real sense includes the non-existent possible.

Correction: The author shows through Qur'anic usage that 'thing' can also refer to non-existent possibles, as seen in 'when you were nothing'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting Hisham and the Jahmiyya on 'Thing'
Author refutes Hisham and Jahmiyya with a Qur'anic verse and a poetic line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a Qur'anic verse and a line of poetry to refute the claims that 'thing' applies only to bodies or only to originated things.

Core Idea
A thing can be an accident and eternal.

This lesson continues the refutation of restrictive views on 'shay'' (thing). The author now addresses Hisham's claim that 'thing' applies only to bodies, and the Jahmiyya's claim that it applies only to originated things. He uses a verse about future actions and Labid's famous line to show that 'thing' includes accidents and the eternal (except Allah).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Note how the author uses a different type of evidence for each refutation.
The structure is similar: verse/poem, explanation, invalidation.
2
Identify the two opponents
Spot Hisham and the Jahmiyya and their respective restrictions. Write down their claims.
Hisham restricts to bodies; Jahmiyya restricts to originated.
3
Analyze the third refutation
Look at the verse 'Do not say of anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow"' [al-Kahf: 23]. How does it refute Hisham? Consider what 'thing' refers to in this context.
The action to be done is an accident, not a body.
4
Analyze the fourth refutation
Now look at Labid's line 'Verily, everything besides Allah is false'. How does it refute the Jahmiyya? Think about the exception.
The exception 'besides Allah' shows that 'everything' includes Allah, who is eternal, so 'thing' is not limited to originated.
5
Consider the objections
The commentary discusses objections to these refutations. Read those carefully. Do you find them convincing?
The author defends his interpretation against counterarguments.

الموجود قديماً أو حادثاً جسماً أو عرضاً ( ونحو : خلقتك من قبل ولم تك شيئاً مریم : ٩ ] ينفي إطلاقه ) بطريق الحقيقة ) على المعدوم ) لأن الحقيقة لا فیبطل به قول الجاحظ (و) قوله : والله على كل شيء قدير ﴾ [الممتحنة: ٧] ينفي اختصاصه بالقديم لأن القدرة إنما تتعلق بالحادث دون القديم والأصل في الإطلاق الحقيقة، فيبطل به قول أبي العياش الناشي (و) قوله : ( ولا تقولن لشيء إني فاعل ذلك ) ينفي اختصاصه بالجسم فيبطل به قول هشام ( و) قول لبيد ( ألا كل شيء ما خلا الله باطل ) ينفي (اختصاصه بالحادث لأن الأصل في الاستثناء أن يكون متصلاً، فيبطل به قول الجهمية

قوله : ( ينفي اختصاصه بالجسم إذ فعل العبد ،عرض وما قبل : إنه ينفي اختصاصه بالموجود أيضاً لأن الذي سيفعل معدوم فمدفوع، لأنه معدوم حال القول لا مطلقاً، فالمعنى لا تقولن الموجود بإرادته تعالى في وقته المقدر له : إني أفعل ذلك غداً إلا أن تقول إن شاء الله

قوله : ( ينفي اختصاصه بالجسم فيه أن ظاهر الآية ينفي الاختصاص بالموجود أيضاً، إذ تمام الآية ولا تقولن لشيء إني فاعل ذلك غداً إلا أن يشاء الله ﴾ [الكهف: ٢٢و٢٣]، والذي الآن، والحمل على المجاز يبطل الاستدلال على عدم اختصاصه بالجسم، ويمكن

The First Observatory - Sixth Section: Is the Non-Existent a Thing or Not? It is correct to deny it, the existent whether eternal or originated, substance or accident (and similar to: 'I created you before when you were nothing' [Maryam: 9] negates its application) in the way of reality (to the non-existent) because the reality does not invalidate the saying of al-Jahiz (and) His saying: 'And Allah is over all things competent' [al-Mumtahina: 7] negates its exclusivity to the eternal because ability is only connected to the originated, not the eternal, and the principle in unrestricted usage is reality, so it invalidates the saying of Abu al-Ayyash al-Nashi' (and) His saying: 'And do not say of anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow"' negates its exclusivity to the body, so it invalidates the saying of Hisham (and) the saying of Labid: 'Verily, everything besides Allah is false' negates (its exclusivity to the originated) because the principle in exception is that it is connected, so it invalidates the saying of the Jahmiyya. His saying: (and similar to 'I created you' etc.) is a refutation of the opponent's claims after establishing his claim. His saying: (because the reality etc.) i.e., the word according to its real meaning cannot be denied of what that meaning applies to. His saying: (only connects to the originated etc.) So the meaning of the verse does not hold otherwise, if it meant the existent in meaning, and the verse would be of the type of general specified; and as for that it is not derived from the verse, for then his power over possible non-existents becomes valid and does not harm us. His saying: (negates its exclusivity to the body) since the act of the servant is an accident, and some say: it negates its exclusivity to the existent as well because what will be done is non-existent; this is refuted because it is non-existent at the time of the statement, not absolutely. So the meaning is: do not say of the existent by His will in its appointed time: 'I will do that tomorrow' unless you say 'if Allah wills'. His saying: (negates its exclusivity to the eternal) If you say: the noble verse indicates the negation of exclusivity of the thing to the existent as well, because Allah is able over possible non-existents as well; and similarly it indicates the negation of its application to the eternal, not just the negation of its exclusivity to it; and each of these contradicts the original claim. I say: the two indications are weak; as for the first, the utmost that follows is that ability over the non-existent is not derived from this verse; as for the second, its utmost is that the word 'thing' is general and some of it is specified, and that is permissible. Yes, if it is specified as not having a meaning for the eternal? Mutamanna'. His saying: (negates its exclusivity to the body) It contains that the apparent meaning of the verse negates exclusivity to the existent as well, for the complete verse is 'And do not say of anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow" except if Allah wills' [al-Kahf: 23-24]; and that which will be done is non-existent now, and carrying it metaphorically invalidates the inference about non-exclusivity to the body; and it can be said: it does not follow from the verse that the term 'thing' is applied to what will be done before it is done, so contemplate.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a proof by linguistic preponderance. The author argues that the term 'shay'' (thing) in Arabic, when used unrestrictedly, applies to both existent and non-existent, as shown by Qur'anic verses. He refutes four restrictive positions: (1) that 'shay'' only applies to the existent (al-Jahiz) is refuted by the verse 'I created you when you were nothing' where 'nothing' indicates non-existent thing; (2) that it applies only to the eternal (Abu al-Ayyash) is refuted by 'Allah is over all things competent' which includes originated things; (3) that it applies only to bodies (Hisham) is refuted by 'Do not say of anything, "I will do it"' which includes actions (accidents); (4) that it applies only to originated things (Jahmiyya) is refuted by Labid's line 'Everything besides Allah is false' which includes the eternal except Allah. The commentary then addresses objections to each refutation, explaining why the verses are valid proofs and defending the interpretation against counterarguments. The overall conclusion is that 'shay'' in its real sense includes the non-existent possible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'thing' in Arabic only refers to physical bodies.
Correction: The author shows through the verse about future actions that 'thing' includes accidents (non-physical actions).

Checkpoint
Which verse is used to refute Hisham's claim that 'thing' applies only to bodies?
A) 'I created you before when you were nothing' [Maryam: 9]
B) 'Allah is over all things competent' [al-Mumtahina: 7]
C) 'Do not say of anything, "Indeed I will do that tomorrow"' [al-Kahf: 23]
D) Labid's line

Key Takeaway
The term 'thing' includes accidents and the eternal, as shown by Qur'anic and poetic evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, do you truly acknowledge that every action you intend is a 'thing' under Allah's control? How would your reliance on Him change if you repeated 'Except if Allah wills' before every plan?
?
This lesson proves that 'thing' includes the eternal and the non-existent. Does your own concept of Allah's power include authority over things that do not yet exist? If not, what fears or limitations are you placing on His sovereignty?
?
Labid's line declares everything besides Allah is false. What 'thing' in your life have you been treating as if it were true and lasting besides Allah? Are you ready to let that falsehood go today?

Two Views on Attributes of Non-Existent Substances
Sanusi states the two positions on attributes of non-existent substances and defines generic attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main positions in the kalām dispute over whether non-existent substances possess any attributes.

Core Idea
Non-existent substances: bare essences or bearers of generic attributes?

This lesson introduces the central disagreement between Abu Ishaq ibn Ayyash and the majority of Ashʿarī theologians on the status of non-existent essences. Ibn Ayyash held that pre-creation essences are completely devoid of attributes, while others argued they possess generic essential attributes like substance-hood. The passage sets the stage for deeper arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the two views
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the two positions: one from Ibn Ayyash and the other from 'others'.
Notice that the phrase 'gayr Ibn Ayyash' indicates the majority view.
2
Understand the definition of generic attributes
The commentary defines 'generic attributes' as attributes not due to an added meaning (al-sifat al-nafsiyya). Reflect on what this means for the nature of substance.
These are attributes like 'substance-hood' that are essential to the quiddity.
3
Reflect on why this matters for divine attributes
Consider how this dispute connects to the broader theology of divine attributes. If non-existent substances have no attributes, how can divine attributes be eternal?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جعلها موجودة لا في كونها ذوات ثم اختلفوا فقال أبو إسحاق بن عياش الذوات في العدم معراة عن جميع الصفات ولا تحصل لها الصفات إلا حال الوجود وقال غير ابن عياش إنها في حال العدم متصفة بصفات الأجناس. قوله : (لا) في كونها ذوات أشار بذلك إلى أن اختصاص التأثير في كونها موجودة إضافي، فلا ينافي تحقق التأثير باعتبار التركيب والاتصاف بالأعراض. قوله : ( فقال أبو إسحاق إلخ ) تحرزاً عن لزوم السفسطة . قوله : ( متصفة بصفات الأجناس أي الصفات النفسية هي ما لا تكون حاصلة لأجل معنى زائد على الذات. قوله : ( متصفة بصفات الأجناس قالوا لأنها متساوية في الذاتية فلو لم تتخالف بالصفات لكانت واحدة والجواب أنها متخالفة بالماهيات، وإن كانت متساوية في مفهوم الذات. ثم قالوا : لأنها متساوية في الذاتية فلو لم نتخالف بالصفات لكانت واحدة ولأنها متخالفة، إذ لو تماثلت في العدم لتماثلت في الوجود لأن ما بالذات لا يزول، والتخالف إنما هو بالصفات ضرورة اشتراكها في الذاتية والجواب أن مفهوم الذات عارض للحقائق لإتمام حقيقتها كما توهموه والتساوي في العارض لا يمنع الاختلاف بالحقيقة كالحقائق المتشاركة في الوجود، وحينئذ لا يرد شيء مما ذكر وبهذا يبطل أيضاً تمسك ابن عیاش على التعري بأنها لما كانت متساوية في الذاتية، فاختصاص بعضها بصفة معينة لا يكون لذاتها وهو ظاهر ولا لصفة أخرى وإلا لتسلسل ولا لفاعل موجب لتساوي نسبته إلى الكل بل لفاعل مختار وفعله حادث فيلزم كون المعدوم مورداً لمتزايلات وهو باطل بالاتفاق، فتعين أن يكون ذلك حالة الوجود ووجه البطلان جواز أن يكون لذاته المخصوصة.
He made them existing not in their being essences. Then they disagreed and said: Abu Ishaq ibn Ayyash: The essences in non-existence are stripped of all attributes and do not acquire attributes except in the state of existence. And other than Ibn Ayyash said: They are in the state of non-existence qualified by generic attributes. His statement: (not) in their being essences: he indicated by that that the specification of effect in their being existing is relative, so it does not negate the realization of effect by consideration of composition and being qualified by accidents. His statement: (So Abu Ishaq etc.) as a precaution against falling into sophistry. His statement: (Qualified by generic attributes) meaning the essential attributes are those that are not acquired by a meaning additional to the essence. His statement: (Qualified by generic attributes) they said because they are equal in essentiality, so if they were not differing by attributes, they would be one, and the answer is that they differ by quiddities, even if they are equal in the concept of essence. Then they said: because they are equal in essentiality, so if they were not differing by attributes, they would be one, and because they are different, for if they were similar in non-existence, they would be similar in existence, because what is essential does not vanish, and difference is only by attributes due to their commonality in essentiality, and the answer is that the concept of essence is accidental to realities to complete their reality as they supposed, and equality in the accidental does not prevent difference in reality like realities that share in existence, and thus none of what was mentioned follows, and by this also the argument of Ibn Ayyash is invalidated in the exposure, because they are equal in essentiality, so the specification of some of them with a particular attribute is not due to their essence, which is apparent, nor due to another attribute, otherwise it would lead to infinite regress, nor due to an agent that necessitates equal proportion to all, but rather a free agent, and His act is temporal, so it follows that the non-existent is a locus of [similar?] which is invalid by consensus, so it is determined that that is in the state of existence, and the invalid nature is the possibility that it is due to its specific essence.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the central dispute about whether non-existent substances have any attributes. First, two positions are stated: Abu Ishaq ibn Ayyash holds that non-existent substances are completely bare of attributes until they exist; others hold that they possess generic attributes (like substance-hood) even in non-existence. The commentary then explains the matn. The phrase 'لا في كونها ذوات' indicates that the specification of effect in making them existent is relative, so it does not contradict the reality of composition and accidents. Ibn Ayyash's position is justified as a precaution against sophistry. The generic attributes (al-sifat al-nafsiyya) are defined as attributes that are not due to an additional meaning. The main argument proceeds: if non-existent substances were equal in essentiality (al-dhatiyya), they would be identical unless differentiated by attributes; but they must be different because they have different quiddities (al-mahiyyat). The opponent might argue that if they were similar in non-existence, they would be similar in existence, since what is essential does not change; difference must be due to attributes. The response is that the concept of 'essence' (al-dhat) is accidental to realities, so equality in that accidental concept does not prevent real difference, just as existents differ despite sharing existence. Then the specific argument of Ibn Ayyash is refuted: if all non-existent substances are equal in essentiality, then a particular attribute cannot be due to the essence itself (obviously), nor due to another attribute (infinite regress), nor due to a necessitating agent (since his relation to all is equal), so it must come from a free agent acting in time, making the non-existent a locus of [similar things], which is invalid by consensus. Thus the specification must be in the state of existence. The invalidity lies in the possibility that it is due to the specific essence itself.

المعدوم
الصفات النفسية
صفات الأجناس
الذاتية
أبو إسحاق بن عياش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Ashʿarīs agree that non-existent substances have no attributes.
Correction: Ibn Ayyash held the minority view; most Ashʿarīs affirm generic attributes for non-existent substances.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Ishaq ibn Ayyash, when do essences acquire attributes?
A) In non-existence, they already have generic attributes.
B) They acquire attributes only in the state of existence.
C) They never acquire attributes.
D) They acquire attributes before non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The dispute centers on whether non-existent essences have any attributes; Ibn Ayyash denies all, while others affirm generic essential attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own nature before you were born, do you think of it as a blank slate without any attributes, or as already bearing certain qualities? How does the answer you lean toward affect the way you view human potential and moral responsibility?
?
The passage warns that denying all attributes to non-existent essences can lead to sophistry (safsata). In your own life, are there areas where you have been too quick to strip away attributes from people or things, leading to a distorted view? What would it mean to recognize their essential, intrinsic qualities instead?
?
If God knows non-existent essences as having generic attributes, what does that imply about the depth of His knowledge? Based on this lesson, how does this understanding challenge you to trust that God sees the full reality of your being, even the parts not yet manifested?

Arguments and Refutation on Non-Existent Attributes
Sanusi presents the argument for generic attributes and refutes Ibn Ayyash's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main arguments for attributing generic attributes to non-existent substances and how the response refutes Ibn Ayyash's position.

Core Idea
Equality in essentiality does not prove identity; realities differ by quiddities.

This lesson delves into the detailed argumentation: proponents of generic attributes argue that without attributes all non-existent substances would be identical, and the opponents' appeal to essentiality is refuted. The passage also invalidates Ibn Ayyash's specific argument based on the impossibility of a necessary agent acting without differentiation.

Guided Path
1
Follow the argument for generic attributes
Read the passage from 'قوله : ( متصفة بصفات الأجناس قالوا...' and note how proponents reason that without attributes, all non-existent substances would be identical.
They argue from equality in essentiality (dhatiyya) to the necessity of differentiation by attributes.
2
Understand the response about quiddities
The answer (jawāb) distinguishes between the concept of essence (mafhūm al-dhāt) and the actual quiddities (al-mahiyyāt). Equality in the accidental concept does not prevent real difference.
This is a key point: sharing the concept 'substance' does not make all substances identical.
3
See the refutation of Ibn Ayyash's own argument
The passage then shows that Ibn Ayyash's reasoning leads to an infinite regress or to the conclusion that non-existent essences are loci of temporal acts, which is absurd. Instead, the specification can be due to the specific essence itself.
This refutation turns his own logic against him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Ayyash's argument proves that attributes cannot be from a free agent.
Correction: The response shows his argument leads to a contradiction; instead, attributes can be due to the specific essence itself, avoiding the free-agent problem.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is the reason non-existent substances can differ without attributes?
A) They differ by their quiddities (al-mahiyyat).
B) They have different temporal origins.
C) They are assigned attributes by a free agent.
D) They are identical in non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Non-existent substances can differ by their quiddities even if they share the concept of essence, and Ibn Ayyash's reasoning collapses because it fails to account for the specific essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence before you were created, do you believe you had any identity or quiddity? How does Sanusi's assertion that quiddities are distinct even in non-existence challenge your assumptions about your own origins?
?
Have you ever assumed that sharing a label (like 'human' or 'believer') makes you essentially the same as others? This lesson teaches that equality in an accidental concept does not mean identity in reality. How can you apply this to avoid judging others superficially?
?
The refutation here defends that specific essences themselves can be the source of difference without needing external attributes. In your spiritual life, what is one quality you have wrongly attributed to external circumstances rather than to your own created essence? How will you reclaim it as your own?

Dividing Attributes: The Whole and the Detail
Sanusi divides attributes into two kinds and explains the first kind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi divides attributes into those of the whole and those of detail, and why attributes like life require a bodily structure, making them specific to substances.

Core Idea
Attributes of the whole require a structure; attributes of detail do not.

Imam al-Sanusi begins his classification of attributes by drawing a fundamental distinction: some attributes belong to the whole composite structure, while others belong to each individual part. The first kind—life, power, knowledge—cannot exist without a bodily frame. This passage lays out that distinction and explains why these attributes are tied to substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two categories
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the two divisions: attributes of the whole and attributes of detail. The passage also includes commentary clarifying the scope.
2
Understand why life and its followers need a structure
The text states that life is conditioned by the structure (al-binya) as a balance of temperament. Consider why this makes these attributes specific to substances.
3
Note the exception: the structure itself is not an attribute
The commentary points out that the composition (tarkīb) is relational (iʿtibārī) and not counted among attributes of the whole, though there is some reflection.
This is a subtle point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي أي الصفات على الإطلاق إما عائدة إلى الجملة أي البنية المركبة من أمور عدة أو إلى التفصيل أي إلى كل واحد من متعدد بلا اعتبار تركيب بينها و القسم الأول العائد إلى الجملة هو الحياة وما يتبعها من القدرة والعلم والإرادة والكراهية وغيرها فإنها محتاجة إلى بنية. قوله: (أي الصفات على الإطلاق) أي سواء كانت صفات الأجناس أو لا، وسواء كانت قائمة بالموصوف حال الوجود أو حال العدم، فإن الوجود مثلاً لا يقوم بالمعدوم حال العدم وكذا المشروطية. قوله: (هو الحياة وما يتبعها) المراد من الصفات المقسومة إلى الأقسام ما هي من مقولة مخصوصة مركبة من جواهر فردة فهذا القسم مختص بالجواهر إذ لا يتصور حلول الحياة في الأعراض المركبة. قوله: (فإنها محتاجة إلخ) لأن الحياة مشروطة بالبنية لكونها اعتدال المزاج أو تابعة له والباقي مشروطة بها. قوله: ( وإنما لم يعد نفس التركيب من صفات الجملة لأنه اعتباري وفيه تأمل .
And they, i.e., attributes in absolute terms, are either referring to the whole, i.e., the structure composed of several things, or to the detail, i.e., to each one of multiple without consideration of composition between them. And the first section, referring to the whole, is life and what follows it of power, knowledge, will, hate, and others, for they need a structure. His statement: (attributes in absolute terms) meaning whether they are generic attributes or not, and whether they subsist in the described in the state of existence or in the state of non-existence, for existence does not subsist in the non-existent in the state of non-existence, and similarly conditionality. His statement: (is life and what follows it) the intended meaning of the attributes divided into sections is that they are from a specific category composed of individual substances; this section is specific to substances, for it is inconceivable for life to inhere in composite accidents. His statement: (for they need etc.) because life is conditioned by the structure, being the balance of temperament or following it, and the rest are conditioned by it. His statement: (And the composition itself was not counted among the attributes of the whole because it is relational, and in it there is reflection.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage defines the division of attributes into two kinds: attributes of the whole (al-jumla) and attributes of the detail (al-tafsīl). Attributes of the whole are those that require a composite structure: life, power, knowledge, will, hate, etc. These attributes need a bodily structure (al-binya) because life is conditioned by the balance of temperament or follows it. The commentary explains that 'attributes in absolute terms' includes generic attributes and non-generic ones, and includes attributes that subsist in the described during existence or non-existence (though existence itself does not subsist in the non-existent). The intended meaning of these attributes is that they are from a specific category composed of individual substances, so this section is specific to substances (al-jawāhir) because life cannot inhere in composite accidents. The structure (al-tarkīb) itself is not counted among the attributes of the whole because it is relational (i‘tibārī), although there is some reflection on this point.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division of attributes into whole and detail applies to God's attributes.
Correction: This passage discusses attributes of created substances (jawāhir). God's life, for example, is not conditioned by a bodily structure.

Checkpoint
Why are attributes of the whole (like life) specific to substances rather than accidents?
A) Because life is a simple accident.
B) Because they require a bodily structure (بنية).
C) Because they are eternal.
D) Because they are generic attributes.

Key Takeaway
Attributes of the whole require a bodily structure, making them specific to substances, while attributes of detail do not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life depends on a fragile physical structure that can be disrupted by a single imbalance. How does this truth confront your sense of self-sufficiency? What would it mean to live today with the awareness that your very existence is sustained moment by moment by the One who created that balance?
?
The lesson teaches that your power, knowledge, and will are all contingent upon life. In what ways do you act as though your abilities are permanent possessions rather than borrowed gifts? Commit to one action today that acknowledges your total dependence on Allah for every capacity you have.
?
If the structure of your body is what makes life possible, then death is simply the dissolution of that structure. Are you living as if this reality is distant, or are you preparing for the meeting with your Lord? What is one change you will make today to prioritize your akhirah over your temporary physical existence?

Four Types of Attributes for Substances
Sanusi enumerates four types of attributes for substances, with a clear explanation of three and a fragmented fourth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three specified attributes of substances and the debate over the nature of spatial occupation (kawn), as well as the unresolved fourth attribute.

Core Idea
Substances have four attributes, but only three are fully explained in this passage.

Sanusi begins enumerating the attributes that substances possess by virtue of their nature. He identifies three clearly: substance-hood, existence, and spatial occupation. The fourth is introduced but not completed in this excerpt. This lesson explores the definitions and the theological debate surrounding spatial occupation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the four attributes
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author lists four types, but the fourth is cut off. Try to identify the first three and the beginning of the fourth.
The passage comes from a commentary on the matn, so the fourth attribute is likely explained in the commentary's continuation.
2
Understand substance-hood and existence
The first attribute is substance-hood (al-jawhariyya), which is present even in non-existence. The second is existence (al-wujud), which is bestowed by the agent. The agent does not create the essence or quiddity, only the attribute of existence.
This reflects the Ash'ari position that quiddities are not created.
3
Analyze the debate on spatial occupation (kawn)
The third attribute is spatial occupation (al-tahayyuz), also called al-kawn. There is a disagreement: some say kawn is distinct from motion, rest, conjunction, and separation (the majority view), while others say it is one of these four.
The argument using a single substance shows that kawn can exist without any of the four, supporting the majority view.
4
Reflect on the incomplete fourth attribute
The text begins the fourth attribute with 'الرابع أن قوله : ...' but does not complete it. The commentary that follows does not identify it explicitly, leaving it ambiguous. Consider why this lacuna might exist and how it affects the enumeration.
This is a known issue in the manuscript; the fourth attribute may be clarified in subsequent pages or other sources.
5
Check your understanding with the quiz
Answer the multiple-choice question about the majority view on kawn to test your comprehension of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و القسم الثاني العائد إلى التفصيل إما للجواهر وإما للأعراض فللجواهر أنواع أربعة من الصفات الأول الصفة الحاصلة للجوهر حالتي الوجود والعدم وهي الجوهرية التي هي من صفات الأجناس الثاني الصفة الحاصلة من الفاعل وهو الوجود فإن الفاعل لا تأثير له في الذوات لأنها ثابتة أزلاً، وإثبات الثابت محال ولا في كون الجوهر جوهراً، لأن الماهيات غير مجعولة عندهم، بل في جعل الجوهر موجوداً، أي متصفاً بصفة الوجود الثالث ما يتبع الوجود أي وجود الجوهر وهو التحيز قالوا : إنه صفة صادرة عن صفة الجوهرية بشرط الوجود، ويسمونه بالكون فمنهم من قال : الكون غير الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق، فإنه إذا فرض أنه تعالى خلق جوهراً واحداً فقط كان له في أول حدوثه كون بدون شيء من هذه الأربعة ومنهم من قال إنه أحد الأربعة الرابع أن قوله : ... قوله: (لأن الماهيات غير مجعولة عندهم أي في كونها ماهيات، وإنما قال عندهم مع عدم الجعل بهذا المعنى متفق عليه، إذ لا يتصور توسط الجعل بين الشيء ونفسه، لأن الكلام في بيان مذهبهم. قوله: ( وهو التحيز) أي الحصول في حيز ما . قوله: ( قالوا : ( أي الجمهور خلافاً للشحام والبصرى كما سيأتي . قوله: ( غير الحركة إلخ) أي لا ينحصر في الأربعة كما يدل عليه الدليل لا أنه ليس شيئاً منها . قوله: ( كان له إلخ) أما الاجتماع والافتراق فلفرض كونه موجوداً واحداً فقط، وأما الحركة والسكون فلكون كل منهما كوناً ثانياً . قوله: ( إنه أحد الأربعة لعدم اعتباره اللبث في السكون . قوله: (بشرط الوجود تصريح لما علم ضمناً إذ التحيز كما عرفت مشروط بالوجود. قوله: (عن صفة الجوهرية بشرط الوجود هذا مذهب الجمهور خلافاً للشحام والبصري كما سيأتي . قوله: ( الكون غير الحركة إلخ لا ينحصر في هذه الأربعة لا أن الأربعة ليست من الكون . قوله: ( إنه أحد الأربعة سيجيء في بحث الأكوان أن أبا هاشم قال : إنه سكون ولم يعتبر الليث والمسبوقية فيه.
And the second section, referring to the detail, is either for substances or for accidents. For substances there are four types of attributes: first, the attribute obtained by the substance in both states of existence and non-existence, which is substance-hood, which is among the generic attributes. Second, the attribute obtained from the agent, which is existence, and the agent has no effect on the essences because they are fixed eternally, and affirming the already existing is impossible, nor in making the substance a substance, because quiddities are not made according to them, but rather in making the substance existing, i.e., qualified by the attribute of existence. Third, what follows existence, i.e., the existence of the substance, which is spatial occupation. They said: it is an attribute emanating from the attribute of substance-hood on condition of existence, and they call it al-kawn. Some of them said: al-kawn is other than motion, rest, conjunction, and separation; for if it is assumed that He, exalted, created only one substance, it would have at its first occurrence a kawn without any of these four. And some of them said it is one of the four. Fourth, that his statement: ... [unclear] His statement: (Because quiddities are not made according to them) i.e., in their being quiddities, and he only said 'according to them' despite the absence of making in this sense being agreed upon, because it is inconceivable for a making to intervene between a thing and itself, and the speech is about explaining their school. His statement: (And it is spatial occupation) i.e., obtaining in some space. His statement: (They said) i.e., the majority, contrary to al-Shahham and al-Basri as will come. His statement: (Other than motion etc.) i.e., it is not confined to the four, as the evidence indicates, not that it is not among them. His statement: (It would have for him etc.) As for conjunction and separation, it is due to assuming it is only one existent; as for motion and rest, because each of them is a secondary kawn. His statement: (It is one of the four) due to not considering dwelling in rest. His statement: (On condition of existence) is a declaration of what was known implicitly, since spatial occupation as you know is conditioned by existence. His statement: (From the attribute of substance-hood on condition of existence) this is the opinion of the majority, contrary to al-Shahham and al-Basri as will come. His statement: (Al-kawn is other than motion etc.) is not confined to these four, not that the four are not from kawn. His statement: (It is one of the four) will come in the discussion of akwan that Abu Hashim said: it is rest, and he did not consider dwelling and priority in it.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four types of attributes for substances as part of the second section (attributes of detail). The first type is substance-hood (al-jawhariyya), an essential generic attribute present in both existence and non-existence. The second is existence (al-wujud), an attribute from the agent; the agent does not create the essence (since it is eternally fixed) nor its quiddity (which is uncreated), but rather makes it existent. The third is spatial occupation (al-tahayyuz), also called al-kawn, which emanates from substance-hood on condition of existence. There is a debate: some say kawn is distinct from motion, rest, conjunction, and separation; if only one substance existed, it would have kawn without any of those four. Others say kawn is one of those four. The fourth attribute is not fully given in the text; the commentary does not clearly identify it, but the matn mentions 'الرابع أن قوله : ...' suggesting it might be related to a statement of the author. The commentary then explains various points: quiddities are not made in their quiddity; spatial occupation means obtaining in a space; the opinion that kawn is not confined to the four is the majority view (against al-Shahham and al-Basri); the argument about a single substance shows kawn can exist without the four, making them secondary; the condition of existence for spatial occupation is stated; and it is noted that Abu Hashim considered kawn as rest, not considering dwelling or priority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: al-kawn (spatial occupation) is identical to motion or one of the four (motion, rest, conjunction, separation).
Correction: The majority view holds that al-kawn is distinct and not confined to these four; it can exist without any of them, as in the case of a single substance.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view mentioned, what is the nature of al-kawn (spatial occupation)?
A) It is identical to motion.
B) It is confined to the four: motion, rest, conjunction, separation.
C) It is distinct from the four and not confined to them.
D) It is the same as substance-hood.

Key Takeaway
Substances have essential attributes that define them, and the nature of spatial occupation (kawn) is debated among theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is a gift from the Creator, not something you earned or deserved, how does that reshape your sense of gratitude and humility today?
?
The scholars disagreed on whether spatial occupation is a distinct attribute or one of four accidents. Are you willing to live with theological uncertainty on matters that may never be fully resolved in this life, or does that unsettle you?
?
Your essence (substance-hood) is fixed eternally, beyond your control. Does that comfort you or trouble you? How does it affect your efforts to change yourself?
